_439 messages · 28 participants · 2003-04 → 2003-05_

### Sets and subsets

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2003-04-20T01:30:13-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<b7tepn$9tb$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net>`

```
As a follow on to RW's incorrect view that an integer that *also* belongs to
the set of whole numbers (or integers) that represent dates in the form
YYYYMMDD "loses" its identity as an integer,  let's see if I have this
correct.

The set of all human beings includes both mammals who (when mature and
healthy) produce sperm or ova.

Robert Wagner belongs to the set of all human beings (I assume).

Robert Wagner belongs to the SUB-set of human beings that are male human
beings, i.e. the subset that (when mature and healthy) produce sperm

Therefore, as Robert Wagner cannot produce BOTH sperm and ova, he/it ceases
to be considered a human being.

   ***

Do I have that logic "correct" (i.e. following the same set of logic as RW
applies to "an integer" versus "the set of all integers")?
```

```
"William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com> wrote:

>As a follow on to RW's incorrect view that an integer that *also* belongs to
>the set of whole numbers (or integers) that represent dates in the form
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
>applies to "an integer" versus "the set of all integers")?

Suppose I brought you a horse and a cow. 

WMK: I see you brought me two animals. 
RW:   Yes, both are ungulates too. One is a horse and the other, a cow.
WMK: I prefer to call them animals; a matter of style.
--later--
WMK: Please milk the first animal.
RW:   Err, which one? The mare and cow both have teats.
DD:    RW has no common sense. It's obvious which one.
RP:    You just made that up. Can you _prove_ the mare has teats? 
          I thought not. This demonstrates that everything you say is wrong.
WMK: The Good Book says both are animals. [Recites entire passage]
-- end of parable --

We create taxonomies to improve communication. Those who refuse to use them
diminish it. 

This integer debate has become repetitious and turned into an ignoratio elenchi.
I'll not respond to any more posts about integers. 

Instead, I'll have a picnic lunch with my lady. On the menu: a berry/lemon cloud
galette and French picnic tart.
```

I'm not an animal rights advocate. I've been vegetarian 30 years for CULINARY
reasons. Meat is boring. People who eat meat every day have no imagination. If
you take away their meat, they'd make mac & cheese the first day, pizza the
second, stir-fry the third, and be out of ideas on the fourth day.

A typical restaurant menu looks more like a butcher shop list than an offering
of food dishes. 'Would you rather have the steak or the chicken?' What culinary
bankruptcy.

Having worked in the supermarket industry, I know the distribution of sales by
department. It is 50% grocery and frozen, 25% meat, 20% non-food and
bakery/deli, 5% produce. Think about that .. 5% real food and 95% mass produced.
The distribution in my cart is 75% produce and 25% other, mostly grains. It's
actually not a single cart; the produce comes from a high-quality produce
market. I make almost everything from scratch. 

When I cook for guests, they enter with a look of doom, depart asking for
recipes. My cuisine is eclectic, with a leaning toward Mediterranean,
Middle-Eastern and French.  I don't use tofu nor shop in health-food stores.

Does that answer your taunt? Now, go have another double-cheese Big Mac.

-- no smoking, no drinking, diet, exercise, etc. -- and the control group were
couch potatoes. Assessement of their health after a few years found it to be ...
exactly the same. You don't read about these studies in the popular press
because they are politically incorrect.

```
"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote:

>My younger brother has small cell lung cancer.  He has never smoked, but his
>first wife was a heavy smoker.   Hardly anybody gets this without being a
>smoker.  He does everything for his health including lots of running.

As former keeper of the FAQ and one of the principal writers in alt.smokers (ca.
1990-94), I've spent hundreds of hours studying the link between second hand
smoke and lung cancer. 

Bottom line: not one valid scientific study has found a causal relationship.
More than a thousand have tried. A few claimed 'success' (thanks to funding
bias), but were later found to be flawed or (gasp) deliberately manipulated. At
least three of those 'disproofs' were authored by me, through the use of better
statistical analysis. 

The most common flaw in these studies is failing to discount the effect of
socioeconomic status. Poor people get sick more often than rich people. If one
looked for a link between watching professional wrestling and getting lung
cancer, (s)he would find a corelation. Tellingly, many studies have found a
negative dose-response relationship -- spouses of one pack a day smokers get
lung cancer more often than spouses of three pack a day smokers. In fact, three
pack a day are back to the baseline of no exposure.  You won't read that in Time
magazine; you must go to original sources. The explanation is simple: poor
people cannot afford to smoke three packs a day. Designers of fraudulent studies
use this knowledge to introduce bias by manipulating demographics. It is
blatantly obvious in the Fontham study, for instance. 

There are 140,000 new cases of lung cancer in the US per year, broken down as:
20K never smokers, 120K ever smokers. Your brother is one of the 20K. 

Why do poor people get sick so much? It's not lack of medical care nor any other
single reason. If it were, epidemiologists would have found it decades ago. It's
a combination of environmental factors aka lifestyle choices aka bad habits. I
believe, with admittedly weak substantiation, the main culprit is diet. Poor
people eat junk food, fried food, hydrogenated fat (margarine), etc. etc. 

Robert
```

"Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
>news:3ea2c17e.150090828@news.optonline.net...
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>How could such a number NOT be an integer?

You arrived too late. The party's over. :)

>What I see in these threads is way short of effective interaction and
>exchange of particles...

Particle exchange belongs in the physics newsgroup, although it might be
appropriate here under the rubric 'communication'. How does an electron know the
nucleus is there to be circled around? The answer's not gravity. They're
constantly exchanging quarks so they know where the other is. You say 'that's
fascinating, I wonder what the communication protocol is.' Then jack-booted QM
guys squash the thread by telling us every revolution spawns a new universe. 

>> This integer debate has become repetitious and turned into an ignoratio
>elenchi.
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>leaves your dignity intact, though...

One would think Set Theory would be a natural for programmers. It looks like
logic because it uses intersections and unions. The evidence shows I was
mistaken. Programmers think in terms of bits and bytes and simplistic
definitions, not abstractions. 

I had a similar experience on the opposite side of the spectrum when I hired a
recent college grad with a degree in the mathematical specialty called Logic.
Naively, I thought this is a natural fit. Logic is the bread and butter of
programming, right? Wrong. He was extremely intelligent (PhD at 22) but had
difficulty grasping basic programming concepts. To gain a better insight into
his thinking, I read his thesis. It had a lot of set theory plus some
specialized algebra more advanced than sets. Its structure and flow wasn't very
'logical'. 

A year later I was about to fire him. He saved me the trouble by finding a
teaching position at a well-known university. 

The smartest programmer I ever knew was a shadowy figure who went by an obvious
pseudonym: Mason Dixon. He got a full scholarship at MIT's School of Education.
I didn't know MIT had one, neither did he. He hitchhiked from Fort Worth to
Cambridge. Three months later he was back, saying "I didn't know anything about
teaching. I thought they were supposed to be the experts. It turned out they
didn't know anything either." That guy was really bright and an outstanding
programmer. He quit the day after I did.

>> Instead, I'll have a picnic lunch with my lady. On the menu: a berry/lemon
>>cloud galette and French picnic tart.
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>achieved <G>.

Would posting the recipe help? I'm quite proud of it. I started with Mollie
Katzan's and improved it over half a dozen iterations. Kinda like posting source
code, eh? <G>

How about Best Practices Cooking offering modernization tips such as  'A dash of
bitters improves any pilaf or risotto, especially when made from barley.' and
'You MUST use a ceramic knife. High-carbon steel is Old School and obsolete. As
for stainless steel, don't get me started.' The latter should _really_ start a
flame war. <G>

No they're not. You are confusing quarks with the (yet to be confirmed)

> >While this is not the ideal mechanism for leaving an untenable position
when
> >under fire (The leopard walk or the monkey crawl are the favoured miltary
> >options <G>) it is certainly effective. Don't be under the illusion that
it
> >leaves your dignity intact, though...
>

> One would think Set Theory would be a natural for programmers. It looks
like
> logic because it uses intersections and unions. The evidence shows I was
> mistaken. Programmers think in terms of bits and bytes and simplistic
> definitions, not abstractions.

I'm a programmer. I understand set theory. And I have imagination. Also, I
can cope with abstractions... and, I know what an integer is <G>.

(I refute your allegations with the above. It's a technique I picked up from
Dr. Johnson... When it was suggested to him  that everything is an illusion,
he slammed his foot into a stone and said "I refute it, thus..." He couldn't
walk for a week but felt it was worth it to win the argument... My approach
is less painful (for me, at least <G>))


<snipped deflection germ>

Pete.

```
"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote

> > No one understand infinity, that is impossible.
> 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> How do you determine what is possible and what is impossible to understand?

Well, you said it in 'flavour'.  It is entirely possible to understand
an abstraction of infinity, or a subset or flavour of infinity.

But, just as you cannot know what happens in a black hole (because
there is no information flow), you cannot understand the whole of
infinity.  If you understand as much as the number of brain cells you
have will hold, there will always be one more factor left out.  That
is why it is called infinity.

(At least that is my understanding of how infinity is represented in
the abstraction that I do understand).
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Sets and subsets

_(reply depth: 15)_

- **From:** "Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2003-04-27T14:28:23+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3eab4057_2@127.0.0.1>`
- **References:** `<b7tepn$9tb$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <3ea88130.21326254@news.optonline.net> <b8ab71$1dv$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz> <3eaaa1e8.160795747@news.optonline.net> <b8eseu$2fv$1@panix1.panix.com> <joe_zitzelberger-B235AC.19492726042003@corp.supernews.com>`

```

"Joe Zitzelberger" <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com> wrote in message
news:joe_zitzelberger-B235AC.19492726042003@corp.supernews.com...
> I said I wasn't going to get involved in this, but I just have to.  The
> purpose of source code is to communicate with maintenance programmers
> and others that might have to support your object code.
>

Only for as long as you still develop computer systems that NEED to have
source code maintained. The modern trend is away from maintaining source
code towards the use of re-usable components, procedures, and packages that
manipulate objects. Rather than change source code you simply invoke a
method of the object. This is a very hard concept for most COBOL people to
get their heads round, especially as there is strong resistance to OOP in
COBOL shops. I believe this is the major reason why COBOL is being sidelined
and the major factor contributing towards COBOL disuse. (I agree it is
arguable...<G>)

> If all you were concerned with was communicating with the machine you
> could toss the source out once you had object.
>

That is exactly what we do when we buy third party components. We never HAVE
access to the source code. Instead we are focussed on the Business
functionality and how well a given object supports it. If it meets the
business requirement we "plug it in". The concept of modifying it is not up
for discussion. The best analogy I can think of is if you were building an
engine for your car. You would buy the parts, crankshaft, bearings, etc. and
then assemble them. You would NOT decide to re-mill the crankshaft or plane
the head yourself. If you wanted these performance enhancing features you
would buy a "sport pack" which already incorporated them and had been found
to work in thousands of engines just like yours. This is one way to minimise
the risk of an engine rebuild.... Modern software engineering is very close
to this.

> I suggest that the purpose of programming is to communicate with the
> machine and other humans equally.

I disagree. Programming is to communicate with the machine. Written and
spoken language is to communicate with other Human Beings.

Pete.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Sets and subsets

_(reply depth: 16)_

- **From:** Joe Zitzelberger <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com>
- **Date:** 2003-04-28T09:05:51-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<joe_zitzelberger-D1AADE.09055128042003@corp.supernews.com>`
- **References:** `<b7tepn$9tb$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <3eaaa1e8.160795747@news.optonline.net> <b8eseu$2fv$1@panix1.panix.com> <joe_zitzelberger-B235AC.19492726042003@corp.supernews.com> <b8fps7$doe$1@panix1.panix.com>`

```

> Joe Zitzelberger  <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com> wrote:
> >
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> >machine and other humans equally.

In article <b8fps7$doe$1@panix1.panix.com>, docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
>
> That conclusion seems to require that source code be the equivalent of 
> programming... those who would write in machine language or hard-wire the 
> patch-boards might disagree with that.

My primary language is OS/390 assembler, and I have occasionally coded 
in pure machine language style (e.g. sans assembler), and I said that.  
Source code is not programming, but it is the language that both  
programmers and machines must both speak if communication is to occur.

It would be completely valid Cobol from me to allocate some storage, 
fill it with some opcodes, create a procedure pointer to it and start 
executing.  The machine would not give it an electrons thought -- any 
programmer, including myself a few days later, might wonder what the 
hell was going on.

Clairity of source is important first for the originating programmer as 
they are writting; second for the emergency middle-of-the-night fix that 
"will never be necessary"; and thirdly if the program ever needs to be 
enhanced.

If machine communication is the ONLY concern -- then why bother with ANY 
high level language?  The machine prefers the lower level ones and 
performs better when presented with hand-coded assembler.  High level 
languages are a luxury for the human programmer end of the communication.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Sets and subsets

_(reply depth: 17)_

- **References:** `<b7tepn$9tb$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <joe_zitzelberger-B235AC.19492726042003@corp.supernews.com> <b8fps7$doe$1@panix1.panix.com> <joe_zitzelberger-D1AADE.09055128042003@corp.supernews.com>`

```
In article <joe_zitzelberger-D1AADE.09055128042003@corp.supernews.com>,
Joe Zitzelberger  <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com> wrote:
>
>> Joe Zitzelberger  <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com> wrote:
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
>programmers and machines must both speak if communication is to occur.

You have written in machine language and you say that 'Source code... is 
the language that both programmers and machines must both speak'; this 
does not appear to contradict your assertion that 'Source code is not 
programming' which supports the conjecture I provided.

[snip]

>If machine communication is the ONLY concern -- then why bother with ANY 
>high level language?

Because human beings are involved in communicating with each other as to 
what the programming is to make the machine do.  Consider:

COMPUTE A = (B * C).

COMPUTE GS = (HR * HW).

COMPUTE GROSS-SALARY = (HOURLY-RATE * HOURS-WORKED).

Programmatically these are identical and (given the same data-types and
values in the variables ) they will compile to the same ASM statements; 
the machine will react identically to all of them.

Given a 3:am S0C7 and a two-year programmer the programmer's reaction 
might not be as sanguine, granted.

DD
```

```
Joe Zitzelberger <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com> wrote:


>If machine communication is the ONLY concern -- then why bother with ANY 
>high level language?  The machine prefers the lower level ones and 
>performs better when presented with hand-coded assembler.  High level 
>languages are a luxury for the human programmer end of the communication.

Because it's faster to program in an HLL. I tested this twice at times when I
very fluent in assembly language -- once IBM mainframe and once Intel. I 'raced
myself' to write the same function in assembly and COBOL. I thought times would
be about the same. COBOL was three times faster in both tests.
```

it's a coincidence. If it happens three times, it's a pattern." Of course, if
we'd said that here, we'd have been flamed for failing to understand statistical
sampling theory. <g>

"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message

I heard the same idea expressed as:

"Once is happenstance,
 Twice is coincidence,
 Three times is enemy action..."

But my favourite logician expresses it best of all in his epic poem, "The
hunting of the Snark":

 "Just the place for a Snark!" the Bellman cried,
		As he landed his crew with care;
	Supporting each man on the top of the tide
		By a finger entwined in his hair.

	"Just the place for a Snark! I have said it twice:
		That alone should encourage the crew.
	Just the place for a Snark! I have said it thrice:
		What I tell you three times is true."

"What I tell you THREE times, is TRUE..."

It always amused me that three identical computers are used on Boeing 747s.
One is flyng the plane, one is watching the one that's flying the plane, and
the third is a backup in case either of the other two fail  <G> (I've been
to the cockpit in flight and confirmed this for myself (back in the days
when they would LET you go to the cockpit... I wouldn't recommend even
ASKING to do so nowadays...<G>)).

Pete.

In this case they appear to be identical, Mr Dashwood... consider a case 
like:

A: (assertion)

B: (disproof of assertion)

A: 'I should have said (second assertion).'

Now I might agree with you, Mr Dashwood, that A's second assertion 
'represents(s) some progress' if it includes an explicit acknowledgement 
of the disproof of A's first statement, such as:

A: 'I should have said (second assertion); (assertion) is wrong.'

>
>"Blessed are the Merciful, for they shall obtain Mercy..."

Ahhhhhhh... in this context I am of very mixed feelings about such things, 
Mr Dashwood.  Consider the contradiction inherent between the two 
following statements:

'When offering criticisms or corrections you should always leave the 
object of them A Way Out; people tend to fight harder and less rationally 
when they feel 'cornered'.'

... and ...

'To permit Sloppy Work is to encourage Sloppy Work.'

I have never felt that I have been able to establish a Proper Balance 
between these two... good thing I've avoided Positions of Management, eh?

DD

I have seen that happen, true, and it has been a Pleasant Thing... but

shown that he can grow roses no matter *what* kind of fertiliser you dump 
in his lap!'

>When I left the last place they had a party for me (not usual for
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>them today.

This is happening to me, now, and things are getting a bit... delicate 
with it.  I made it clear that I'd be available to deal with loose ends 
and unexpected minor messes... but I was not to be used as a free resource 
or a reference-book.  So far, so good.

But first... a bit of history.  I started at this client's site back in 
November, 2000, in a different department for a 'six-to-nine-month 
contract'.  In mid-July of '02 I was given three weeks' notice... not a 
bad run, I got a party at the end (oh boy, greasy pizza!) and I kept in 
touch.  August usually isn't a good month for finding work - too many 
folks on vacation - so I coasted, took a few courses (I'm now one exam 
away from being a certified Oracle DBA) and otherwise enjoyed time on the 
beach.

Second week in September came and I started sending off papers... and I 
get a call from a different manager at the old client, am I available?  
Yeah, sure, no problem, I come back in early October.

(My agency screwed me out of a referral-fee for referring myself to 
them... but that's another story.)

Anyhow, before end-of-project, before my stuff was even moved into Prod, I 
was given three-and-a-half-days' notice.  Not the most... pleasant of 
situations but hey, I'm what they call A Professional, I can deal with it 
and I do so.

That was about three weeks back, the beginning of April... last Thursday I 
got a call from the pimp, am I still available to go back to that client 
for a month's work?  I said this would not be a problem... but my rate 
would be US$n/hr.

The pimp squawked, of course... the *last* thing a pimp wants to do is to 
go to the client and say 'This will cost more money'.  She asked me why 
and I said I was disturbed by the precedents of 'bring me on, fire me, 
bring me back on again, fire me' and that these circumstances effectively 
decreased my billing rate (when annualised).

The pimp said 'this sounded reasonable'... and I've heard nothing back 
yet.

>
>My point is that it isn't always hostile and it certainly doesn't HAVE to
>be.

Of course, Mr Dashwood... nothing is always anything, including this 
statement!

DD

I'm sick of hearing bad behaviour being excused as "Human Nature"...

I'm Human, and I don't behave like that.

Why is it always the BAD side that is referred to as "Human Nature"?

Human Nature has given us scientific achievement, great works of Art and
Music, the capacity to feel and relate, and the spirit to continue when
things get tough.

I'm also sick of the excuse "Well, I'm only Human...". Being Human is not so
bad. It is definitely what you make it and the choice is YOURS!

As for "slick talkers" doing well, the ability to communicate (listen as
well as talk) is certainly an essential part of the survival kit here on
Earth in the 21st Century...

My experience is that the soured ones are the ones who envy the "slick
talkers". The ones who, instead of improving their own communication skills
(and it can be learned just like anything else), sat back and were beaten by
the "slick talkers".

If by "slick talking" you mean someone who communicates well, can think on
their feet, and is persuasive, well, these are all very valuable skills for
a manager to have. If you mean someone who talks down your case so that it
is never heard, who presents better than you do, well, whose fault is that?

Besides, no matter how well presented, a shit case is still a shit case and
it can be easily demolished without any special "slick talking" skills.

I still prefer to see the bottle as half full rather than half empty.

If you are not persuaded, then I guess I'm not much of a slick talker, or
maybe, I have a shit case and just can't see it...<G>

Whatever...optimism works for me and I enjoy my life.

Pete.

```


"Peter E.C. Dashwood" wrote:

> "James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@shaw.ca> wrote in message
> news:3EAFFE5B.3E198F2A@shaw.ca...
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Very true and I love the arts just as much as you do. But en route to our
achievements, don't forget the small number, who had a 'telling effect' on
civilization, Genghis Khan, Attila the Hun, Adolf Hitler, Josef Stalin......... Their
milestones have been in no way insignificant.

>
> I'm also sick of the excuse "Well, I'm only Human...". Being Human is not so
…[24 more quoted lines elided]…
> Whatever...optimism works for me and I enjoy my life.

It would appear I've touched a raw nerve with a 'wordsmith'.

First to Humanity - by far and away the MAJORITY are decent human beings. It doesn't
matter whether it's me sitting this side of the pond or you down there counting sheep,
or the humble fellaheen tilling a small patch along the Sweet Water Canal between
Cairo and Ismailia. The latter's dreams are essentially the same as yours and mine -
family, children, (not in your case - unless there's one you don't know about <G>),
prepared to toil and give his family his best. Other than our personal security, we
don't look to poke someone in the eye to achieve our career goals.

What we want as individuals is not what happens as the norm in the commercial world.
It 'kind of' was in the early 20th century, when merchants were concerned with making
money and at the same time some learned to take a paternal attitude to their staff -
which led to the idea you could work with one employer for a lifetime.
A slightly revamped version of this came back into play after WW2 - for my generation
the world appeared to be an oyster - but then puzzlement, concern, cynicism settled
in, Iron Curtain, Korea, JFK's assassination, Watergate.... you get the drift. To a
great extent the commercial world still tended to work in a genteel fashion, until
what, the late 1970s - and then an abrasiveness set in. Cocksure, can do, like Jobs
and Gates, eventually leading to the Enrons and Worldcomms, and the latter are by no
means finished.

As to being a slick talker, (or writer), that I am not. I just 'play' ME - WYSIWYG.
To illustrate using the 'old' Debenhams. Prior to being swallowed up by Burtons the
tailors, and then being sold off, to become the successful 'new' Debenhams - dammit
look at TV shots of affluent Kuwaitis - you see them ascending/descending an escalator
with DEBENHAMS emblazoned over the archway !

In '74 they got into a real schmozzle having acquired the family supermarket group
Caters. The accountants had screwed up royally in their management decisions - so
badly the auditors were prepared to qualify Debenhams accounts and most certainly were
going to qualify the accounts for Caters for the current year. Big meeting in London
between the bean counters and auditors. On command from the Financial Director I had
to be in attendance and took along one of my henchmen.

Once I got the lie of the land, as detailed above, it was obvious my presence was to
'soften the blow', offer some positives from a computer perspective where, hopefully,
the situation could be turned around, with a touch of this and a touch of that  - i.e.
stop the auditors taking the dreadful step of qualifying the accounts, which would
have sent the stock price down the tubes. No detailed prior knowledge of the topic nor
a pre-arranged script about my role - but the tactic worked - I obviously said the
right things..

I left the meeting with my henchman, Jim who commented, "Well, you handled that very
well". My 'Reply, I didn't handle it at all. I said exactly what I thought and what I
meant". Don't ask me what my personality is like - I've never seen myself on a video.
But sometimes common sense and sheer honesty can pay off - a 'slick talker' can become
very transparent in such a situation. BTW the 'slick talker' I'm referring to below,
who was two above me in the hierarchy, was at that meeting and said sweet FA !

Obviously you don't like 'slick talker' used as a definition - so let's flesh out this
particular 'slick talker' for you.

Some five years older than myself, Jesuit educated and had been trained to think like
a friggin Jesuit ! I don't know his EDP background, but had moved around UK at one
stage working  at Craven A (cigs/tobacco), along with above said Financial Director -
I'm extremely doubtful that he ever analysed and most certainly never programmed.

Tall, thinnish, bloodless, humourless, not particularly friendly, and offering only a
sickly smile. Imagine a pair of spidery hands the finger tips touching as though in
prayer and resting on his chin/mouth. - to my mind definitely an eminence grise. Jim
my sidekick summed it up best when he gave him a nickname which stuck - Kipper -
two-faced, yellow-bellied and gutless ! Remember me in private e-mail talking about
some twit who managed to write a book of 200 pages about Decision Tables - well twit =
Kipper !

I already said in the previous message, God knows how or why, the Financial Director
chose him to head up EDP and O & M, very much against the recommendations of the
consultants we had in at the time - said consultants had the ear of the chairman, (
one of their former directors), but that had no effect. For whatever reason, his
oiliness or smoothness just had some sort of appeal to the financial guy. On a rating
from 1 to 10, I would suggest his *practical* knowledge rated a minus 10.

Well I left Debenhams in '75 to come to the Great White North. Back in '74 we had a
new CEO who also brought along an admin sidekick called Jack. I had a farewell
booze-up (Hey ! Joe Muggins did all the buying. I like it better over here, 'they' not
the departee do the buying <G>). With reference to Kipper, I left Jack with a parting
message , "Get rid of the F..... SOB !".  With satisfaction, some six months or more
later, I heard he was gonzo a month or two after I left.

Whatever made you think I have a hang-up about 'slick talkers' <G>

I'd nearly forgotten. I swear to God, on this side of the pond, I've met two of
Kipper's cousins !!!

As regards being positive - well we have to be, otherwise we would go nuts. Even with
optimism, reflect on what is currently going on in the Middle East - one false step,
however well intentioned - and we are probably screwed for the next 50 years. Also I
would like to think the Israeli and Palestinian 'road map' will work - but when I look
at N. Ireland ??????

As a parting shot aimed at 'slick writers' <G> - you said 'there's a subtle difference
between OO Classes and components'  - I *don't* think so, but like many debates here
it isn't worth pursuing. However I do think the following quote from the text 'Core
Java' says it best :-

    "There are as many definitions of a component as there are programmers".

That being said - where's the starting point for designing a component.

Jimmy
```

> > If by "slick talking" you mean someone who communicates well, can think
on
> > their feet, and is persuasive, well, these are all very valuable skills
for
> > a manager to have. If you mean someone who talks down your case so that
it
> > is never heard, who presents better than you do, well, whose fault is
that?
> >
> > Besides, no matter how well presented, a shit case is still a shit case
and
> > it can be easily demolished without any special "slick talking" skills.
> >
> > I still prefer to see the bottle as half full rather than half empty.
> >
> > If you are not persuaded, then I guess I'm not much of a slick talker,
or
> > maybe, I have a shit case and just can't see it...<G>
> >
> > Whatever...optimism works for me and I enjoy my life.
>
> It would appear I've touched a raw nerve with a 'wordsmith'.

Not at all. Like I said,  I enjoy my life. It just gets tiresome to see the
continual negativity posted here.
>
> First to Humanity - by far and away the MAJORITY are decent human beings.
It doesn't
> matter whether it's me sitting this side of the pond or you down there
counting sheep,
> or the humble fellaheen tilling a small patch along the Sweet Water Canal
between
> Cairo and Ismailia. The latter's dreams are essentially the same as yours
and mine -
> family, children, (not in your case - unless there's one you don't know
about <G>),

You have NO PERCEPTION of my dreams and aspirations so don't make it
personal.

> prepared to toil and give his family his best. Other than our personal
security, we
> don't look to poke someone in the eye to achieve our career goals.
>
> What we want as individuals is not what happens as the norm in the
commercial world.

Then instead of just "wanting" why aren't you "changing" or "doing"?

Yes, bad things in happen in commerce. Yes, there are bad people in good
jobs and there are good people without a job. Bleating about it doesn't
change it. Deal with it.

> It 'kind of' was in the early 20th century, when merchants were concerned
with making
> money and at the same time some learned to take a paternal attitude to
their staff -
> which led to the idea you could work with one employer for a lifetime.

I believe a large number of people in the UK in 1927 would not agree with
this cosy view. In the dust bowl of 1930s America they weren't too enamoured
of it either...

Japan is probably the ONLY place (due to the post war boom in their economy
(in no small measure funded by US aid...) that has ever been able to
guarantee "jobs for life", and even they can't do it any more...

> A slightly revamped version of this came back into play after WW2 - for my
generation
> the world appeared to be an oyster - but then puzzlement, concern,
cynicism settled
> in, Iron Curtain, Korea, JFK's assassination, Watergate.... you get the
drift. To a
> great extent the commercial world still tended to work in a genteel
fashion, until
> what, the late 1970s - and then an abrasiveness set in. Cocksure, can do,
like Jobs
> and Gates, eventually leading to the Enrons and Worldcomms, and the latter
are by no
> means finished.
>

Jobs and Gates have created more jobs and made more millionaires than you've
had jobs in your lifetime. Yet you see "cocksure" and "can do" as negative
traits. I see them as "confidence" and "positivism". It is slanderous to
connect Jobs and Gates with Enron and Worldcomm. Whether we like their
companies and products or not there is no evidence to suggest illegal
dealing.

It is all a question of attitude.

<snip>
>
> As a parting shot aimed at 'slick writers' <G> - you said 'there's a
subtle difference
> between OO Classes and components'  - I *don't* think so, but like many
debates here
> it isn't worth pursuing.

So you haven't grasped it. It is not difficult. An unwrapped OO Class is not
a component. The action of wrapping OO code to comply with a defined
interface (COM or CORBA) enables it to behave as a component. They are not
the same thing but the difference is subtle. If you actually wrote and or
used some components you would realise the difference in an instant. Like
you said, it isn't worth pursuing....


> However I do think the following quote from the text 'Core
> Java' says it best :-
>
>     "There are as many definitions of a component as there are
programmers".
>

That book is out of date. At the time it was written that was a fair
statement. It was written to make people aware that this was a shortcoming
that needed addressing. It has been.  It is also true that from the Java
side (CORBA) there is more debate about what exactly constitutes a
component. On the MS side (ActiveX and COM) it is pretty clear. There are
quite specific definitions now as to what constitutes a "component".
(Although they are by no means unanimous.) The generally accepted one is: "
a .DLL or Java Class that conforms to the COM or CORBA interface
requirements." I use this one when conversing with other people who produce
components and we have no trouble understanding each other...

> That being said - where's the starting point for designing a component.
>

(I'm not sure whether that was a rhetorical question, but, in case it
wasn't...)

As it is intended to provide "pluggable functionality" I usually start with
a description of the functionality I want.

Next, I look at how that specific functionality can be generalised, and what
possible extra behaviours could be valuable. I then define the Properties
(and more and more I am using Properties, rather than the interface, to
determine the behaviours and simplify the Methods. If the properties are
preset before the method is invoked, the code for the method is simpler.
There is also less impact if the component is replaced by a later version,
as the interface to it doesn't change).

Then, the Methods are written and tested.

Finally, the whole thing is wrapped and registered. I let software do this
for me as it a tedious job if you do it manually. Then it is dropped into a
"harness" (container) using a graphic tool (drag and drop with PowerCOBOL)
and it is tested again. I usually also place it into a web page to check
that it works on the Web.

At this point I prepare the Help file for it and the icons.

When it passes all these tests and I am confident with it, it goes on the
component toolbar I use when building applications.

Currently I have 23 icons on this toolbar and the number will grow by the
time I finish the Web Portal I am currently writing.

The next step is to get into SOAP and Web Services and I expect to be doing
that during the Winter here.

The STARTING POINT is Object Oriented programming.

Pete.

"Peter E.C. Dashwood" wrote:

> > However I do think the following quote from the text 'Core
> > Java' says it best :-
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Possibly arguable that a book published in 1999, (4th Edition) and
sanctioned/published by Sun,, might lag behind current thinking, but I don't think the
following quote is in disagreement with what you have written :-

"Java will allow the component-based model of computing to take off.

No two people mean the same thing when they talk about components. (** This is the
second time they make this statement ***). Regarding visual controls, like ActiveX
components that can be dropped into a GUI program, Java 1.1. includes the Java Beans
initiative (see Volume 2). Java Beans can potentially do the same sorts of things as
Activex components except that they are automatically cross-platform and
automatically  come with a security manager. All this shows the success of the Java
Beans initiative will go far to making Java successful".

IF and WHEN I should get involved in components then it is very likely I would use
this book as a basis for modelling - there just isn't literature in COBOL.

You do have the advantage with Fujitsu, easily supplying the support runtime. Sure you
could sit down and write components in Net Express - but the real drawback for
distribution - those damned runtime fees !

Now even your approach using String2Num can be paralleled in a desktop GUI approach. I
commented once, "It was a nuisance allowing the user to input integers and then go
back to the to the Business class to reformat - i.e. re-display 9(05) as z9.999 BLANK
WHEN ZERO.". You made the observation this could be achieved.

I thought about it. As I've indicated I use one generic Dialog class, passing it the
particular parameters, (which you correctly refer to as Properties). I am setting up
the Properties for one instance. Taking the above pic formats, this allows me to
separately indicate lengths for an input field of 5 and a display field of 6.

The following is achieved within the generic Dialog, no matter how many 'screens' I
want to generate. 'On event....'.. take the input, check the format property, divide
by 1,000 and redisplay as z9.999. (My parameter has already set that this entryfield
as Numeric as opposed to text).

Naturally it is limited to the formats I currently need. If I was to use that generic
Dialog for accounting, (very, very highly unlikely), then I just add new Level 88s to
the format Property parameter to indicate the dollar/sterling/Euro formats . It
requires very little code to achieve this - and those money formats are merely added
as additional Level 88s in the copyfile.

Now that is in no way disputing your Component approach, and I am well aware that you
can do the above reformatting very easily, plus do your components right and you can
make a buck selling component units to other than the COBOL community. However, if I
don't get into either the Internet or Intranet - then any merit in my designing
Components is dubious. I think <G>. Better watch I don't say, "Never, never..." - the
penny might drop at a later date.

Ticking away in the back of my mind are possibilities such as follows; given a 10 x 24
table of values, perhaps highlight the lowest value, per column,  by re-displaying red
on white. Further for Items being inspected where I indicate 'Top' , Bottom', 'North'
'South' and have 'Exception lines' such as 'At Weld Join', highlight the description
as black on yellow. In the case of the Lowest Value that implies the ability to search
a column for the lowest. Other than checking the table data first, (after the user has
keyed in a value), I don't think either a Dialog or a Component could respond easily
giving the appropriate colour combination.

numeric input validated and then, once it is valid, providing it in a
number of formats, some of which are edited (like currency,
f'r'instance). This is achieved through properties of String2Num which
(once the component is instantiated) remain available...


> I thought about it. As I've indicated I use one generic Dialog class, passing it the
> particular parameters, (which you correctly refer to as Properties). I am setting up
> the Properties for one instance. 

Not sure about this, Jimmy. If you are passing it something at the
time you invoke it, that is an interface parameter. (It might well be
included in the object as a property also...) What I found through
some hard experience was that the LESS you provide through the
interface like this, the more stable the component is, the maintenance
when you issue a new version of it (maybe with some new methods), is
less impacted, and the Methods themselves are simpler to write, bcause
the properties are available internally. What I do now is instantiate
the component, make calls to get and set the properties, then invoke
the Method I want. Works for me...<G>

BTW, "BLANK WHEN ZERO" is best avoided...(it calls a subroutine for
many compilers). You can achieve the same effect by zz.zzz

<>Taking the above pic formats, this allows me to
> separately indicate lengths for an input field of 5 and a display field of 6.
>

"Peter E. C. Dashwood" wrote:

>
> I sympathise. Perhaps an approach for you could be to move towards
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>

V 4.0 is officially out in May, (that's this month now). I'm not sure but I believe it will
give the .Net link. Unfortunately - probably at a considerable price. I don't currently pay
maintenance. Chances are they will say, "You are now buying a NEW product - full price
please".

> I don't recall... There is certainly a case for having the allegedly
> numeric input validated and then, once it is valid, providing it in a
…[21 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Good point about BWZ.

Yes I've read your articles which as usual were enjoyable and informative. I'll have to
backtrack through the setup because it gave me an error, (can't find in Registry).
Simplest solution is probably Remove and then re-Add.

Let's see if we can tie this one down - by making a rough parallel comparison. String2Num as
I understand from your narrative is set with methods to accept and display a string, each
"method" saying "I want Text Uppercase", "This text as a numeric 99.999",  etc.

You design a dialog/window/webpage which needs three numeric fields :-

Entryfield A = 9,999,999.99
Entryfield B =        9,999.99
Entryfield C =            99.99

Can we accept for discussion that we both want only numeric ? So we now have three controls
with differing sizes - can be input as decimal point as above or could be integers as above
minus one character, (the decimal point) and the result is displayed as the full picture..

How do you achieve this - (a) three copies of String2Numeric or (b) StringAtoNumeric,
StringBTo..., AStringCTo..... etc. because of the differing sizes. Certainly option (b) is
the more bullet proof  as it is 'cast in stone'.

Now to myDialog - two entry point methods for Entryfields, (c) Pre-coded entry fields from
the designer (Dialog Editor) or (d) dynamically create the entry field -
(c) above contains the 'C' type #define for the ID, whereas (d) contains the x,y, w and h
co-ordinates. After that distinction, from the parameters it is determined whether they are
'text' or 'numerics', and for the latter are set up according to the display format.
Remember this is one instance of MyDialog and if I had, say, 30 different numeric display
formats within the one Dialog -  the 'events'  are recorded against a minimal number of
events - Method-ids "setup" (the (a ) and (b) above,  "display" and "accept and redisplay".
Regardless of the visual content, this reduces the code DRAMATICALLY.

Yes you can certainly screwup on the parameters in the first place - but that really is
covered by thorough testing of the parameter(property ) class. And it is the parameter class
I am testing - NOT MyDialog, which has already been proven. In the case of a 10 x 24 table -
no danger there, as each Entryfield is a repeat format done with a PERFORM VARYING and each
iteration of the PERFORM accessing method (d) above.

Hope that's clear enough. Again not disputing Components, but how does your design stack up,
( or rather ) compare to the above. Should I ever move to components then it makes sense, I
don't want to be out in left field, and hopefully, have the ability to re-use my existing
methods.

>
> Easy if you do it with SQL. And there is no need then to limit it to
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Yep I get that, displaying an existing record or sets of DB rows. The idea of colouring was
just a thought, a bit gimmicky and might not be introduced. Certainly indicating the
'lowest' for a column on input, would tend to signal to a user that perhaps they had made a
typo.

> Well you would have colour properties indicating foreground and
> background required. These could be preset (using SET) before the
> actual method was invoked. I'm sure it could be done, and fairly
> easily.
>

Disagree if necessary - but in my scenario above I see the colour properties being passed to
the Dialog Entryfield object based on an evaluation of the DB entries in Working Storage. (I
have a "setTheColour" method already in MyDialog - default of course is black on white).

Jimmy

> > "Peter E. C. Dashwood" wrote:

```
James Gavan wrote

> DANGER ALERT FOR BRITS : I know it's been in usage since the late 'twenties - but tell your good
> lady or spouse to stop using, "I am going to hoover the carpets". - we really shouldn't impinge
> on trademarks <G>
> 

Apparently, according to an article I read recently, but can't
presently find, it is not improper or illegal to verbalize (use a
noun, etc as a verb with or without an added suffix) a trademark,
though obviously trademark owners try to discourage it.  For example,
it is not valid to describe any vacuum cleaner as a hoover, unless it
is made by Hoover, but it is valid to hoover the carpet even if not
done with a Hoover product.  I think that Aspirin is beyond recall as
it has passed into the language, but I have never heard of anyone
being aspirin, aspirined or aspirinized when given or prescriped
aspirin.

Robert
```

```

"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:3eb5a10e.92130855@news.optonline.net...
> robert@jones0086.freeserve.co.uk (Robert Jones) wrote:
>
> >James Gavan wrote
> >
> >> DANGER ALERT FOR BRITS : I know it's been in usage since the late
'twenties -
> but tell your good
> >> lady or spouse to stop using, "I am going to hoover the carpets". - we
really
> shouldn't impinge
> >> on trademarks <G>
> >>
> >
> As for nouns, opium was originally a trademark of Beyer, the aspirin
company.

As a sometime shareholder of Bayer (note spelling please) I refute this
calumny vehemently. <G>

Bayer AG is a good and socially aware reputable company.

My etymological sources show the derivation of the word opium as:

opium - 1392, from L. opium, from Gk. opion "poppy juice, poppy," dim. of
opos "vegetable juice."

The first use of  the word "opiates" was around 1540 and it was derived from
Middle Latin going back to the original Latin source noted above.

As Bayer AG was not even a gleam in anyone's eye in the late 14th century,
they cannot be held responsible for the global use of the word.

As for registering it as a trademark... it is generally NOT possible to
register a word in general use as a trademark. (Although trademarks
sometimes come into general use, as we have seen in this thread).

You might as well try and register the use of "sugar"  or "water" or
"bullshit" as a trademark...

Quote your sources or retract please, Robert.

Pete.

> Let's see if we can tie this one down - by making a rough parallel comparison. String2Num as
> I understand from your narrative is set with methods to accept and display a string, each
> "method" saying "I want Text Uppercase", "This text as a numeric 99.999",  etc.
>

No, not at all.

It accepts a single string as a parameter when it is invoked. It
doesn't "want" anything or "expect" anything, not
even numbers. You pass it a string and ask it to see if that string
represents a valid number. (The number
[if it IS a number] could be edited, not edited, any length (up to 18
digits, although the passed string can be
longer to allow for sign characters and editing characters). It
decides whether it does represent a number or not,
and says "yes" or "no".

If the answer is "yes", it provides the number in a bunch of different
formats, along with some information about the
number.

Some examples might help...

Let's say the following are typical input strings (all of them would
set "itsANumber" to TRUE):

a. 1234.567890
b. -123,456,789.012345
c. 123
d. 123-
e. -123
f. .12345678901234567
g. -.123456789012345
h. $1,234.78
i. +12345.
j. 12345.+


You instantiate the component in the usual way...(Probably in an
INITIALISE-PARA)

	invoke COM "CREATE-OBJECT" 
               using "String2Num.String2Num.1" 
               returning String2Num
        end-invoke

(At this point we have an Object Reference to the component...So we
can invoke its methods and access its properties.)


	invoke String2Num "getNumber"
	       using inString
        end-invoke

(Let's say that "inString" contained example "a" above... Then the
state of the component's properties after this
invocation would be: [please note the word "integer" here is taken to
mean "the digits to the LEFT of the decimal point"; the word
"decimal" is taken to mean "the digits to the RIGHT of the decimal
point" - no correspondence will be entered into <G>])

dblDecimal    - decimal digits as 64 bit binary
.567890

dblInteger      - integer  digits as  64 bit binary
1234

intDecimal     - number of digits to right of the point
6

intInteger       - number of digits to left of the point
4

ItsANumber   - Boolean TRUE or FALSE
TRUE

realCurrency  - 64 bit VB type currency [14.4]
00000000001234.5678

realNum        - 64 bit real number floating point
(FP representation of +1234.567890)

strDecimal     - decimal digits as a string
.567890

strInteger       - integer digits as a string
+1234

Now I'll show how these properties can be useful by applying it to
your example...
 
> 
> You design a dialog/window/webpage which needs three numeric fields :-
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> How do you achieve this - 

Pretty easily by the following... I am assuming that you want data
entered by a user in the entry
fields, processed, and returned in edited format if it is valid, and
the user notified and given
a chance to correct it if it isn't (?)

Start by defining all the entry fields as string. It doesn't matter
what size you make them as
long as they are big enough to accommodate the largest entry you will
accept. (I would make them all
20 bytes so they will align nicely on the GUI screen, but it really
doesn't matter.

First, a couple of working-storage fields...

01      inString   pic x(20).

01      edit-mask.
	12 edit-mask-num  pic 999,999,999,999.99. (or zzz,zzz,zzz,zzz.zz or
whatever...)

Now, to business...

	move "Text" of EntryfieldA to instring
	perform check-numeric
	if "itsANumber" of String2Num = TRUE
        *>
	*> It is a valid numeric string (You would do any other validations
here
        *> before processing with it...)
	*>
           move edit-mask to "Text" of EntryfieldA
           (process using "realNum" or "realCurrency" which are
suitable for arithmetic
	    or simply store one of these values into a variable for later
processing after
	    validation is complete. There are a number of ways to do this but
you get the idea...)
	else
	   *>
	   *> It is not numeric. The User has been notified. We need to
insert the
           *> cursor on the offending field and exit to let him try
again...
	   *>
	   invoke EntryfieldA "insert-cursor"
           end-invoke
           (there are a number of ways to code this as
well...whichever way you choose
	    it needs to exit this process and update the screen, then wait
for input...)
	end-if

	move "Text" of EntryfieldB to instring
	perform check-numeric
	if "itsANumber" of String2Num = TRUE
        *>
	*> It is a valid numeric string (You would do any other validations
here
        *> before processing with it...)
	*>
           move edit-mask to "Text" of EntryfieldB
           (process using "realnum" or "realCurrency" which are
suitable for arithmetic
	    or simply store one of these values into a variable for later
processing after
	    validation is complete. There are a number of ways to do this but
you get the idea...)
	else
	   *>
	   *> It is not numeric. The User has been notified. We need to
insert the
           *> cursor on the offending field and exit to let him try
again...
	   *>
	   invoke EntryfieldB "insert-cursor"
           end-invoke
           (there are a number of ways to code this as
well...whichever way you choose
	    it needs to exit this process and update the screen, then wait
for input...)
	end-if
        
	move "Text" of EntryfieldC to instring
	perform check-numeric
	if "itsANumber" of String2Num = TRUE
        *>
	*> It is a valid numeric string (You would do any other validations
here
        *> before processing with it...)
	*>
           move edit-mask to "Text" of EntryfieldC
           (process using "realnum" or "realCurrency" which are
suitable for arithmetic
	    or simply store one of these values into a variable for later
processing after
	    validation is complete. There are a number of ways to do this but
you get the idea...)
	else
	   *>
	   *> It is not numeric. The User has been notified. We need to
insert the
           *> cursor on the offending field and exit to let him try
again...
	   *>
	   invoke EntryfieldC "insert-cursor"
           end-invoke
           (there are a number of ways to code this as
well...whichever way you choose
	    it needs to exit this process and update the screen, then wait
for input...)
	end-if
	...

 check-numeric section.
 cn000.
*
*  This subroutine checks the input field for valid numeric and edits
it if it is.
*
*  If it isn't, the user is notified by an intrinsic method in the
component, which
*  gives examples of correct input.
*
*  It is the caller's responsibility to place the edited data in the
appropriate screen
*  field if it is valid, or to insert the cursor on the screen field
if it isn't...
*

	invoke String2Num "getNumber"
               using inString
 	end-invoke
        if "itsANumber" of String2Num = TRUE
           move "realCurrency" of String2Num to edit-mask-num
        else
	   invoke String2Num "ShowError"
           end-invoke
        end-if
	.
 cn999.
	exit.

...


The code shown is somewhat contrived but it is intended to 
show how you can use encapsulated functionality in a COBOL
environment.
(This is NOT the way I personally use components, but it is _A_
way...)

I would not pretend that this is particularly elegant code but it
certainly would do
the job and do so under a number of possible scenarios.

(It could be improved by generalising it to be one set of code for ANY
entry field. If it
was my application, I'd do this, but this code would be necessarily
more complex and I am
trying to show an example. It therefore needs to be simple...)

As I have mentioned elsewhere, the important thing is to focus on the
functionality.

Here, the functionality is simply to establish whether something is
numeric or not and
to present it in a series of useful formats if it is. A single
polymorphic input field is
used and the component recognises what is required.

Using string for numeric input provides a number of advantages over
using numeric attributes on
a screen. The user can key the actual values, edited, or unedited, and
edited data can be
returned to the field, which it often can't be when numeric screen
attributes are used.

Furthermore, most hardware platforms support string input but NOT all
support numeric attributes
on screen fields.

As you know Jimmy, I HATE posting code in CLC because many people see
only style and not
what is intended to be achieved. (And I am really tired of these
endless discussions that persuade
nobody. I don't make my living from writing COBOL any more and I
really don't care.)

I have done it on this occasion because I know you prefer the 
tangibility of source code to an abstract discussion, but I won't be
entering into discussions
on this code unless they deal with the functionality it is seeking to
achieve.


>(a) three copies of String2Numeric or (b) StringAtoNumeric,
> StringBTo..., AStringCTo..... etc. because of the differing sizes. Certainly option (b) is
> the more bullet proof  as it is 'cast in stone'.
> 
 
No, as you have seen, none of those... The differing size is handled
by polymorphism, one string; one edit mask.


> Now to myDialog - two entry point methods for Entryfields, (c) Pre-coded entry fields from
> the designer (Dialog Editor) or (d) dynamically create the entry field -
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> iteration of the PERFORM accessing method (d) above.

You have lost me here...I really don't understand this. It looks a lot
more low level and complex than
I remember DIALOG being. I see no need to deal with GUIs at the X,Y,Z
level. Each field should be
an object with its own methods (like "insert-cursor"). in fact, I
treat each of these GUI items as
components...

> 
> Hope that's clear enough. Again not disputing Components, but how does your design stack up,
> ( or rather ) compare to the above. Should I ever move to components then it makes sense, I
> don't want to be out in left field, and hopefully, have the ability to re-use my existing
> methods.

That is legitimate concern. You SHOULD be able to use existing methods
just by re-packaging the Class.
> 
> >
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
> have a "setTheColour" method already in MyDialog - default of course is black on white).

I'd make the colours properties of the superset and inherit them down
the line. But there are many
ways to do this.

Hope this helps. It didn't take three hours this time, but it isn't as
comprehensive as the version that was lost <G>.

Cheers,

Pete.

That's fairly close...

It isn't that our ISPs won't pick them up. It is far more likely they
simply weren't relayed to a backbone here.

Sometimes if an ISP has a history of misbehaving (relaying spam and
refusing to stop, operating an open relay that can be used by
spammers, are two examples...) other ISPs will refuse to relay his
messages to any hubs. For private mail it isn't a big deal, but for
posts to public Newsgroups like Usenet, it simply means that that
channel is not available to him.

I wouldn't mind betting that shaw.ca is on a "blacklist" somewhere in
this part of the World. If so, it will be because of past
misdemeanours. But they should have been notified of this. Maybe they
were and just chose to ignore it...

Never mind, I can see your posts in Google. (The disadvantage there is
that they appear much later than they would directly in Usenet, and
bloody Google has now lost two responses on me so I'm loth to use
it...)

Anyway, glad you got the stuff and hope it gets the wheels turning.

Pete.
