```


"Peter E. C. Dashwood" wrote:

> OK here we go again...<G>
>
> I'm trying it one more time in Google and will attempt to save it
> occasionally...
>

OK printed and had a good read and I think I understand what you are driving at.
Thank you for taking the time out to write the detail - yes I *do* prefer tangible examples.

If our friend 'Grinder' is reading this - it's an update on what I gave your previously.

However, it all really starts at that quote "There are as many definitions of components as there
are programmers". Perhaps more accurately there are as many ways of designing programs as there
are programmers. We each arrive at an individual style. Given say a dozen of us wrote a program to
Edit Customerfile, using a DB which was either COBOL files or (R)DBMS, non-OO or OO we would get
12 different solutions all achieving the same result, perhaps some with slicker techniques than
others.

Some clarification on what I'm doing and particularly the word 'Dialog' which I think has misled
you.

- VISOC - came solely with Dialog Editor and GUIs, Win APIs and Screen Section, but not Dialog
System or Bill's favourite 'toy' Report Writer. Although many went after the beta at about $800 -
many existing Dialog System users held off.

- Net Express V 3.0 - re-introduced the Dialog System you know - but with enhancements. Talk about
on again, off again --   Report Writer was re-introduced. V 3.1  introduced many enhancements, but
the ones I'm specifically aware of - Type Library Assistant (using OLEs), Java Support classes and
the module Forms for web pages - haven't used this but somewhat like your F/J it generates
supporting source to create Web pages.

- Dialog System - that's the 'animal' you know from DOS days, but with enhancements and ability to
invoke OO classes. I haven't used it, already committed to GUI classes, but have referred to
examples and took the basis for my menu creation from a D/S example.

- Dialog Editor and GUIs - that's what I use. Difficult to describe, without going to great
lengths, but anybody interested can bring up the MicroSOFT version - \msTools\bin\dlgedit.exe

It gives you an an empty palette with a Toolbox with control icons which you can drag and drop
into the palette, resize etc. As you create a control you give it a unique ID, e.g. ID_ENTRYFIELD1
and allocate a unique number. The finished dialog is saved to the multi dialog files xxxx.h
(containing #define statements for linking) and xxxxx.res which is an 'image' of your screen with
control co-ordinates and a set or properties per individual control.

I use the Micro FOCUS version of dlgedit.exe which is based on above. The toolbox contains 23
control icons. There are a set of default properties per control, but let's concentrate on
ENTRYFIELD, (which is what String2Num is about) - but the same general concepts apply to other
controls. The properties are reasonably comprehensive but I'm guessing (from the screenshots I've
seen) not quite to the degree of F/J, although that web Forms module probably is. You can change
the default font to Roman or Courier - Whoops ! It changes the font for the whole dialog - that's
not what we want, so invoke a GUI method to change the font for a particular Entryfield. (But see
my parameter method below - I can tell it which font to use for a particular entryfield)
Similarly, colour is not listed as a property, again invoke Colour class.

For an entryfield the default is Text pic x(...); you select 'Number', pic 9(...), if you want an
integer - note, no decimal point allowed. At this stage the only significance to length is how
you've designed, reference your comment about lining them up to make the dialog aesthetically
pleasing. (No problem of course to allow a user to enter a decimal number if you specify the
default as 'Text').

The tool above and how to use it. Recall some years back I sent you that source code ? You thought
it was 'ORRIBLE - so did I ! But to be fair, the M/F demo was doing it in 'longhand' to illustrate
umpteen techniques. Back to design again, where to start. I had a book 'APIs How To", and thumbed
through it and found what appealed to me. Say Customer Edit :-

Left-hand side of screen a set of entryfields, perhaps radio buttons etc and on the right a
Listbox to display Existing records. That turned my crank - show the user what already exists in a
listbox, from which (s)he can select or alternatively enter a fresh record on the left-side. At
the bottom of course the traditional pushbuttons.. This 'split screen' approach doesn't work with
everything but it's not a bad basis on which to design.

Back to using the Dialog in the resource file. You now go through a laborious routine of
specifying each field to create in your dialog, plus add methods for other than default property
settings. Fairly early on I got the message, my own dictum, "If you find yourself writing this
more than once, take a second look - can it be turned into a generic method". It has developed
over stages, becoming more refined, plus as we all tend to have our coding style it is possible to
come up with an outline class template for Edit File X.

This is how it works currently :-

[Edit Class]-------[Property/Parameter Class]------[Instances of MyDialog]

Initially checking the resource file I set the Propertties up manually, but a bit of a pain in the
butt for accuracy and time wise. Then a comment from Richard about how he generates parameters
triggered something lurking in the back of my mind. I backtracked. (Incidentally 'Backtracking' is
something Will Price refers to in his book, and it's also an admission by Donald. With a complete
lack of OO COBOL code to refer to, at this stage it is inevitable. Hopefully as time goes on and
more code becomes available, we will get a progresion at Mr. B enhances code from Mr. A, Mr. C
enhances code from Mr. B etc....) : -

- 1 - Templates for classes (a) Edit X,  and (b) Parameters X
- 2. Read the Resource file (line sequential) and extract the info I need.
   (Not picture perfect getting this info, but it works 90% plus).
- 3 Having done #2, read in the 'master' Edit X to output 'Edit X + 1' to include the skeleton for
methods for entryfields,
   identified as methods "EF-fieldName", similarly LB = Listbox, RB = RadioButton and PB =
PushButton
- 4 Using #2  again, read in the 'master' ParametersX to output 'ParametersX + 1',  generating the
WS table per method for
   each type of control.

MyDialog contains methods to create all controls and return events for those controls I currently
use. Let's assume the Edit Class needs 6 dialogs - then we have Property/Parameter Classes 1
through 6 and we create instances of MyDialog 1 through 6. Doesn't matter if I'm creating a
zillion controls they all are controlled through MyDialog, repetitively using methods particular
to a specific control or 'generic' methods like "show", "hide", "enable", "disable" etc.

- Edit Class -

            invoke ParameterClass using self(Edit Class) returning MyDialogInstance

- ParameterClass -

        invoke MyDialog using EditClass(the self above) returning MyDialogInstance
                - this instance is passed back to Edit Class so that it can subsequently invoke
MyDialogInstance directly

- MyDialogInstance -

        stores the reference to 'self' (Edit Class) so that it can return events directly to Edit
Class

Parameter Class - contains something like :-

*>-------------------------------------------------------------
Method-id. "begin".
*>-------------------------------------------------------------
Local-storage section.
01 ls-id                            pic x(4) comp-5.
etc...
Procedure Division using     lnk-Caller
                             lnk-Parent
                             lnk-Resource
                   returning lnk-Dialog.

set os-Parent   to lnk-Parent
move 0770 to ls-id ( this is the ID allocated to the particular Dialog in Dialog Editor)

invoke TabbedMyDialog "start"
       using     lnk-Caller, os-Parent, lnk-Resource, ls-id
       returning os-Dialog

 set lnk-Dialog to os-Dialog

 *> Note sequencing of methods below which activates controls
 *> in the same sequence as their field numbers, so that field
 *> number = index position in the Dialog Control Collections

 invoke self       "ControlCollections"         *> Control Type
 invoke self       "EntryFields"                *> ++02
 invoke self       "RadioButtons"               *> ++03
 invoke self       "Pushbuttons"                *> ++04
 *>invoke self     "CheckBoxes"                 *> ++05
 *>invoke self     "Droplists"                  *> ++06
 invoke self       "Listboxes"                  *> ++07
 invoke self       "Labels"                     *> ++08
 invoke self       "ListboxColors"              *>
 invoke self       "LastField"
 invoke os-Dialog  "zCloseEvent"

End Method "begin".
*>-----------------------------------------------------------

Concentrating on the entryfield parameters :-

*>--------------------------------------------------------------
Method-id. "EntryFields".
*>-------------------------------------------------------------

*> This method only handles entryfields created using the Dialog
*> Editor which are allocated IDs in the files mfres.h and
*> mfres.cpy. Dynamically created entryfields are handled
*> separately.

*> See file "dilogprm.cpy" for list of parameters

Working-storage section.
78 TL                                value 28.
78 ML                                value 30.
01 ws-A.

*> Resource Sequence Field
*> ID       Number   Length    A D  R,C F P V    MethodName
*> IIII     SSS      LLL       9,99,9,9,9,9,9,x-------30--------
*> ----     ---      ---       - -- - - - - - ------------------

*> ws-Alphanumeric :
*> A : 0   = Numerick      1 = Lowercase    2 = Uppercase

*> ws-DisplayFormat :
*> I : 00 = Integer       13 = Decimal 1.3  etc.....

*> ws-ReturnType :
*> E : 0  = No return      1 = Return value to Invoker

*> ws-ClearType :
*> C : 0  = EmptyField     1 = InsertField

*> ws-FontType :
*> F : 0  = DefaultFont    1 = MsFont       2 = SystemFont
*>                                          3 = MonospacedFont
*>

*> ws-Position :
*> P : 0 = LeftJustify    1 = Centered      2 = RightJustify

*> ws-ViewType
*> V : 0 = NotReadOnly    1 = ReadOnly

  05.
    10 pic x(TL) value '0778,001,004,2,00,1,0,0,0,0,'.
    10 pic x(ML) value 'EF-PROBE-ID'.
  05.
    10 pic x(TL) value '0771,002,015,1,00,0,0,0,0,0,'.
    10 pic x(ML) value 'EF-PROBE-MAKE'.
  05.
    10 pic x(TL) value '0772,003,003,0,12,0,0,0,2,0,'.
    10 pic x(ML) value 'EF-PROBE-FREQUENCY'.   *> 9.99
  05.
    10 pic x(TL) value '0773,004,004,0,13,0,0,0,2,0,'.
    10 pic x(ML) value 'EF-PROBE-DIAMETER'.    *> 9.999
  05.
    10 pic x(TL) value '0775,005,010,1,00,0,0,0,0,0,'.
    10 pic x(ML) value 'EF-PROBE-TESTBLOCK'.
  05.
    10 pic x(TL) value '0776,006,010,1,00,0,0,0,0,0,'.
    10 pic x(ML) value 'EF-PROBE-CALIBRATION'.
  05.
    10 pic x(TL) value '0751,007,004,0,00,0,0,0,0,0,'.
    10 pic x(ML) value 'EF-DATE'.

01 ws-B redefines ws-A.
   05 ws-C occurs MaxEntryFields.
      10 ws-ID                       pic 9(04).
      10                             pic x.
      10 ws-field                    pic 9(03).
      10                             pic x.
      10 ws-Length                   pic 9(03).
      10                             pic x.
      10 ws-Alphanumeric             pic 9.
      10                             pic x.
      10 ws-DisplayFormat            pic 99.
      10                             pic x.
      10 ws-ReturnType               pic 9.
      10                             pic x.
      10 ws-ClearType                pic 9.
      10                             pic x.
      10 ws-FontType                 pic 9.
      10                             pic x.
      10 ws-Position                 pic 9.
      10                             pic x.
      10 ws-ViewType                 pic 9.
      10                             pic x.
      10 ws-MethodName               pic x(ML).

01 n                                 pic x(4) comp-5.

Procedure Division.

  initialize Dialog-Params
  perform varying n from 1 by 1 until n > MaxEntryFields

      move ws-ID             (n) to Dialog-ID
      move ws-field          (n) to Dialog-field
      move ws-Length         (n) to Dialog-Length
      move the rest etc......

      invoke os-Dialog "zCreateEntryfield" using Dialog-Params
  End-perform

End Method "EntryFields".
*>-------------------------------------------------------------

Note that the Level 05s in the above table were generated from the Resource file as I described
above, with a set of defaults. I manually change to show lengths, decimalization (DisplayFormat)
etc...

Next Stage - the Instance of MyDialog and how Entryfields are handled :-

*** Step A - creation - invoke "zDynamic...." or "zCreate....."

*>------------------------------------------------------------
Method-id. "zDynamicEntryfield".
*>------------------------------------------------------------
Linkage section.
copy "dilogprm.new" replacing ==(tag)== by ==lnk==.
Procedure Division using lnk-Params.
 invoke TextEntry "new" using self returning lnk-object
 invoke lnk-object "SetRectangle" using x y w h
 invoke lnk-object "SingleLineEntry"
 invoke self "zCreateEntryField2" using lnk-Params
End Method "zDynamicEntryfield".
*>------------------------------------------------------------
Method-id. "zCreateEntryfield".
*>-------------------------------------------------------------
Linkage section.
copy "dilogprm.new" replacing ==(tag)== by ==lnk==.
Procedure Division using lnk-Params.
 invoke self "getObjectFromId"
        using lnk-ID returning lnk-object
 invoke self "zCreateEntryfield2" using lnk-Params
End Method "zCreateEntryfield".
*>-------------------------------------------------------------
Method-id. "zCreateEntryfield2".        *> PRIVATE
*>-------------------------------------------------------------
Local-storage section.
01 ls-event                                 pic x(4) comp-5.
01 ls-Format.
   05 ls-Format1                            pic 9.
   05 ls-Format2                            pic 9.
01 ls-length                                pic x(4) comp-5.
Linkage section.
copy "dilogprm.new" replacing ==(tag)== by ==lnk==.
Procedure Division using lnk-Params.
 invoke lnk-object "accessSystemEvents"
*> Dialog-Alphanumeric
 Evaluate true
     when NumericK   invoke lnk-object "numeric"
     when UpperCase  invoke lnk-object "uppercase"
 End-evaluate
*> Dialog-FontType
 if lnk-FontType <> zeroes
    invoke lnk-object "setfont" using os-Font(lnk-FontType)
 End-if
*> Dialog-Position
 Evaluate true
     when Centered       invoke lnk-object "centered"
     when RightJustify   invoke lnk-object "rightjustified"
 End-evaluate
*> Dialog-ViewType
 Evaluate true
   when ReadOnly
       invoke lnk-object "readonly"
       move zeroes to Ls-Length
   when lnk-DisplayFormat <> zeroes
        move lnk-DisplayFormat to ls-Format
        compute ls-length = Ls-Format1 + Ls-Format2
   when other
        move lnk-Length to ls-length
 End-Evaluate

 if ls-length <> zeroes
    invoke lnk-object "setLength" using ls-length
 End-if

 initialize                ws-MethodRec
 move lnk-MethodName    to ws-MethodName
 move lnk-ClearType     to ws-ClearType
 move lnk-DisplayFormat to ws-DisplayFormat
 move lnk-ReturnType    to ws-ReturnType

 if lnk-Line is numeric
    move lnk-Line          to ws-Line
    move lnk-Column        to ws-Column
 End-if

 invoke os-Collection(1) "add" using lnk-object
 invoke os-Collection(2) "add" using ws-MethodRec

*> Dialog-ClearType

 move gained-focus to ls-event
 invoke lnk-object "setEventto" using
   ls-event, self, "setGainedFocus ", lnk-Field

*> Dialog-DisplayFormat and Dialog-ReturnType

move lost-focus to ls-event

Evaluate true
   when lnk-DisplayFormat <> zeroes
        invoke lnk-object "setEventto" using
            ls-event, self, "displayDecimals ", lnk-Field
            *> See method below "displayDecimals"
   when MakeReturn
        invoke lnk-object "setEventto" using
          ls-event, self, "returnEntryNow ", lnk-Field
   when other
        invoke lnk-object "setEventto" using
          ls-event, self, "ElementChanged ", lnk-Field
End-evaluate

*> Note : For specific records, when the user is displaying or
*> entering data, the format by Default = IMPERIAL. If the user
*> has selected 'METRIC' from the Application Menu, then as dialogs
*> are created from the appropriate Dialog parameter class - Metric
*> indicators are substituted for Imperial formats - e.g., the
*> Readings Dialog shows Imperial values of 13 (9.999 inches) in the
*> parameters. Based on the Metric flag - 23 (99.999 cms) is
*> substituted in the Entryfield table to replace the '13'

End Method "zCreateEntryfield2".
*>--------------------------------------------------------------

Strictly speaking "zCreateEntryField2" is far too big, negating one of my own self-impposed rules,
"A method should not normally be more than half a page". Arguably I could split it up, but it is
to do with the functionality of creating one
control.

*** Step B - Event triggers user has entered something; if it is numeric formatted,
invokes self for this method :-

*>--------------------------------------------------------------
Method-id. "displayDecimals".   *> PRIVATE
*>--------------------------------------------------------------
*> Used for re-displaying decimal numbers entered as integers

Local-storage section.
copy "dilogprm.new" replacing ==(tag)== by ==Dialog==.
copy "displayF.cpy".
*>------------------ diusplayF.cpy ---------------------------
01 ls-display12                  pic   z9.99  blank when zero.
01 ls-display13                  pic  z9.999  blank when zero.
01 ls-display23                  pic zz9.999  blank when zero.
01 ls-display33                  pic zz9.999  blank when zero.

Note : The copyfile contains just what I currently need for inches and
centimetres. Get involved in accounting - just add formats required.
*>-----------------------------------------------------------
01 n                             pic x(4) comp-5.
01 ls-length1                    pic x(4) comp-5.
01 ls-length2                    pic x(4) comp-5.
01 ls-object                     object reference.
01 ls-string                     object reference.
01 ls-Text1.
   10 occurs 1 to 10
      depending on ls-length1    pic x(01).
01 ls-Text2.
   10 occurs 1 to 10
      depending on ls-length2    pic x(01).

Linkage section.
01 lnk-Field                     pic x(4) comp-5.

Procedure Division using lnk-Field.

 invoke os-Collection(2) "at"
        using Lnk-Field returning ws-MethodRec
 invoke os-Collection(1) "at"
        using lnk-Field returning ls-object
 invoke ls-object "getText" returning ls-string
 invoke ls-string "trimblanks" returning ls-string
 invoke ls-object "Getlength" returning ls-length1
 invoke ls-string "getValueWithSize"
        using ls-Length1 returning ls-text1
 invoke ls-string "finalize" returning ls-string

Evaluate true

 when ws-DisplayFormat = 12
        compute ls-Length2 = function length(ls-display12)
        compute ls-display12 = (function numval(ls-Text1) / 100)
        move ls-display12 to ls-Text2

 when ws-DisplayFormat = 13
        compute ls-Length2 = function length(ls-display13)
        compute ls-display13 = (function numval(ls-Text1) / 1000)
        move ls-display13 to ls-Text2

 when ws-DisplayFormat = 23
        compute ls-Length2 = function length(ls-display23)
        compute ls-display23 = (function numval(ls-Text1) / 1000)
        move ls-display23 to ls-Text2

 when ws-DisplayFormat = 33
        compute ls-Length2 = function length(ls-display33)
        compute ls-display33 = (function numval(ls-Text1) / 1000)
        move ls-display33 to ls-Text2
End-evaluate

initialize                        Dialog-params
move lnk-Field                 to Dialog-Field
move ls-Length2                to Dialog-length
move ls-Text2(1:Dialog-Length) to Dialog-text

invoke self "setEntryField" using Dialog-Params
invoke self "ElementChanged" using Dialog-Field

End Method "displayDecimals".
*>--------------------------------------------------------------

Of course, for existing records, I'm passsing - from Edit class data I have for COBOL files/DBMS -

which displays :-

*>-----------------------------------------------------------
Method-id. "setEntryField".
*>-----------------------------------------------------------
Local-storage section.
01 ls-object                         object reference.
01 ls-string                         object reference.
Linkage section.
copy "dilogprm.new" replacing ==(tag)== by ==lnk==.

Procedure Division using lnk-Params.

  invoke CharacterArray "withLengthValue"
         using lnk-length, lnk-text returning ls-string
  invoke os-Collection(1) "at"
         using lnk-field returning ls-object
  invoke ls-object "setText" using ls-string
  invoke ls-string "finalize" returning ls-string

End Method "setEntryField".
*>-----------------------------------------------------------

To summarize - for a decimalized entry field, four methods :-

    (1) Create - registering the event (callback) to a class/method
    (2) Display for existing records
    (3) Lost focus - this is the event/callback setup by # 1; redisplay using # 2
    (4) PushButton OK - return entryfield values to Edit class - addressed to specific 'receiving
methods'

While MyDialog contains a lot, without actually poring over the code, it's difficult to get a
handle on what it is doing in total.
Still, with ENCAPSULATION there's no need to know is there <G> However a summary, and indicating
some of the 'why(s) I'm doing it this way'. Now the following list is not in any particular order,
and many features are inter-related, coupled of course with my *own* design concepts.

- Firstly N/E does not as yet have auto garbage collection but contains following methods to
destroy objects - (1) finalize a single, (2) deepfinalize a collection and (3)
deepfinalizeWithDepth for multi level collections.

- Recall Simon's source for Print Preview ? Umpteen objects each identified singularly. Ugghhh !
He's latched on since <G>.
Prior to creating controls I set up two OrderedCollection (added to, next in sequence), (a) one
collection holding the displayable objects and (b) the second an associated text reference holding
the method-name to return to, plus some other info - check back on the methods above to see
reference to these two collections. I'm constantly referencing these collections to store/retrieve
objects by field number - Level 88's from the Parameter Class Now if I really had it figured, I
would clone one class to hold both the object reference and the text in one element - rather than
accessing parallel collections..

- I want to destroy the dialog - invoke the two collections above "deepFinalize" - gone !

- Entryfield lengths - input and displayable. While I might show a (text) entryfield as 60
characters long, for appearance, if it was say,
a Corporate Customer Name (stored as 40 characters) - I don't want the user happily keying in 56
characters. When he re-displays that field next time he will see I've truncated it to 40
characters. So specifying input length - the keyboard comes to a dead halt after the 40th
character. Similarly with display I want to COMPUTE NUMVAL etc... based on the pre-designed
length.

- This particular application is well over 80% just keying in numbers 99.999, but most are  0.123
with the odd 1.234. Like
the old DOS system, for quickness user keys in without decimal point and I do the dividing,
re-displaying.

- Using x, y, w and h (in Microsoft-speak = x, y, cx, cy). Recall up above about designing Dialogs
and allocating unique names and IDs. In trepidation that's how I started out with a screen
containing 325 controls - doesn't work; not unreasonably there's a maximum of 250 or 255 controls
per dialog. Apart from which doing the following READING1, READING2 .... etc., all the way up to
READING240 - is definitely a non-starter ! So when you get into repetitive table fields it makes
sense to DYNAMICALLY create them - back to the methods above no difference between 'static' and
'dynamic' once you've identified which you are creating - they both then invoke "zEntryField2".

- I asked somebody what they did when they changed data in an existing record - Ripley's Believe
it or not - 'Well given the speed of modern computers, just update the whole record.". To use your
phrase, "Not on my watch !". Particularly that 10 x 24 table which is no longer a COBOL occurs
record. So initially MyDialogInstance has a MasterChangeFlag set to false. Anything changed the
flag is set to true and when user clicks Pushbutton-OK the flag is returned to the Edit Class,
which in turn invokes  MyDialog to return field values. However as new fields are being entered or
changed in MyDialog, I also set an ElementChangeFlag within the collections referred to above.
Given say 280 elements (dialog controls) in the collection, and ONLY one field has been changed,
then that is the only one signalled back to the Edit Class.

- Validation of records occurs after PB-OK, being done in Business Logic (Edit Class)

- Colouring - at the moment I'm just using the standard set of colour names, black, cyan,
lightcyan etc., and use colours on an exception basis. (At some future stage I may dabble with the
Colour Common Dialog to get different hues). I stick with the conventional defaults of grey and
white, at the moment highlighting Listboxes in different colours. At this stage I see no necessity
to have a colour code as an initial parameter - just send the backgroun/foreground colours to a
particular object as appropriate.

Now I could go on an on with insights as to why I'm doing it this particular way - you can bet as
soon as I've sent this message, I'll remember some more ! So any observations and I'll try and
explain them.

As you observed it is LOW LEVEL - whereas you've gone with what you have, quote : "For most of the
components I build, I use Fujitsu PowerCOBOL because it takes care of the 'housekeeping'
(Component Wrapping) so beautifully, and spares me a lot of mind-numbing detail, but that is not
the only way".

Bearing in mind the tool I had didn't generate code, I had to follow from that basic route. Took
one hell of a while to get there, but so far given a set of Properties/Parameters I have,
regardless of number of dialogs, controls  or styles  :-

- 80% generated Business Logic code
- Properties/Parameters generated
- MyDialog - one class that handles it all the Windowing

Stretching it a bit, but one could view my methods above as being a "Multi-Purpose Entryfield"
component with four methods. Certainly I would like to see a fair number of the 'Whys' above built
into any specific component I designed. And logically at this time, can't see I'll be dashing
around the world trying to flog components anyway <G>

Jimmy
```

```
I went through this, Jimmy, but I simply haven't the time at the moment to
sit down and apply myself to it blow by blow.

It seems to me you have built something that is very workable for you and
that can only be commended.

What I saw looks fine.

There is a lot more detail here than I would want to know, and it still
looks like maintaining source code to me, but it doesn't matter if you find
it useful.

I like simple, encapsulated functionality. That's why I use components.

If I had to solve your problem of huge amounts of input I would look at the
functions I want... say, check and validate numbers, set colours, display
back to the user (edit, if you like)... and so on, and then I would build a
little component "production line" that would take raw input from a text
field and end up with something usable internally, and an updated screen.
Once  each little "machine" was installed in the "production line", then it
is a fairly trivial exercise to ensure that each input field gets processed
by it. It's a bit like pushing scrap metal in one end and getting knives and
forks out the other. Each "machine" does a bit more towards the end product.
The "machines" themselves are very simple (like String2Num) and that usually
means they are fast...

As far as I am concerned, it isn't about selling components (although I do
intend to have a crack at that sometime this year), it is about simplifying
system development and eliminating source code maintenance.

I say it again...it is the Business Functionality we should be concentrating
on, not the intricacies of computer programming. It is because that HASN'T
happened that more and more users are doing it for themselves (without
"expert" computer programmers) and more and more Companies are outsourcing
their IT and focusing on their primary business instead.

Modern software and development techniques are empowering non technical
users to be able to build their own systems. This is the death knell for
us...(although it will be a few years yet before we are COMPLETELY redundant
<G>) and we can see the first effects of this sea change in lowered contract
rates and fewer jobs.

Ironically, I wrote around 6 years ago that "History will show 'computer
programming' was a phenomenon of the latter half of the 20th century." We
are certainly heading that way...

If nothing else, this exercise will have given you an idea of how
encapsulated packets can be made to work.

If you get any glimmers from it, then I consider my time well spent. <G>

Pete.

<snip of very comprehensive post>

Not *quite*, Mr Plinston... at least according to 
http://info.astrian.net/jargon/terms/g/godwin_s_law.html :

--begin quoted text:

Godwin's Law prov.  [Usenet] "As a Usenet discussion grows longer, the
probability of a comparison involving Nazis or Hitler approaches one."
There is a tradition in many groups that, once this occurs, that thread is
over, and whoever mentioned the Nazis has automatically lost whatever
argument was in progress. Godwin's Law thus practically guarantees the
existence of an upper bound on thread length in those groups. However
there is also a widely- recognized codicil that any intentional triggering
of Godwin's Law in order to invoke its thread-ending effects will be
unsuccessful.

--end quoted text

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Sets and subsets

_(reply depth: 15)_

- **From:** "Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2003-04-27T21:07:21+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3eab9ddb_1@127.0.0.1>`
- **References:** `<b7tepn$9tb$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <3ea88130.21326254@news.optonline.net> <b8ab71$1dv$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz> <3eaaa1e8.160795747@news.optonline.net> <b8eseu$2fv$1@panix1.panix.com> <3eab4d82.204732010@news.optonline.net>`

```

"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:3eab4d82.204732010@news.optonline.net...
> docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
>
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> No no no. When I program, I'm not talking to the machine, I'm talking to
the
> next programmer who must understand my code.

That has certainly been the role of COBOL over the years. It's main strength
has been it's "English like" nature which meant it could communicate to the
next programmer (or bean counter) as well as to the machine.

However, if you think about this a bit it fails on several counts...

1. ANY language (spoken or programming) communicates to people who know that
language.
2. It is no longer desirable (or, in some cases, even possible...) to
maintain the corporate Network with a single language. Certainly it is
possible to obtain multi-lingual (in the computer sense) programmers, but
they cost more, and more and more companies are simply cutting their losses
and outsourcing their IT.
3. There is only a requirement for "the next programmer to understand my
code" if that code is likely to need maintenance. The use of components and
re-usable objects (often purchased from third parties) renders this
requirement obsolete for all but the last bastions of Fortress COBOL. The
world is changing. COBOL sites are not... Logical conclusion; COBOL will
die.

(Actually, I'm becoming hopeful that it will survive for things like Batch
processing which we are still going to need for some time yet, and I see
building business components in COBOL as a very viable use for the Language,
but the traditional use of COBOL as the "one language wonder" has a very
limited life span. I wrote a few years ago that I expect to see it gone by
2015. I still hold that view.)

Pete.

>"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message

>next programmer (or bean counter) as well as to the machine.

COBOL's English-like grammar is a convenience to the programmer, but it is truly
a programming language. A non-programmer cannot really understand it; he can
only read at a superficial level, recognizing words here and there. Have you
ever watched a movie in a foreign language you understand slightly but aren't
fluent in? That's what it's like for an accountant trying to read COBOL. 

>However, if you think about this a bit it fails on several counts...
>
>1. ANY language (spoken or programming) communicates to people who know that
>language.

I just agreed. 

>2. It is no longer desirable (or, in some cases, even possible...) to
>maintain the corporate Network with a single language. Certainly it is
>possible to obtain multi-lingual (in the computer sense) programmers, but
>they cost more, and more and more companies are simply cutting their losses
>and outsourcing their IT.

Outsourcing doesn't remove people from the loop. They outsource to the companies
I work for, ultimately to me. 

>3. There is only a requirement for "the next programmer to understand my
>code" if that code is likely to need maintenance. The use of components and
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>die.

Do you realize what you're saying in this COBOL forum? You have more guts than
me. <g>

What language will these reusable objects be written in? How about COBOL? It
seems to me that OO is just adding one level of isolation to the architecture. I
would liken it to going from assembly language to HLL. 

I'm not criticizing nor minimalizing OO. I love OO, although for different
reasons. If nothing else, it portends to raise the quality of code by taking it
out of the hands of corporate IT departments, which have a poor track record
over the last 40 years. 

>(Actually, I'm becoming hopeful that it will survive for things like Batch
>processing which we are still going to need for some time yet, and I see
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>2015. I still hold that view.)

We've been reading similar predictions for more than 20 years. I recall hearing
visionary Ed Yourdon predict 1990 in the late '70s. The deadline always seems to
be 10-15 years out. Yourdon thought C and Unix would replace COBOL and
mainframes. Well, there was _some_ validity in his prediction.

It requires no particular courage to express an unpopular opinion, provided
it is backed up with the evidence as to how you reached it. Also, regulars
here know that I am committed to the COBOL language and it is very dear to
my heart.

However, I would be stupid if I let my love for the language blind me to
what is happening in the market place. I have genuine concerns about the
careers of youngsters trying to get into commercial computer programming and
maybe thinking that COBOL will do the trick. It did for us, but that was
thirty years ago... There IS still a place for COBOL but it is not
altogether clear what that place is at the moment. It is almost certain that
it will NOT be the place it has traditionally held.

Whether I am courageous or not is debatable; whether I am stupid or not, is
not...<G>


> What language will these reusable objects be written in? How about COBOL?
It
> seems to me that OO is just adding one level of isolation to the
architecture. I
> would liken it to going from assembly language to HLL.
>

My opinion about using the best tools for the job (including COBOL) is a
matter of record.  The objects will be (and are being) written in the
language that best suits the purpose for which they are intended. At the
moment I am the only person I know who regularly writes components in COBOL
(if anyone reading this is doing this too, please drop me a note; it gets
lonely out here on the frontier...<G>). I am doing everything I can to
persuade others to do the same. Some of the regular posters here are using
OO COBOL and doing a good job of it. I see components as being "the next
step" on from this.

> I'm not criticizing nor minimalizing OO. I love OO, although for different
> reasons. If nothing else, it portends to raise the quality of code by
taking it
> out of the hands of corporate IT departments, which have a poor track
record
> over the last 40 years.
>
> >(Actually, I'm becoming hopeful that it will survive for things like
Batch
> >processing which we are still going to need for some time yet, and I see
> >building business components in COBOL as a very viable use for the
Language,
> >but the traditional use of COBOL as the "one language wonder" has a very
> >limited life span. I wrote a few years ago that I expect to see it gone
by
> >2015. I still hold that view.)
>
> We've been reading similar predictions for more than 20 years.

Well that dismisses mine then...<G>

The "we've heard it all before" reaction is fairly typical and what I have
come to expect. It is exactly this attitude that is causing a false
complacency amongst some COBOL people. And yet we see more and more COBOL
jobs disappearing and people being scrapped. I warned of this 7 years ago
and suggested what could be done about it.  I have consistently during that
time advocated the expansion of skill sets in just about everything I have
written. Some people took that advice and are working today.

I really don't care whether you agree with my position or not. (at the end
of the day, it is not me who suffers...) I simply call them like I see them.

Pete.

"Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message

>> Outsourcing doesn't remove people from the loop. They outsource to the
>companies
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>over the market. 

I've worked for the major players -- EDS, Accenture, Keane -- as well as minor
players. The difference is majors roll in their own management team and
methodology whereas small players are just body shops. The number of jobs
doesn't change, just the methodology.

Company management thinks they're saving money by going with small players.
Wrong. Bill rate is about the same -- $100-150/hour either way.

>No wonder there is less and less scope for COBOL
>programmers within industry.

While that might be true, I don't think it should be attributed to sourcing.
It's primarily a function of CS-grads' language preference. They've been taught
to hate COBOL --  too verbose, dinosaur language, etc. 

I worked for a very bright and ambitious manager, three years out of a
prestigious college, who didn't even know COBOL was a language. I said something
like 'it's written in COBOL' to which he responded, "No it's not. It's written
in Batch." He thought COBOL was a scripting language, and he was directly
supervising COBOL programmers,

All new development in that shop was C++. COBOL was maintenance only. 

At another shop, the manager said, "If we have to make extensive changes, we'll
just rewrite the whole program in C. It's easier."

>The objects will be (and are being) written in the
>language that best suits the purpose for which they are intended. 

They'll be written in the language de jour, currently C#, Java and maybe Python.
Next year, something else. Selection isn't based on suitability for the job,
it's based on fashion. 

>At the
>moment I am the only person I know who regularly writes components in COBOL
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>step" on from this.

I'm tempted to join you.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Components

_(reply depth: 16)_

- **From:** LX-i <DanielJS@NOSPAMKnology.net>
- **Date:** 2003-04-27T16:23:16-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3EAC4A44.1080505@NOSPAMKnology.net>`
- **References:** `<b7tepn$9tb$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <3ea88130.21326254@news.optonline.net> <b8ab71$1dv$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz> <3eaaa1e8.160795747@news.optonline.net> <b8eseu$2fv$1@panix1.panix.com> <3eab4d82.204732010@news.optonline.net> <3eab9ddb_1@127.0.0.1>`

```
Peter E.C. Dashwood wrote:
> 3. There is only a requirement for "the next programmer to understand my
> code" if that code is likely to need maintenance. The use of components and
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> die.

You know, I've seen you say something like this several times (even went 
to aboutlegacyprogramming.com and read your article there).  I realize 
that the breadth of your programming experience far exceeds mine, and 
this question isn't meant to be "smart-aleck"-y at all.

What happens when these components need to do something different? 
You've proclaimed that they will next to eliminate the need for 
maintenance programming, but it seems to me that as your business rules 
and other needs change, you may need different behavior from these 
components than you currently get.  So, it would follow that you would 
still need someone knowledgeable in COBOL to "maintain" this COBOL 
component.

You've also got the issue of the "driver" program that executes the 
components in a specific order.  This may need to change as well.

I realize that one of the benefits of components is that they don't 
necessarily have to be written in COBOL - you can have a COBOL driver 
utilizing a few components in C++, some in COBOL, and some from other 
languages.  I guess I still don't see that components, while reducing 
maintenance, will eliminate.  I'm the programmer for the closest thing 
we have to system-level components, and for this project, I've ended up 
rewriting almost all of them.  :)

> (Actually, I'm becoming hopeful that it will survive for things like Batch
> processing which we are still going to need for some time yet, and I see
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> 2015. I still hold that view.)

I wish I had a good PC-based COBOL compiler to play with some of the 
.dll stuff.  How much do they cost (in general)?
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Components

_(reply depth: 17)_

- **From:** "Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2003-04-28T12:39:54+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3eac786a_2@127.0.0.1>`
- **References:** `<b7tepn$9tb$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <3ea88130.21326254@news.optonline.net> <b8ab71$1dv$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz> <3eaaa1e8.160795747@news.optonline.net> <b8eseu$2fv$1@panix1.panix.com> <3eab4d82.204732010@news.optonline.net> <3eab9ddb_1@127.0.0.1> <3EAC4A44.1080505@NOSPAMKnology.net>`

```

"LX-i" <DanielJS@NOSPAMKnology.net> wrote in message
news:3EAC4A44.1080505@NOSPAMKnology.net...
> Peter E.C. Dashwood wrote:
> > 3. There is only a requirement for "the next programmer to understand my
> > code" if that code is likely to need maintenance. The use of components
and
> > re-usable objects (often purchased from third parties) renders this
> > requirement obsolete for all but the last bastions of Fortress COBOL.
The
> > world is changing. COBOL sites are not... Logical conclusion; COBOL will
> > die.
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>

It's OK, Daniel, you have as much right to an opinion as anybody else
here...<G>

> What happens when these components need to do something different?
> You've proclaimed that they will next to eliminate the need for
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> component.

Yes, in dynamic markets the Business needs DO change. But if you bought the
component from a third party, you simply tell them that you need to add some
more methods and THEY do it. (In many cases, like Tax legislation changes,
the vendor will simply send you an update  as part of your subscription or
even as goodwill to encourage future business...) Or you replace it with a
more comprehensive component from another vendor. Either way, the risk to
your Business is minimal and you don't need to maintain an expensive IT
development department.

But, more fundamentally, the WAY in which systems are designed and developed
is different in such an environment. The system is designed around required
Business functionality and it is Business Analysts and Users, working
jointly, who prototype the needed functions and decide what components to
use. They will use tools which are basically "drag and drop" and manipulate
components via a graphic interface. In this way, work that would
traditionally take a week of analysis and programming can be done in
minutes. (I've seen it, and it blew me away...<G>)

The implications of moving to component based design are far too huge for me
to outline here, and it would be wrong for me to debate this way of working
in the context of a traditional COBOL development framework. (It is a
quantum shift away from the traditional COBOL development methodology).The
approaches to Component Based design are much more RAD oriented than the
usual COBOL "Waterfall" (see: http://66.152.52.10/archives/v4/v40501.asp )

I appreciate your reading the article which desribes the use of components
and gives a sample one (I was amazed at the number of people who actually
took the time to download the sample component, it was in the hundreds, and
I expected maybe a dozen. More than 12,000 people read the article in the
first few weeks of it being published...)

Could I press you to read the original article outlining what COBOL people
need to know about components, located at:
http://66.152.52.10/archives/v3/v30201.asp ? It should help to clarify how I
see components fitting into the future.

I'm sorry to post these links, but it is the only way I can cover the ground
you may wish to debate. There is far too much to post here.

>
> You've also got the issue of the "driver" program that executes the
> components in a specific order.  This may need to change as well.
>

That is being handled more and more by scripting. OO languages like Java are
also ideally suited to this purpose. For myself, I tend to use JavaScript,
VBScript, and ASP for quick Web Deployment (and EVERYTHING is Web Based now,
whether it be for the Company Intranet or the actual Internet). I can't
afford some of the fancy tools that will graphically glue your components
together so I still do this manually, but more and more corporates are
opting for packages.

Changing the sequence of operations, inserting and removing components, is
done in minutes. The proces is then tested and when all concerned are
satisfied it does what it is supposed to, it is releaseed to live. The whole
operation can take just a couple of hours and most of that is getting
agreement from all interested parties <G>...


> I realize that one of the benefits of components is that they don't
> necessarily have to be written in COBOL - you can have a COBOL driver
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Like I said above, you are working in a different environment. It is like
comparing apples and oranges.

> > (Actually, I'm becoming hopeful that it will survive for things like
Batch
> > processing which we are still going to need for some time yet, and I see
> > building business components in COBOL as a very viable use for the
Language,
> > but the traditional use of COBOL as the "one language wonder" has a very
> > limited life span. I wrote a few years ago that I expect to see it gone
by
> > 2015. I still hold that view.)
>
> I wish I had a good PC-based COBOL compiler to play with some of the
> .dll stuff.  How much do they cost (in general)?
>

You can get Fujitsu 3.1 for free. It is a good compiler and it can produce
.DLLs. try www.adtools.com

Pete.

> > component from a third party, you simply tell them that you need to add
some
> > more methods and THEY do it.
>
> So we're just passing the buck to them.

No Howard, that is neither accurate nor fair.

We are making a Business decision to obtain IT service from sources outside
the company.

The advantages of this are that we no longer need to maintain an expensive
in-house IT development group.

(Instead, Business users can put systems together themselves, using advanced
software. The IT department is a few people who maintain the Network and
spend time training users...even this CAN be outsourced. This approach is
very attractive to senior management because it means they can get on with
running the business (which is what they understand), rather than being
sidetracked into an IT morass that sucks funds and achieves little, but
everybody keeps saying they HAVE to have...)

Do you consider it "passing the buck" when you send your car to be serviced?

Yet you are quite capable of changing the oil and filters yourself (it
requires no special expertise...)

You are basing your objection on the environment you understand at the
moment.

As I mentioned elsewhere, that is not appropiate to Component Based
Development and it would be stupid for me to debate this on your ground. (It
just isn't sensible...)


> The coders need to write code that is
> understood by other coders.  It doesn't have to be understood by the user.
If
> the IS staff is users instead of coders, that wisdom doesn't change.

It does when NOBODY (within your business) is writing code. I know it is
hard to grasp that concept after a lifetime of working in a COBOL
environment, but nevertheless, that is the case. The "code" is OBJECT code,
the "glue" for it is generated by advanced graphical interfaces like SQL
Server's Data Transformation services (DTS), and the systems are put
together by end users working jointly with Business Analysts. There is no
on-site "programming", as is understood in the COBOL coding sense.

The sad thing about this is that if COBOL shops had been less resistant to
new techniques like OO (and from there to component development) many of
them by now would be developing components for their own Business and
selling them to other similar businesses. In this way, instead of being cost
centres, they would be profit centres and thereby justify their own
existence and the jobs of the people in them.

It didn't happen. Now it is too late. The Fortress has been bypassed by the
barbarian (anti-source code heretic) hordes on their swift little ponies. It
isn't even worth besieging it... It is just irrelevant as it is being
overtaken by events.

Never mind. Batch programs can be fun to write...<G>

Pete.

```

On 28-Apr-2003, "Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote:

> Do you consider it "passing the buck" when you send your car to be serviced?

I consider the advantages of making the car easily serviceable as important to
the service station as they are to me.   The people who built the car need to
take into consideration the requirement for service when they design and build
the car.

It doesn't matter whether the service is done at the dealer or at the home.  
Changing to the dealership can change the design somewhat - for instance,
requiring an expensive tool to read and program the car's computer.   But still,
the car needs to be designed with service in mind.

Same thing for source code.   Maybe you don't see the source code.  But that
doesn't mean it doesn't need to be serviced (maintained).   The responsibility
for its maintenance is passed (the buck) to different programmers.   But someone
still has to be able to work with the source code.   So design the source code
with this in mind.
```

```
"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote:

>On 27-Apr-2003, "Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote:
>
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
>If the IS staff is users instead of coders, that wisdom doesn't change.

The programmers no longer work for management whose expertise is widget
manufacturing. They now work for a company whose only business is software.
Generally, they would be more competent (beause they're hired by peers) and more
receptive to new technology. 

In corporate IT departments, the incentive for IT management is butt-covering,
not saving money. This produces inefficiencies and  long delays. For instance,
retesting every application when a reusable component is changed. 

In third-party companies, the incentive is opposite. Get the product out the
door QUICKLY and (less important) inexpensively. They don't make money unless
they deliver. 

This isn't idle speculation. I've worked both sides and seen these dynamics. The
'pimple-faced geeks' working for computer-industry companies can program circles
around 99% of applications programmers in terms of quality, and get the job out
in one tenth the time .. in my limited experience.
```

<dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote:

> That has certainly been the role of COBOL over the years. It's main strength
> has been it's "English like" nature which meant it could communicate to the

> language.

There was an interesting bit in the latest Mike Murphy movie 
"Goldmember" where he and his script father feel the need to communicate 
without being understood.  They drop off into this mess of UK slang that 
was not understandable to anyone.  But they were speaking English (UK), 
how is it that people who also speak English cannot understand?

It is often not enough to understand the language on a word for word 
level, to understand all things spoken in it.  Cobol has its constructs 
that can be hard to understand for one not completely immersed.

(CharClasses in SpcNames come to mind as one of these slangs)


> 2. It is no longer desirable (or, in some cases, even possible...) to
> maintain the corporate Network with a single language. Certainly it is
> possible to obtain multi-lingual (in the computer sense) programmers, but
> they cost more, and more and more companies are simply cutting their losses
> and outsourcing their IT.

Computer languages have the burden of communicating between the human 
and the computer.  This is true for Cobol and all others.  Even when 
outsourced emergency fixes will still be necessary.  The outsourcee 
company still needs the ability to maintain the software or the 
outsourcing company will find its business at risk. 


> 3. There is only a requirement for "the next programmer to understand my
> code" if that code is likely to need maintenance. The use of components and
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> die.

As the old PHB joke goes "what I need is a specific list of unexpected 
problems that might occur".  I recently had a brain dead consultant from 
(company that rhymes with "accent" and "cure") hold up initial 
production for 23 hours waiting for this list.

As silly as it sounds, it is an interesting problem.  What do you do 
when things don't work?  When the system is dumping and you have nothing 
to look at?  This may be surprising to some on CLC, but code is 
sometimes not perfect on the v1.0 release and users are always better 
then testers at discovering bugs.

Quite often the "next programmer" is the "original programmer" a few 
weeks later trying to correct his/her mistakes.

I've spent half of the last decade diagnosing vendor code bugs (the 
other half creating my own bugs).  I'm totally unable to agree with your 
idea that purchased code is bug free code, it is just the hardest to fix.


> (Actually, I'm becoming hopeful that it will survive for things like Batch
> processing which we are still going to need for some time yet, and I see
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> 2015. I still hold that view.)

I don't think Cobol has ever been a "one language wonder" anywhere but 
CLC.  But I expect to see it well past 2015.  I don't think it will die 
in the forseeable future.

"Peter E.C. Dashwood" wrote:

> A good response Joe.
>
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
> Pete.

I agree you probably wont persuade Joe, but Howard *might* give a little. It's not
intentional but you've created a 'mystique' or red herring by your reference to being
anti source, and you don't have to be bothered about source.

So either I write an OO class or you  design a component - one or a combination of the
following happens, in your case :-

1. Pete Dashwood writes OO COBOL code
2. POWER FORM generates code from your visual presentation design
3. You may code some in Java
4. You may add in a third party component to your component

Like it or not there *is* friggin source code - it sure ain't called vapourware <G>.
The only source that is encapsulated (hidden) to Pete Dashwood is # 4. Assuming you
use #'s 1 through 3, the source for those are also are encapsulated - quite simply
because Pete Dashwood is not going to give the source to a fourth party, but just
provides method names and sending/receiving parameters for the various methods to
work.

Now as to the point do the methods in a component work without failing - try this one
on them - take the source for just one method from your StringtoNum2 for a particular
feature and show them it is 'proven'..

In response to Charles' posting the Murtach source code, I listed two file classes,
Line Sequential and ISAM. In a sense these too, could be considered as components,
totally reusable once written. A dialog is a visual presenter of data, whereas a COBOL
file is a repository for storage of data. Now my examples do not include all the file
VERBS, just those I personally need. To accommodate Richard, I could have included
Open Extend, and before he jumps all over me, specifically test that only file-status1
= "0" <G>.

If anybody can see the likelihood of possible bugs in my file classes - sing out - If
I have potential errors, I will thank you most profusely and immediately change my
code so I wont get caught with my pants around my ankles <G>.

Now proven code also applies to SQL written in Net Express, PROVIDING you use their
ESQL Assistant to generate your query syntax - it is bullet proof - and that claim
applies to both non-OO and OO programs.

A rider - what pissed me off about RW's, "You might just as well do performs....".
He says he loves OO - well writing one big chunk of COBOL code with a series of
PERFORMS  and annotating it with 'Method-id" before the first line and "End Method"
after the last line - just isn't OO. It's back to the Section crap (ooh sorry - forgot
PECD likes Sections <G>). To my mind that approach can lead to bugs. Surely the whole
of purpose of OO is to write short and sweet self-contained methods (paragraphs),
attempting as much as possible to avoid using global W/S with values 'localized' to a
method via USING.... RETURNING.... And these small snippets of code, (methods), stand
on their own merit.


Jimmy, Calgary AB

> vapourware <G>.

If the objects are simple enough, the source can be used only to compile more
object code for different environments.   If the source is more complex, then
somebody will be doing some maintenance, or at least cloning it to new objects
that work differently.

And if we replace programs with systems, and commands with objects - our
complexity comes back.   We are just coding systems instead of programs.   Our
systems need to be designed with the same criteria for maintenance that used to
be used for coding programs.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Sets and subsets

- **From:** "James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@shaw.ca>
- **Date:** 2003-04-29T17:35:23+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3EAEB41D.C67C5F3D@shaw.ca>`
- **References:** `<b7tepn$9tb$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <3ea88130.21326254@news.optonline.net> <b8ab71$1dv$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz> <3eaaa1e8.160795747@news.optonline.net> <b8eseu$2fv$1@panix1.panix.com> <3eab4d82.204732010@news.optonline.net> <3eab9ddb_1@127.0.0.1> <joe_zitzelberger-7C25B7.09273428042003@corp.supernews.com> <3eadb1d5_2@127.0.0.1> <3EADE6CB.8ECFF964@shaw.ca> <b8m3a3$rjm$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`

```


Howard Brazee wrote:

> On 28-Apr-2003, "James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@shaw.ca> wrote:
>
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
> be used for coding programs.

You still don't get it do you ? Let's step back before the eighties, when I think it
is a fair statement to say, Wang was predominant in word processing. Secretaries at
single Wang work stations or a series of work stations tied into a hard disk, just
typed. The 'attractive' part of those systems was that Wang automated the drudgery,
opening files, closing files, copying, moving, calculating - all done by the typists
with macros - from memory a combination of two keys.

In programming terms, the equivalent of those macros could be linked together -
example - bring up an invoice format, access a customer names list and for products
access a product list; typist enters quantity and the software extended the cost,
and eventually produced the invoice total.

The above could be extended to producing statistical reports. Grossly inefficient as
it took all night, (I know, having done it !) - the weak feature being the software
had to search through text character by character or by jumping TABS. You could see
it visually happening on your screen.

Crude perhaps, but those macros are parallel to methods. Within each macro you can
only do specific things - they are pre-programmed. I've long forgotten specifics,
but I'm sure if you wanted 'myMacro', that could probably be achieved by linking two
or more Wang macros.

OO COBOL - without question, things can go wrong, but the errors are gong to occur
in your Business Logic or Business Control programs. Look back to my Line Sequential
and ISAM classes - I haven't had any errors from them, they merely report GIGO sent
from the Business Logic. As an example I change an existing record and invoke
"Write" instead of "Rewrite" in the file class - sure enough I will get a file
status error, display a window message and notify the error back to the Business
class.

The important thing is to distinguish between :-

- Business programs
- Support classes (utilities)

Micro Focus provide me with in excess of 200 support classes, covering the groups,
Base, GUIs, OLE, Java support. They *do* provide the source, (helping newcomers, as
a reference to how things can be done). But other than a cursory look, I *never* go
to those sources. In terms of MAINTENANCE - not once have I had an M/F class blow up
in my face ! (As a purely wild guess I probably use some 50-80 of those classes,
many indirectly because they are referenced through inheritance).

It's worth noting that both F/J and M/F provide their users with IDEs which are OO.
For the most part these work extremely smoothly, for the bread and butter stuff.
Occasionally users pick up on errors, which tend to be attributable to the less
commonly used features. The vendors find these errors and issue Fixpacks. Ah haa ,
it does happen ! But bear in mind these IDEs are far *more* complex than anything
you or I am likely to write.

I suggested some time back that control of in-house support classes should be
controlled perhaps by the DBA. The installation determines what will be their set of
support classes and they are produced accordingly. KISS must prevail in the design
of these classes. The complexity you refer to *must* be confronted by 'chopping up'
the complex logic into sub-levels - individual methods which only do part of the
job. Any changes to business rules then allow you to confine the change to the
sub-method (i.e., perhaps where you invoke Self).  If every programmer in an
installation is 'allowed' to write his own support classes - then that's like
awarding him the 007 badge !.

So a current support class no longer provides all your needs. Take my ISAM class -
somebody makes a request for READ PREVIOUS. (1) Add the method "readPrevious" and
(2) test the recompiled support class with one Business program that reads the
results - it is proven by one business program, without testing every conceivable
program that could access that routine.

It's interesting if you look at Smalltalk or Java NewsGroups - they never get their
knickers in a twist fiercely debating the equivalent of SECTIONS, PERFORM THRU EXIT,
PERIODS or GO TOs. Their groups are an interchange of 'How do I do this ?" using the
appropriate syntax or support classes/methods.

It's history now - but reflect on what was achievable on the Wang w/ps.

Jimmy
```

On 29-Apr-2003, "James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@shaw.ca> wrote:

> You still don't get it do you ?

I guess not.   The discussion is whether code should be designed to be
maintainable.   You keep telling me why you don't see the code at an atomic
level and expect me to infer from that that it does not have to be maintainable.

I keep replying that SOMEBODY has to see the code.

Maybe "atomic" is the wrong word.  The people who write compilers write at the
atomic level.  I bet they consider how difficult it is to maintain their code. 
Compilers change.    Then the people who write CoBOL programs program at the
molecular level.  We need to make sure our programs are maintainable.

You are saying the molecular level is obsolete.   You are building at the brick
level.  Or maybe you are assembling pre-built walls and roofs.   All find and
good.   But you are also building cities where we were building a kitchen.

Don't your cities need to be maintained?   Don't you still need to design your
assembly of components to be maintainable?   Or do you simply throw out your
existing city and build another when business needs change?

Your components are bigger and more powerful I agree.   I just don't see how
this means that they don't need to be designed to be maintainable.

Howard Brazee wrote:

> On 29-Apr-2003, "James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@shaw.ca> wrote:
>
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Yes somebody H-A-D to see the code. In my case - ME, (one-man band). I was the class
builder. But once I have written that code and proven it - it is forgotten. Much of
my code, I've forgotten what it does, just doing a quick reference check on methods
names and parameters. (In the case of large installations, this has to be
documented). But you have to differentiate between Class builders and, I hesitate to
use the word, coders.

> Maybe "atomic" is the wrong word.  The people who write compilers write at the
> atomic level.  I bet they consider how difficult it is to maintain their code.
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Yes building at the brick level if you like; in Pete's case (components) - he has a
box of Leggo (pre designed modules) and puts the pieces together. No my name is not
Saddam - I'm quite happy at the kitchen level.

>
> Don't your cities need to be maintained?   Don't you still need to design your
> assembly of components to be maintainable?   Or do you simply throw out your
> existing city and build another when business needs change?
>

Bad analogy. Of course cities need to be maintained - sewers clog up, pipes break,
due to corrosion or severe snowstorms, flooding etc., electricity, telephone and
cable TV systems have to be repaired. You call it maintenance, repair would be a
better word - same difference.

You can't use the engineering word 'maintenance' in the same breath for programs -
they don't corrode. Let's breakdown program maintenance :-

- You inherit gawdawful systems, (as I have as a Systems Manager). You can sometimes
get into a protracted political fight - the only viable solution is a rewrite NOT
maintenance.

- Pretty amateur coding by some who lack professionalism, and full of screw ups each
time you run it. This is not maintenance but a failure on quality control. In
extreme cases the offender should be fired. These 'bad' programs can hang around for
sometime - in hindsight the program should have been handed over to somebody else to
do completely from scratch.

- Business rules change. There's no simple answer to this - are they built into the
Business Logic program or do they reside in a support class, or are they accessed
from a database *only* via a support class. Does this particular *rule* only exist
in one Business program, or is it referenced from several business sources, albeit
with slightly different returned data. If there are multi accesses to this
particular business rule, then it does appear to be a candidate for 'class-ifying'.

>
> Your components are bigger and more powerful I agree.   I just don't see how
> this means that they don't need to be designed to be maintainable.

Spell out exactly what you mean by the latter. What is the difference changing a
PARAGRAPH or changing a METHOD.

At this point I'll retire. Nobody is feeding me money to plug OO - just that I use
it and it works for me. I have no idea what is going on in the mainframe world, nor
F/J because they don't have a chat site. Micro Focus - believe me, there's an awful
lot of folks, from the Americas, (North and South), Europe (East and West) plus the
folks from the Antipodes, who are as busy as bees coding away in OO/GUI/Web.

If you wish to continue in generalizations in an abstract sense - that's not my
preference. I'm much more interested in seeing elegant code which has been posted
here recently - including some from RW ! (Whether it works or not, I wasn't
interested because it dealt with issues either not relevant or not particularly
important to OO at the PC level). There's sure a lot of the abstract that goes on in
here - to what purpose ? They all go away and do their own thing, without the
slightest change to their thinking - some, by admission, sweating it out until
retirement.  Meanwhile, back at the ranch - folks are having problems getting COBOL
jobs. (I sincerely wish them luck).

If you wish to get specific and say, "Now here's a concrete example, with some code,
show me how OO could be beneficial, plus how do I maintain it ?", then I'll give it
a shot.

Other than that, as Eliza Doolittle sang :-

"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message

> On 29-Apr-2003, "James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@shaw.ca> wrote:

Howard. You still don't get it...

>
> I guess not.   The discussion is whether code should be designed to be
> maintainable.   You keep telling me why you don't see the code at an

> level and expect me to infer from that that it does not have to be

changed, Howard.

with OO and Components.

Bad analogy. We are still building buildings. The collections of them may

COBOL paradigm. As for the molecular level being obsolete, it is not that we
don't need molecules, it is just that we can pop them into reality much
faster...<G> And they are "smarter"... they can clone themselves and they
"understand" various requirements and provide the required functionality...

Yes, but NOT by changing or maintaining source code... Instead, we use
graphic tools to drag and drop components into the correct sequences. It is
completely flexible and we can test it immediately and change it if it does
not provide the right FUNCTIONALITY. We may add or delete components but we
DON'T CHANGE them.

The focus is on Business FUNCTIONALITY, not computer programming.
(Many will say "It's about time...")


>  Or do you simply throw out your
> existing city and build another when business needs change?
>
No, only in extremis. See above.

They are not necessarily bigger... The collections of them are certainly
more powerful.

Here's a thought for you... If you decided to change the garden wall, what
change would you make to a brick?

Pete.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Sets and subsets

_(reply depth: 23)_

- **From:** "Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net>
- **Date:** 2003-04-30T14:40:25+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<b8on8p$iel$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`
- **References:** `<b7tepn$9tb$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <3ea88130.21326254@news.optonline.net> <b8ab71$1dv$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz> <3eaaa1e8.160795747@news.optonline.net> <b8eseu$2fv$1@panix1.panix.com> <3eab4d82.204732010@news.optonline.net> <3eab9ddb_1@127.0.0.1> <joe_zitzelberger-7C25B7.09273428042003@corp.supernews.com> <3eadb1d5_2@127.0.0.1> <3EADE6CB.8ECFF964@shaw.ca> <b8m3a3$rjm$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <3EAEB41D.C67C5F3D@shaw.ca> <b8ml18$83p$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <3eaf3622_2@127.0.0.1>`

```

On 29-Apr-2003, "Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote:

> Yes, but NOT by changing or maintaining source code... Instead, we use
> graphic tools to drag and drop components into the correct sequences. It is
> completely flexible and we can test it immediately and change it if it does
> not provide the right FUNCTIONALITY. We may add or delete components but we
> DON'T CHANGE them.

For various values of "source code".

I don't worry about the machine language used by my program's COMPUTE statement.
  That machine language is not "source code" for me.   It is for the people who
write the compiler.

I also don't worry about the source code in supplied Java libraries.

It is very rare that I look at either of these.

My source code is how I put my components together.

If your components are CoBOL commands, then your source code is in CoBOL.   If
your components are library objects, then your source code is whatever you use
to invoke those library objects.    For a CEO of a company, his "source code" is
the commands he gives to his upper management.

In each case, someone will inherit the work.   Maybe it will work hunky-dory
without being changed.   But the new CEO will need to understand what the
previous one did, and he will need the ability to make changes.   Whomever
inherits your system will need to understand what you did and will need the
ability to make changes.   Whomever inherits my programs will need to understand
what I did and make changes.  Whomever inherited the compiler that I used will
need to understand how the compiler works and make changes.   Whomever inherited
the CPU will need to understand how the CPU works and make changes.

A short track bicycle racer has a bicycle designed for one thing and it has one
gear.   But most bicycles are designed to be flexible, with multiple gears.   In
the old unstructured days, my bicycle didn't have stops for its derailleurs.  I
had to be more skilled in stopping the chains movement than I do with more
structured derailleurs of today.   The new derailleur is designed to be easier
to adjust to my changing needs.    But either case, the bicycle can have its
wheels replaced, its chains replaced, and its seat adjusted.   My new tires may
be of a completely different material than was available when the bicycle was
designed.    That doesn't mean that I need to understand the chemical makeup of
the tire - but I still need to be able to change the tire.   Ease of maintenance
is at whatever level the maintenance needs to be at.

Drag and drop some objects to create a system.    Now you either use the system
as created, or you go back to your GUI and make changes when business needs
change.   If your design methodology anticipated such maintenance, your job will
be easier than if it didn't.
```
