A few comments below.

"James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@shaw.ca> wrote in message news:<3EADE6CB.8ECFF964@shaw.ca>...
> "Peter E.C. Dashwood" wrote:
> 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> >

"The nice thing about banging your head against a wall is how good it
feels when you stop..."

To be fair, what I am discussing here is way beyond the normal COBOL
environment. There is such a HUGE amount of background required to
understand my position that it just doesn't make sense to debate it on
the grounds of "Source code maintenance" and "Waterfall development".
So I won't...<G>.

(I'll just note in passing that my correspondence with LX-i, where he
DID go and read the articles I wrote covering this, proved far more
productive than arguing with people's pre-conceived ideas about how
things MUST work, based on what they've done for the last 30 years...)

> > It IS possible for code to be bug free, PROVIDED it is simple and performs
> > clearly defined functions in a documented way. (In other words it does what
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Can't see that...there is nothing mystical about it; it is just a
different approach. I have explained how I arrived at this conclusion
fully in two articles that are a matter of public record. Anybody who
is mystified can simply go to the site and download. Daniel (LX-i)
did. At least he is now in an informed position to debate anything he
doesn't agree with or questions.
 
> So either I write an OO class or you  design a component - one or a combination of the
> following happens, in your case :-
> 
> 1. Pete Dashwood writes OO COBOL code

Sometimes. More often I let a tool (PowerCOBOL) generate it for me...
I can't be bothered with the verbosity of OO COBOL so I let PowerCOBOL
handle it. All I have to do is write the Methods and define the
Properties/events. On very rare occasions I use Fujitsu COBOL97 to
write a Class, in full OO COBOL, but most of the time I don't do this
any more.

> 2. POWER FORM generates code from your visual presentation design

Never used POWER FORM in my life and never needed to. Incorrect
assumption, Jimmy.

> 3. You may code some in Java

I have only coded a few Java Beans just to explore the actuality of
doing it. I don't normally mix Java and COBOL, unless there is a VERY
good reason to...

Of course, mixing Java Beans and ActiveX components to achieve certain
Application Functionality, would be fine. (You'd need to set up the
latest CORBA environment to run it though...<G>)

> 4. You may add in a third party component to your component
> 
NEVER! Components are entities and encapsulated. I WOULD mix third
party components written in God-knows-what with my own components
written in OO COBOL, as PART OF AN APPLICATION build process, but
never "combine" components. (You can't anyway; they are
encapsulated...)

One of my favourites is an application I wrote some years back when I
first started playing with components. It has third party components
written in VB and C++ which all work seamlessly with COBOL components
(that I wrote) on the same page. The user simply sees functionality.
THAT is the point about why source code is irrelevant. I will NEVER
maintain ANY of these components. If I needed to increase or change
the FUNCTIONALITY, I would either add or replace components; I would
NOT take these encapsulated components apart and "maintain" them. (I
couldn't even if I wanted to because (apart from the ones I wrote
myself) I don't have the source code (and I don't CARE about it!)).

The ONLY important thing here is the business FUNCTIONALITY!
(Incidentally, these same components get reused in other applications,
providing exactly the same functionality, but with ONE copy of the
component registered on my machine. (I believe the need for
registraion has been obviated in the .NET environment, but as I'm not
using it (yet) I don't know...))

> Like it or not there *is* friggin source code - it sure ain't called vapourware <G>.
> The only source that is encapsulated (hidden) to Pete Dashwood is # 4. Assuming you
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>

The source is irrelevant as discussed above. 

 
> Now as to the point do the methods in a component work without failing - try this one
> on them - take the source for just one method from your StringtoNum2 for a particular
> feature and show them it is 'proven'..

Why?

I don't care whether they believe me or not... If they download the
component from 'aboutlegacycoding' they can see it for themselves. The
article called "Components 101" was written because I had a number of
private e-mails for clarification. More than 12,000 people have read
it, several hundred have downloaded and tried the component (I have
had mail that several people are using it in their own applications
and delighted with it), the article explains exactly why components
are important, and yet people here want to debate the concepts and
even the details, based only on their own background and
understanding, without even bothering to read or download the
explanation I spent many hours preparing and posting. Are you
surprised I'm not responsive <G>?
> 
> In response to Charles' posting the Murtach source code, I listed two file classes,
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
> PECD likes Sections <G>)

Don't apologise for sections on my account <G>. I use them because it
makes sense to me to do so...I don't care what other people do...

. To my mind that approach can lead to bugs. Surely the whole
> of purpose of OO is to write short and sweet self-contained methods (paragraphs),

You had it right when you said "methods". Whether the "methods" are
paragraphs, sections, nested programs, or called modules, does not
matter as long as the criteria you mentioned apply.

> attempting as much as possible to avoid using global W/S with values 'localized' to a
> method via USING.... RETURNING.... And these small snippets of code, (methods), stand
> on their own merit.
> 

There IS a difference between OO Classes and components. It is subtle
but important.

You might well maintain an OO Class (add new
methods/properties/events) but you wouldn't do this with a component.
Once it is "wrapped" it represents encapsulated functionality that
performs in a documented way. That is WHY it doesn't need maintenance.
If you wanted to extend it you would simply replace it.

Pete.

"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message
news:b8onf0$ihq$1@peabody.colorado.edu...
>
> On 29-Apr-2003, dashwood@enternet.co.nz (Peter E. C. Dashwood) wrote:
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> Your "source code" is how you combine these components.  You do
maintenance on
> this "source code" when you add and replace components.

Only in YOUR mind, Howard.

I oversimplified the process in attempting to describe it to you and I
should not have done so. It is a different world.

It is a process unlike anything you are familiar with and that is why you
are desperately trying to make it into something you know and understand.

There is absolutely NO similarity between assembling components for a
solution and maintaining COBOL Source code.

The environment is different, the tools are different, the people doing it
are different, the process is infinitely different, the requirements to
carry out the process successfully are different, consequently, the skill
sets required are different. And the overall Methodology is different (RAD
vs the Waterfall).

The ONLY similarity is that at the end of it you have an application
processing computer system.

Pete.

As is any non-programming build.   I was generalizing from other construction

even business.  If it needs maintenance, it should be designed with this need in
mind.

> It is a process unlike anything you are familiar with and that is why you

Projects that will need to be maintained that I am familiar with, such as CoBOL
programming, Java programming, house building, family building, gardening,
country building, airplane building, and team building should be designed with
maintenance in mind.    OO programming at your level may be the singular
exception to this observation, or maybe there is some other exception I am

When the first code generators came out, they all seemed most lacking in the
ability to do maintenance.  Gradually they improved in this area, and as they
improved, they became more acceptable.

I have worked with generated code that almost did what I wanted.  I tweaked the
code and depending upon the generator, I could continue using the tool, or I was
done using the tool.

The former is useful, the latter was more trouble than it was worth.

```
"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote:


>Projects that will need to be maintained that I am familiar with, such as CoBOL
>programming, Java programming, house building, family building, gardening,
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>unaware of.

Airplane building is analogous to what Peter is talking about. When you build an
airplane, you don't design, build and maintain avionics at the board level. You
buy tested components and plug them in. Same with engines, radios, fuel tanks
and seats. If the component doesn't work right or needs to be changed, you
replace the whole component; you don't tinker with its internals.

Real aircraft engines are expensive, so they often are disassembled and rebuilt.
If they cost less than $100, like their software analogs, we wouldn't bother,
we'd discard and replace them. 

Designing a brand new airplane could be as simple as drawing an outline of the
fuselage (airframe) and wings, plugging in components, and test flying it on a
flight simulator. In fact, that's how airplanes ARE designed today. By the time
they get to real test flights, we already know how they are going to behave. If
there is a discrepency between actual and model, it is most likely an error in
the simulator. 

For instance, the SST acts differently depending on its center of gravity.
Expert pilots learned long ago, from expensive experience, how to balance the
load by moving fuel between tanks when transitioning from subsonic to
supersonic. Now that the dynamic is understood and incorporated into software,
we no longer need experts at the helm. The fuel transfer is automatic. The
upgrade was done by replacing software, which costs little, rather than
retraining pilots, which is expensive. 

In this analogy, you and I are the 'craftperson' pilot who's been superannuated.
We can embrace the change by learning OO, we can ignore it until we get
replaced, or we can find employment at second and third-tier providers, where
our role is maintaining 'the old stuff'. Rarely we find a fourth alternative:
new development. That's what I'm doing now and I love it.
```

On 28-Apr-2003, "Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote:

> It IS possible for code to be bug free, PROVIDED it is simple and performs
> clearly defined functions in a documented way. (In other words it does what
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> do what it was documented to do.)

I've had a CoBOL compiler that had a command that had a bug in it that I
reported.

When you get bigger and more complex systems, the components are like commands
in a simpler environment.   Windows has lots of bugs because components are
still a small part of the system.

The only way to be completely bug free is to be simple and isolated.   Once you
integrate lots of components, you have a complex "program" at a new level.

"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:3eabdf6c.242091596@news.optonline.net...
> riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote:

> >
> >Exactly the point: It is only your _opinion_ that it is 'bad', yet you

> We all agree that ALTER and PERFORM THRU are bad. What if someone posted

False assumption - please use GOOGLE (or equivalent) and review previous
discussions of PERFORM THRU.  There are *many* programmers who ACTIVELY
defend the "PERFORM ABC <section> THRU ABC-EXIT <section>" style of coding.
Until one has a compiler with "EXIT PARAGRAPH/SECTION" this type of style
does provide a USEFUL feature of an "immediate get out" of a procedure - and
as it still has a "single exit" for procedures, it meets the definition of
"structured programming" according to some - but not all - sources.

Personally, I cannot remember any discussions justifying the continued use
of the ALTER statement, but I am not certain that there aren't some shops
where this is still considered a "valid" (if not "good") programming style.

    ***

I will continue to recommend (as I have in the past) that programers NOT use
techniques that are identified as OBSOLETE in the '85 or 2002 Standard - or
those identified as ARCHAIC in the 2002 Standard, but I hesitate to call
them "BAD".

Well Bill, I will argue with you on this one. I have personally always found
that the PERFORM-THRU statement syntax was both clumsy and caused
considerable unnecessary and undesirable complexity within code.

As a caveat to that, outside of COBOL on a DPS7 and an ancient HP/3000, I
have not been in shops that mandated the use of PERFORM-THRU, and even then,
I strongly disagreed with it.

Note that I am not saying that straight-line or inline code is bad, just
that the PERFORM-THRU is a pretty horrible emulation of a subroutine call.
It comes pretty close to being 'bad' coding practice, though you and others
may disagree.

What was the thinking behind the implementation of PERFORM-THRU? I'm sure
there was a very good reason for it, I just cannot imagine what it might
have been. :)
-
Paul

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: "We all agree that" (was: Sets and subsets

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2003-04-27T15:28:35-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<b8hehr$p7n$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<b7tepn$9tb$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <3EA55E79.6090702@newsguy.com> <3ea5ed65.19257366@news.optonline.net> <3EA69C04.7090208@newsguy.com> <3ea88130.21326254@news.optonline.net> <b8ab71$1dv$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz> <3eaaa1e8.160795747@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0304261638.35e83817@posting.google.com> <3eabdf6c.242091596@news.optonline.net> <b8h9d7$v28$1@slb3.atl.mindspring.net> <UvWqa.38198$q02.1338663@twister.austin.rr.com>`

```
Sorry if I didn't make may post clear enough.

PERSONALLY, I much, MUCH prefer avoiding PERFORM ... THRU.  Furthermore,
with compiler supporting the new EXIT PARAGRAPH/SECTION statements, I can't
think of ANY valid reason to use it.  However, I do *not* see anything
"intrinsically" wrong with the structure WHEN the following rules are
"applied"

1) No other paragraph/section header appears between the ABC and ABC-EXIT
headers.

2) The *only* GO TO within the scope of such a range is to the ABC-EXIT

3) Such "structures" are limited to cases where there is a need to "escape"
from within a procedure's logic (due to an error condition or some other
"not all that common" condition)

    ***

I do, however, disagree with the claim that "all programmers AGREE that this
is a bad technique".  There certainly are many (probably? possibly?  even a
majority) programmers who agree with this - but it is FAR from universal.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: "We all agree that" (was: Sets and subsets

_(reply depth: 17)_

- **From:** robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner)
- **Date:** 2003-04-28T01:26:22+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3eac6d8e.278483072@news.optonline.net>`
- **References:** `<b7tepn$9tb$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <3EA55E79.6090702@newsguy.com> <3ea5ed65.19257366@news.optonline.net> <3EA69C04.7090208@newsguy.com> <3ea88130.21326254@news.optonline.net> <b8ab71$1dv$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz> <3eaaa1e8.160795747@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0304261638.35e83817@posting.google.com> <3eabdf6c.242091596@news.optonline.net> <b8h9d7$v28$1@slb3.atl.mindspring.net> <UvWqa.38198$q02.1338663@twister.austin.rr.com>`

```
"Paul Raulerson" <praulerson@hot.NOSPAM.rr.com> wrote:

>Note that I am not saying that straight-line or inline code is bad, just
>that the PERFORM-THRU is a pretty horrible emulation of a subroutine call.
>It comes pretty close to being 'bad' coding practice, though you and others
>may disagree.

I think all PERFORMs are bad. The longer I program, the fewer PERFORMs I use.
I'd rather CALL a nested or external program. My dislike for PERFORM is not lack
of parameters, it's lack of data encapsulation. You have an 'ocean of data' in
WORKING-STORAGE with no clear delineation of which data belongs to which
'function'. There is often data passed 'sideways' between functions. If that
data is an indicator containing a decision, it really screws up logic flow.
You're passing control, not data, 'under the table'.

I used to think it was a matter of granularity. I'd perform small functions and
call larger ones. Now I realize that was superficial. It's about DATA structure,
not procedural granularity. 

>What was the thinking behind the implementation of PERFORM-THRU? I'm sure
>there was a very good reason for it, I just cannot imagine what it might
>have been. :)

I'm speculating the Founding Fathers (and Commodore) thought we would write:

a. stuff
b. stuff
c. stuff

.. then sometimes PERFORM a THRU c, sometimes b THRU c and sometimes a THRU b.
What a silly idea. Long before the invention of structured programming, we had
the concept of a single entry and exit point.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: "We all agree that" (was: Sets and subsets

- **From:** "Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net>

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<b8jjiu$ehn$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`

PERFORM READ-ROUTINE UNTIL EOF-READ

- **From:** robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner)

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3eaef386.443876034@news.optonline.net>`
- **References:** `<b7tepn$9tb$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <3EA55E79.6090702@newsguy.com> <3ea5ed65.19257366@news.optonline.net> <3EA69C04.7090208@newsguy.com> <3ea88130.21326254@news.optonline.net> <b8ab71$1dv$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz> <3eaaa1e8.160795747@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0304261638.35e83817@posting.google.com> <3eabdf6c.242091596@news.optonline.net> <b8h9d7$v28$1@slb3.atl.mindspring.net> <UvWqa.38198$q02.1338663@twister.austin.rr.com> <3eac6d8e.278483072@news.optonline.net> <b8jjiu$ehn$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`

```
"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote:

>
>On 27-Apr-2003, robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote:
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>> of parameters, it's lack of data encapsulation. You have an 'ocean of data'
in
>> WORKING-STORAGE with no clear delineation of which data belongs to which
>> 'function'. There is often data passed 'sideways' between functions. If that
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>program logic?   If so, why should this be encapsulated?   It IS the program.

Does the system design say 'the function of program x is to read a file?' No, it
says 'the function of program x is to produce a report.' So the report logic
should be the 'driver' and reading a file should be at the bottom of the
hierarchy rather than the top. 

>Or in a typical report program - what should be encapsulated?   Read a file or
>database, check to see if they match some parms, and then report with some
>summary.   I can see an encapsulated parm editing call.   

I encapulate io on a permanent file (not necessarily flat output files such as
reports) in a callable program. This allows for multiple versions of the logical
view. When the file changes, you need not recompile every application program
that uses the file because the version number is a parameter. Old programs ask
for (or write) version 1, new programs specify version 2. The one-time
conversion program reads version 1 and writes version 2. 

I access database tables the same way, one io module per table. 

>Is that what you want
>encapsulated, or do you want the print routine encapsulated.   I have been in a
>shop where the print HAS been a call, and I hate it.   If that's what you like,
>we should share experiences here.   If your interests in encapsulating lie
>elsewhere, please explain.

I share your dislike for encapsulating reports. Producing a report involves a
lot a logic, not just low level io. I usually put the report in the top level
program. 

My report logic telescopes, from the highest level (final total) to the lowest
(detail line) like this: 

main. 
    perform one-report
    goback.
one-report.
    call 'read-next'
    initialize totals (1)
    perform one-department until end-of-file
    format and print final total.
one-department.
    initialize totals (2)
    move department to previous-department
    perform one-detail until end-of-file or 
        department not equal to previous-department
    add totals (2) to totals (1)
    format and print department total.
one-detail.
    initialize totals (3)
    move detail-data to totals (3)
    add totals (3) to totals (2)
    format and print detail record
    call 'read-next'.

Do you see how easy it would be to add a level in the middle? Note that the
report 'driver' is at the top and reading a record at the bottom. Do you see the
symmetry between levels?

A program written this way isn't 'driven' by the input file; it is driven by the
report and 'sucks data' from the file. Picture a middle manager writing a
report. He goes along for a while until he needs data, say a department total.
So he picks up the phone, calls a department manager and asks for last month's
sales. The department manager picks up his phone to call a worker and ask for a
list of invoices. The worker calls a file clerk to pull the invoices. He lists
them and returns the total to the middle manager, who then calls the next
department manager. At the end, the middle manager enters total sales on his
report and delivers it to top management. 

I'm pleased to see a substantive programming question posted to CLC, rather than
flammage and nit-picking over word definitions.
```

has the technician, Mr. bottom-up, doing design work. 

I once wrote:

"If you're
not familiar with the bicameral brain
concept it says that you have dual
processors inside your head.  The left
brain deals with things;  the right
brain with people.  The left brain
solves problems from the bottom up;  the
right brain from the top down.  The left
brain is a technician;  the right brain
a designer ("design engineer" is an
oxymoron).  The left brain wants
evolution; the right brain revolution.
In most people, one or the other is
dominant.  In a minority, they're
equal.

  My hypothesis is that really good
programmers are "balanced" while the
hordes of average programmers are
left-brain dominant (a right-brain person
can't make it in the computer world, (s)he
usually becomes a manager).  The left
brain is a bad designer.  For examples
look at most system software.
The tendancy is to take a simple problem
and make it complicated.  The right brain
is a bad technician.  It wants simple
answers to complex problems.  For evidence,
just talk with any salesman.  I believe the key to
success is ambivalence.  The word commonly
means indecisive but to me it means fast alternation
between left and right. Doing so requires
internal harmony. The left and right brains must
cooperate rather than compete for control."

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: "We all agree that" (was: Sets and subsets

_(reply depth: 19)_

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2003-04-29T14:04:09-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3EAEBE99.800@newsguy.com>`
- **References:** `<b7tepn$9tb$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <3EA55E79.6090702@newsguy.com> <3ea5ed65.19257366@news.optonline.net> <3EA69C04.7090208@newsguy.com> <3ea88130.21326254@news.optonline.net> <b8ab71$1dv$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz> <3eaaa1e8.160795747@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0304261638.35e83817@posting.google.com> <3eabdf6c.242091596@news.optonline.net> <b8h9d7$v28$1@slb3.atl.mindspring.net> <UvWqa.38198$q02.1338663@twister.austin.rr.com> <3eac6d8e.278483072@news.optonline.net> <b8jjp0$eiq$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`

```
Howard Brazee wrote:
 > On 27-Apr-2003, robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote:
 >
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
 > calls instead of performs?

It's a coupling issue.  If a given chunk of code is implemented as a
section and PERFORMed, all of the containing subprogram's data is in
scope.  If that chunk is moved to a separate subprogram and CALLed, the
only data from the calling subprogram it has access to (barring side
mechanisms like files) is the parameters that are explicitly passed.  So
a maintainer can say, "OK, I see that subprogram Foo will only
examine/modify these particular data items; if I'm not concerned about
those data items, I can ignore Foo for the moment".  With a PERFORMed
section you have no such guarantees.

Like all encapsulation mechanisms, moving code from section to
subprogram limits what the programmer can do, which increases what
assumptions the maintainer can safely make.  It trades effort for the
original programmer against effort for the maintainer.

My take on it, as with every programming technique, is that sometimes
it's justified and sometimes it isn't.  I continue to use sections and
PERFORM when I write COBOL, but I limit what data items they use and
employ techniques like unique data-item-name prefixes to make it clearer
what those are.  In other words, I manually reduce coupling (and I
document that for the maintainer).
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: "We all agree that" (was: Sets and subsets

_(reply depth: 19)_

- **From:** robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner)
- **Date:** 2003-04-30T01:06:20+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3eaf0012.447088304@news.optonline.net>`
- **References:** `<b7tepn$9tb$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <3EA55E79.6090702@newsguy.com> <3ea5ed65.19257366@news.optonline.net> <3EA69C04.7090208@newsguy.com> <3ea88130.21326254@news.optonline.net> <b8ab71$1dv$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz> <3eaaa1e8.160795747@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0304261638.35e83817@posting.google.com> <3eabdf6c.242091596@news.optonline.net> <b8h9d7$v28$1@slb3.atl.mindspring.net> <UvWqa.38198$q02.1338663@twister.austin.rr.com> <3eac6d8e.278483072@news.optonline.net> <b8jjp0$eiq$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`

```
"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote:

>
>On 27-Apr-2003, robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote:
>
>> My dislike for PERFORM is not lack
>> of parameters, it's lack of data encapsulation. You have an 'ocean of data'
in
>> WORKING-STORAGE with no clear delineation of which data belongs to which
>> 'function'. There is often data passed 'sideways' between functions. If that
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>I'm not sure what this means.  

I'll elucidate by example. These two examples equivalent except one passes
control explicitly whereas the other passes it under the table:

if  (condition)
    do other stuff
    perform foo
end-if

if  (condition)
    do other stuff
    set indicator to true
end-if
[later]
if  indicator
    perform foo
end-if

In both cases, the if is invoking foo. In the first case, it's an explicit
transfer of control; in the second, the transfer is disguised to look like data.

> Are you objecting to using the same index for multiple performs?  

No.

> What is your definition of a function and of "sideways"? 

A function is any out of line code that can be explicitly performed, called or
invoked. Technically, it can be inline as well.

Sideways means disguising (deferring) the explicit call by temporarily storing
it as data. It's really not data, it's a logical state. 

>How does logic flow differ using calls instead of performs?   

It doesn't. They are equivalent in re logic flow. Calls offer advantages in data
encapsulation. 

>If your call
>changes an indicator, or your perform changes an indicator, what's the
>difference?

Again, controlled access to data. With calls, the indicator must be publically
(formally) passed to both programs. With performs, the binding between indicator
and function is vague. Every indicator is fair game to every performable
procedure, including those not on the same structural level. A file clerk could
look at and change the CEO's agenda, and vice versa. Logic bugs feed on this
sort of chaos.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: "We all agree that" (was: Sets and subsets

_(reply depth: 20)_

- **From:** riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard)
- **Date:** 2003-04-30T13:07:45-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<217e491a.0304301207.1201c0c9@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<b7tepn$9tb$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <3EA69C04.7090208@newsguy.com> <3ea88130.21326254@news.optonline.net> <b8ab71$1dv$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz> <3eaaa1e8.160795747@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0304261638.35e83817@posting.google.com> <3eabdf6c.242091596@news.optonline.net> <b8h9d7$v28$1@slb3.atl.mindspring.net> <UvWqa.38198$q02.1338663@twister.austin.rr.com> <3eac6d8e.278483072@news.optonline.net> <b8jjp0$eiq$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <3eaf0012.447088304@news.optonline.net>`

```
robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote 

> A function is any out of line code that can be explicitly performed, called or
> invoked. Technically, it can be inline as well.

In another thread you criticised [all] programmers for being vauge in
using the term subroutine.

Now references that I have would have the term subroutine roughly as
'any out of line code ... inline as well', while the term function has
a specific definition:

"The expression in mathematical symbols of the relationship between
variables. .."

The term function is also clearly defined in maths as well and it is
from there that programming takes the meaning.

As an example of a function we can take sin().  It would be possible
to express this as an expression whenever we wanted to calculate this
within a larger expression. By embodying the sine expression in a
callable subroutine that returns the value of the expression we can
replace the complete expression with a reference to the function.

Previously you had called 'nested programs' as 'user functions' when
they are not functions.

In Cobol the intrinsic functions are the only functions that can be
called as such until user functions become available.

It would be easier if you were to align your terminology to common
usage rather than the rest of the world having to rewrite all the
dictionaries and text books and change the programming language.
 
> Sideways means disguising (deferring) the explicit call by temporarily storing
> it as data. It's really not data, it's a logical state. 

It is often that several tests have to be made in sequence to
determine complex states.  For example verifying a series of fields. 
Rathen that storing the state in the deep nesting of the checks it is
preferable to store the state in a data item.

This isn't disguising or deferring, it is establishing the state over
a series of blocks of code.
```

```
robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote 

> >As an example of a function we can take sin().  It would be possible
> >to express this as an expression whenever we wanted to calculate this
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> Your argument about the word function is nearly identical to mine about integer.

No. That is not true. Your 'definition' of integer is _not_ based on
maths, you have mistakenly supported some nonsense about having to add
1 by using a definition of something completely different.

The _actual_ definition of integer in maths, common usage, and as used
in programming is entirely consistent.  It is only your own unique
'definition' that is discordant.

I know that you have an emotional attachment to your creation because
it is the foundation for a very silly argument about the Cobol
standard.

> You want to use the math definition. After you rejected my argument, why do you
> now expect me to accept yours? 

Yes, I know it seems rather ambitious to expect you to accept anything
you haven't made up yourself.

But you may note that I said the programming takes its meaning from
maths, not that it used the maths definition.

> You will point to the COBOL standard's definition of function. Ok, we'll use
> that when discussing COBOL. But my usage is consistant with common usage in
> other languages. 

No,. It is only 'consistent' with your understanding, which is a
different thing.

> A C function returning void is form over substance. 

A void function _doesn't_ return anything, you misunderstand void in
that context.

> It is not a
> statement about the relationship between variables. 

No, and nor is it a 'function' in any meaningful way.  It is simply
that C makes no formal distinction at all between 'function' and
'procedure'.  This does not mean that there is no distinction.

> Further, the receiving field need not even be coded. 

That is correct the C compiler does not care if you discard values. 
This is the result of C not having an assignment statement.

> Permitting a programming function to modify its
> arguments is inconsistent with the math definition as well. 

The const qualifier can be used to ensure that a parameter is not
modified.  Programming correctly relies on the programmer.

> It is common usage in other programming languages. 

Which 'other programming languages' were you imaginging here ?

Pascal makes a firm distinction between FUNCTION and PROCEDURE, in
exactly the way I describe.

BASIC has its DEF FN and GOSUB.

FORTRAN has FUNCTION and SUBROUTINE 

COBOL has FUNCTION (as in Intrinsic function) and PROGRAM (as in
PROGRAM-ID .. END-PROGRAM).

You used the term FUNCTION to be 'any out of line code that can be
performed called or invoked .. or even in-line'.  This is _not_ how
the term is used in _any_ programming language, especially Cobol.

The function may be 'invoked' in an expression. 

In C a statement of the form 'function_name(..);' _is_ an expression.

You criticised 'programmers' for vague use of subroutine but it is
_this_ term that you wrongly substitute 'function' for.

> Yes, we're trying to package a three-dimensional problem into two-dimensional
> code (written in a one-dimensional language). One solution is to make the blocks
> of code out of line functions:
> 
> IF  FUNCTION a() AND FUNCTION b() AND FUNCTION c()

Which can't be done in any current implementation of Cobol that I know
of, except with intrinsic functions of course.

At least you have used the term function correctly here and invoked
them.

The objection was that your claim included:

   "performed"   - which makes the code a procedure, not a function
   "called"      - which makes the code a subprogram, not a function
   "invoked"     - which may be a method
   "inline"      - which makes it, well, code, and not a function

The generic term is 'subroutine', the one you didn't like.
```

subsets") and it is true in "normal" usage.

of your ideas that DO have value.

numbers or natural numbers'?  Or 'The integers, or whole numbers, consist 
of the natural numbers (0, 1, 2, ...) and the negative whole numbers (-1,

(20365) or twenty million, thirty thousand, three hundred twenty-nine
(20030329).  Looks like those dates *are* integers... didn't someone here

Robert Wagner <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:3eb30c24.25102579@news.optonline.net...
> riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote:
>
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> You're full of it. It isn't _my_ definition, it is based on set theory,
and it
> isn't nonsense.

The word integer came into usage in 1571.
< http://www.m-w.com/cgi-bin/dictionary >

Set theory was founded about 300 years later by Cantor.

Cantor, Georg (Ferdinand Ludwig Philipp)
born March 3, 1845 , St. Petersburg, Russia
died Jan. 6, 1918 , Halle, Ger.

German mathematician who founded set theory and introduced
the mathematically meaningful concept of transfinite numbers,
indefinitely large but distinct from one another.

"Cantor, Georg" Encyclop�dia Britannica  from Encyclop�dia
Britannica Premium Service.
<http://www.britannica.com/eb/article?eu=20386>
[Accessed May 3, 2003].

Set theory relies on the definition of integer that existed prior
to its founding.

To claim that set theory defines integer to mean something
other than that upon which set theory relies is absurd.


[snip]
> >> You will point to the COBOL standard's definition of function. Ok,
we'll use
> >> that when discussing COBOL. But my usage is consistant with common
usage in
> >> other languages.
> >
…[20 more quoted lines elided]…
> Huh? The vast majority of C statements are assignment.

C has an 'assignment expression' and an 'expression statement'.

An assignment expression may appear where an expression
may appear. An expression statement may consist of an
expression that is not an assignment expression.

Where an assignment expression appears within an expression
statement, this may appear to be an assignment statement as
it is understood in other languages; but C has no assignment
statement.

> >> Permitting a programming function to modify its
> >> arguments is inconsistent with the math definition as well.
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> COBOL has no such protection except BY VALUE. I cannot imagine pushing a
1M
> table onto the stack.

COBOL also has BY CONTENT.

> >Pascal makes a firm distinction between FUNCTION and PROCEDURE, in
> >exactly the way I describe.
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
> 'Subroutine' is a term from the '60s. 'Function' is from the '80s. What's
the
> modern equivalent?

You may be interested in the definition of procedure from:
< http://www.ncits.org/tc_home/k5htm/p3.htm#procedure >.

"A subprogram that does not return a data value, except as
part of the parameter mechanism. In COBOL, a procedure
is a paragraph, or a group of logically successive paragraphs,
or a section (consisting of zero or more paragraphs) within
the procedure division. In some programming languages
(i.e., C and C++), the procedure language construct is not
differentiated from the function language construct except that
returned data values may be void or not used. Synonymous
with subroutine."
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: "We all agree that" (was: Sets and subsets

- **From:** Richard Plinston <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2003-05-04T11:12:25+12:00

- **Message-ID:** `<b91ief$a99$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz>`

```
Robert Wagner <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message

> >This is the result of C not having an assignment statement.
>
> Huh? The vast majority of C statements are assignment.

In C there is _no_ assignment statement.  Assignment is an operator which 
may, or may not, be used in an expression, or may be used several times 
just like any other operator.

> >FORTRAN has FUNCTION and SUBROUTINE

> 'Subroutine' is a term from the '60s. 'Function' is from the '80s. What's
> the modern equivalent?

FORTRAN, from the 50s has both FUNCTION and SUBROUTINE.  Your assertions 
about dates is quite wrong.  This also confirms that they are different 
enough to use distinct keywords for, and disproves your 'as found in other 
languages'.

In fact the usage in FORTRAN follows common usage that existed when the 
language was developed.

Now it may well be that you never heard the term 'function' prior to the 
80s and then made up the idea that it was a 'new' term for subroutine. You 
probably feel 'modern' in using it, but you use it wrongly.
```

The following quotes are from:
Morris Kline, "MATHEMATICS, HISTORY OF"
Copyright � 1996 P. F. Collier, L. P.  All rights reserved.

The first "systematic, clear, and comprehensive treatment
of the integer and ratios of integers" was "'Introductio Arithmetica'
of Nichomachus (c. A.D. 100)."

"The earliest known use of negative numbers was by
Brahmagupta around A.D. 630."

"Mahavira (A.D. 850) stated rules for operating with zero ...."

"The legitimacy of negative numbers was still disputed in the
16th century."

Aristotle (c. 384 - 322 B.C.) contributed to the concept of infinity
and Euclid (about 300 B.C.) used infinity.

Thus, by the time the word 'integer' came into usage in 1571,
all that was required to understand "the set of all integers"
was understood.

Number theory is the branch of mathematics concerned with
the integers, or whole numbers, and generalizations of the
integers. [Encyclop�dia Britannica]

Number theory appears to have preceeded set theory by
about 100 years.

> >You may be interested in the definition of procedure from:
> >< http://www.ncits.org/tc_home/k5htm/p3.htm#procedure >.
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> Mainframers will be delighted to hear someone supports them. Who is the
author
> and what are his bona fides?

The information is supplied at
< http://www.ncits.org/tc_home/k5htm/Foreword.htm >
which states, in part,

"Under the direction of the American National Standards
Institute (ANSI) � National Committee for Information
Technology Standards (NCITS), the Technical Committee
on Vocabulary, K5, prepared this American National
Standard Dictionary of Information Technology (ANSDIT).
The dictionary was developed by studying the use of terms
throughout the field of information technology, including
computers, data communications, data processing, text
processing, artificial intelligence, virtual reality and related
fields. The ANSDIT has been harmonized with ISO/IEC-2382,
Information Technology � Vocabulary (ITV), for which
Technical Committee NCITS/K5 served as the technical
advisory group for the American National Standards Institute."

This is followed by a list of the members of K5.

```

Robert Wagner <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:3eb5864e.85282383@news.optonline.net...
> "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net> wrote:
>
…[45 more quoted lines elided]…
> According to this document, a subroutine in COBOL is a paragraph, not a
called
> program.

A procedure (aka, subroutine) may be a single paragraph;
but not always. The COBOL standard definition obviously
'influenced' the ANSDIT definition of 'procedure'; though
the ANSDIT description is not accurate.

ISO/IEC 1989:2002, FDIS, page 381, 14.3 Procedures,
"A procedure is composed of a paragraph, or a group of
successive paragraphs, or a section, or a group of
successive sections within the procedure division."

Maybe J4 should send a note to K5! <g>
```

- **From:** Peter Lacey <lacey@mb.sympatico.ca>
- **Date:** 2003-04-27T23:19:52-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3EACABE7.74B02A94@mb.sympatico.ca>`
- **References:** `<b7tepn$9tb$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <3EA55E79.6090702@newsguy.com> <3ea5ed65.19257366@news.optonline.net> <3EA69C04.7090208@newsguy.com> <3ea88130.21326254@news.optonline.net> <b8ab71$1dv$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz> <3eaaa1e8.160795747@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0304261638.35e83817@posting.google.com> <3eabdf6c.242091596@news.optonline.net> <b8h9d7$v28$1@slb3.atl.mindspring.net> <UvWqa.38198$q02.1338663@twister.austin.rr.com>`

```
Paul Raulerson wrote:

> Well Bill, I will argue with you on this one. I have personally always found
> that the PERFORM-THRU statement syntax was both clumsy and caused
> considerable unnecessary and undesirable complexity within code.
>

Well, Paul, I will argue with you on this one.  I have personally never found
that the PERFORM-THRU statement syntax was clumsy (especially compared to such
wonders as the EVALUATE statement); nor have I found that if it was used
properly (stress properly) did it cause any noticeable complexity.
"Unnecessary" is a function of the application.

I will add that after following this newsgroup for quite a few years I can't
think of many verbs that somebody or other hasn't objected to.  Even such
innocent things as COMPUTE have their detractors.  Equally so, even the most
obscure and complex constructions have their enthusiastic proponents.  I have
always held that if a program is properly planned (and the planning published in
some form) it doesn't matter what style is used to implement it (by a competent
programmer, of course): the result will be easy to understand, easy to maintain,
and will probably be sufficiently efficient.

PL
```

```
"William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com> wrote:

>"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
>news:3eabdf6c.242091596@news.optonline.net...
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
>"structured programming" according to some - but not all - sources.

I thought that's what SECTIONs are for. Rather than PERFORM ABC THRU ABC-EXIT,
make ABC a SECTION and PERFORM ABC. I don't advocate either, but if one wants to
program that way, SECTION seems like the logical feature to use. 

Before we got EXIT PARAGRAPH, COBOL provided NEXT SENTENCE for hasty exit. Of
course that means no periods except at the end. 

What flavor of structured programming permits, much less encourages, GO TO?

I haven't seen anyone defend PERFORM .. THRU in the last few months. When the
subject came up, posters agreed it should not be used. Yukonmama pointed out it
is required for CICS HANDLE CONDITION, but advocated inline error handling.

>Personally, I cannot remember any discussions justifying the continued use
>of the ALTER statement, but I am not certain that there aren't some shops
>where this is still considered a "valid" (if not "good") programming style.

Nah, they use GO TO DEPENDING ON or IF to build their 'state machine'.

I don't see much difference between ALTER and PROCEDURE-POINTER. In most
implementations, they're the same. ALTER doesn't change code. On many modern
machines, that would cause a protection exception abend. ALTER changes an
invisible pointer in the data area. 

>I will continue to recommend (as I have in the past) that programers NOT use
>techniques that are identified as OBSOLETE in the '85 or 2002 Standard - or
>those identified as ARCHAIC in the 2002 Standard, but I hesitate to call
>them "BAD".

The only ones that I've seen in common use are debugging lines and NEXT
SENTENCE. I don't advocate or use either, but debugging lines, especially, are
all over the place in some mainframe shops. They are used like TRACE, to display
paragraph names. Apparently, there was once a program which inserted them.

It is unfortunate one must change source code to turn them on, because that
requires checking the program out from the CM system. It would be nice if a
compiler option turned them on.
```

```

"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:3eac51ed.271408820@news.optonline.net...
> "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com> wrote:

> I don't see much difference between ALTER and PROCEDURE-POINTER. In most
> implementations, they're the same. ALTER doesn't change code. On many
modern
> machines, that would cause a protection exception abend. ALTER changes an
> invisible pointer in the data area.
>
Oh Really? And just how exactly does changing a "hidden pointer" in the DATA
area,  cause a change of behaviour in the PROCEDURE area?

Having examined the generated code from ALTER on IBM mainframes I can assure
you that is NOT the case.

Although my Intel Assembler is somewhat rusty, I wouldn't mind betting that
code generated by a COBOL ALTER replaces the operand of an unconditional
Branch instruction in a PROCEDURE segment...

A protection exception would only apply to storage OUTSIDE the loaded
program. Programs have been able to modify their own code since the dawn of
computing. I once wrote a program that used ONLY "AND" "OR" "XOR" and
"NEGATE" instructions. It read paper tape, processed the input, wrote it to
a magnetic tape, and produced a report, much to everyone's amazement. It did
this by modifying bit strings to become machine instructions. No protection
was violated. I did it for a bet and to show that at the machine language
level the only thing that matters is binary. (The program was written in
PLAN for an ICL 1900).

What makes you think otherwise?

Pete.

```
"Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>
>"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>area,  cause a change of behaviour in the PROCEDURE area?

The GO TO loads the pointer into a register and branches on it, or uses
'indirect addressing' if the machine offers that.

>Although my Intel Assembler is somewhat rusty, I wouldn't mind betting that
>code generated by a COBOL ALTER replaces the operand of an unconditional
>Branch instruction in a PROCEDURE segment...

Using Intel as an example of "modern" machines, every memory segment has three
protection permissions: read, write and execute. Code segments are supposed to
be execute only. If a program tries to read code, it probably has an errant data
pointer. Data segments are usually read/write, although they can be read only.
If a program tries to execute data, it has an errant procedure pointer. 

>A protection exception would only apply to storage OUTSIDE the loaded
>program. Programs have been able to modify their own code since the dawn of
>computing. 

They haven't been able to that since the 286 ca. 1988. At least not applications
running in privilege level 3. Programs which can must be in a higher
(numerically lower) level. For instance, those device drivers which keep
crashing Windows and giving Microsoft an unjustified black eye.

>I once wrote a program that used ONLY "AND" "OR" "XOR" and
>"NEGATE" instructions. It read paper tape, processed the input, wrote it to
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>PLAN for an ICL 1900).

You wrote that in the Good Old Days. Nowadays you'd have to produce an
executable, then get the operating system to run it. That's not easy, either, on
file systems that have real security. 

>What makes you think otherwise?

My experience writing an operating system, which dealt with memory management.
```

"Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote 

> "Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote 

> > Using Intel as an example of "modern" machines, 

'Intel' is a brand that covers a variety of different CPUs.  For
example it may have been used to mean 8085 or 8086 which have _no_
protection mechanisms.

Alternately even a more advanced CPU may be run in 8086 mode or even
using Virtual 8086 in which case there is no protection mechanism for
the program running there.
```

- **References:** `<b7tepn$9tb$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <3EA55E79.6090702@newsguy.com> <3ea5ed65.19257366@news.optonline.net> <3EA69C04.7090208@newsguy.com> <3ea88130.21326254@news.optonline.net> <b8ab71$1dv$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz> <3eaaa1e8.160795747@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0304261638.35e83817@posting.google.com> <3eabdf6c.242091596@news.optonline.net> <b8h9d7$v28$1@slb3.atl.mindspring.net> <3eac51ed.271408820@news.optonline.net> <3eaceb09_2@127.0.0.1>`

```
"Peter E.C. Dashwood" wrote:
> 
> "Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
> you that is NOT the case.
This was true on COBOL D for DOS 360 Release 26 where the ALTER modified
a branch instruction (B PARA or hex 47F0xxxx) but later versions would
have the ALTER replacing an ADCON for a load and branch (L R15,ADDRPARA
followed by BR R15  hex 58F0xxxx07FF).  Thus for re-entrant programs it
would be modifying something in a working area that had been initialized
with the original branching address constants.  I say this as someone
who has read generated code for all of the IBM COBOL compilers he worked
with.  It was quite disconcerting to work with Microfocus COBOL on HP-UX
and not have the foggiest notion of either the generated code or the
instruction set.  
> 
> Although my Intel Assembler is somewhat rusty, I wouldn't mind betting that
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
> ---= 19 East/West-Coast Specialized Servers - Total Privacy via Encryption =---
```

Not me. I used ALTER as a first time switch in programs for years (during
the 60s). Nobody ever had any problem with it and many people maintained my
code (as I did theirs). I was sorry when the bleeding hearts got it dropped
from the language <G>.

It was efficient (at a time when that really mattered) and, provided it was
used carefully and documented properly, anybody of average intelligence
could see what it did.

I used GO TO ...DEPENDING ON and the world didn't end either...<G>

It was the computer scientists who came along and persuaded everybody these
things are bad...In MY day we cut real COBOL code and didn't need all this
namby-pamby "eliminate GO TO" stuff....harrrummpfff!

programmer. That's what I do...<G> If the sytle prevented me from
maintaining it, I shouldn't call myself a programmer. It is (only) COBOL.

robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote

> Even in the 60's, before CS, my gut instinct told me ALTER was wrong. So too was
> self-modifying assembly language, which we did a lot of in those Good Old Days. 

So why did you do a lot of it if you thought it was wrong ?

One reason why self-modifying code was used, both in assembler and
Cobol, was that the machines of the time didn't have a stack.  On
machines that I worked on a PERFORM set the return address into the
end of the perform range where an EXIT had conceptually reserved space
for it.  It wasn't wrong, it was the way the machine worked.

>  not because I handed it down engraved on tablets. 

Yet you have tried "handing it down engraved in 'best practices'"
here, so just how do you square your assertion that you do know how to
do it ?

```
riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote:

>robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote
>
>> Even in the 60's, before CS, my gut instinct told me ALTER was wrong. So too
was
>> self-modifying assembly language, which we did a lot of in those Good Old
Days. 
>
>So why did you do a lot of it if you thought it was wrong ?

I meant we did a not of assembly language. It was not self-modifying. 

>One reason why self-modifying code was used, both in assembler and
>Cobol, was that the machines of the time didn't have a stack.  On
>machines that I worked on a PERFORM set the return address into the
>end of the perform range where an EXIT had conceptually reserved space
>for it.  It wasn't wrong, it was the way the machine worked.

That wasn't true on IBM mainframes. They did BALR 14,15 to call, RET 14 to exit.
If the called program or paragraph was more than trivial, it stored the return
address in 14 into a data area. Later we stored all registers (STM) into a data
area provided by the caller, thus making the called program 'reusable'. 

>>  not because I handed it down engraved on tablets. 
>
>Yet you have tried "handing it down engraved in 'best practices'"
>here, so just how do you square your assertion that you do know how to
>do it ?

Then it was in-person and collaborative; here it is at-a-distance on a debate
forum.
```

"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:3eade498.374507313@news.optonline.net...
> "Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote:
>
<snip>
> >But we pointed out that we COULD do arithmetic on date integers.
>
> Yeah, right. As in ADD 1 TO date-integer? You can do that in SQL (which
uses
> Julian dates), but not in COBOL.

This is why people get so annoyed with you.  You just don't read (or you
can't understand) the replies.

In COBOL it is TOTALLY valid syntax to add 1 to a date-integer.  You may or
may not get a valid date back, but that doesn't mean that the sending field
isn't an integer and that the resulting field is not an integer. It only
means that the resulting integer may not be a valid date.

Some (relatively obscure - but still "valid") examples were even provided to
you showing how the ability to add 1 to a date-integer  (Such as the
returned value  from DATE-OF-INTEGER, much less INTEGER-OF-DATE) would
provide valid COBOL logic.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: RW repeats one of his long-outstanding errrors (was: Sets and subsets

_(reply depth: 18)_

- **From:** Richard Plinston <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2003-04-29T18:30:34+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<b8l6a5$ql6$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz>`
- **References:** `<b7tepn$9tb$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <b8jidp$e15$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <3eade498.374507313@news.optonline.net> <b8l14q$2ig$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net>`

```
William M. Klein wrote:

> In COBOL it is TOTALLY valid syntax to add 1 to a date-integer.  

Coincidentally, I have just had a request to provide a new web page for one 
of my systems which is to display a page of control data for the statistics 
of a complete month.

Taking the input from a URL I take the specified year and month number to 
give an integer of the form yyyymm01 (eg: 20030401) in Date-Request PIC 
9(8) and calculate the number of days in that month.  The code then is 
approximately:

   PERFORM
       Number-Of-Days TIMES

       MOVE Date-Request       TO Date-Formatted    *>  PIC 9999/99/99
                                                    *> eg 2003/04/12

       EXEC SQL DECLARE control CURSOR FOR
                    SELECT * FROM controls 
                       WHERE day = :Date-Formatted END-EXEC
       EXEC SQL OPEN control ...         
       PERFORM UNTIL ...
          EXEC SQL FETCH control INTO ...
          *> set up control item totals
       END-PERFORM

       MOVE Date-Request     TO Day-Number     *> PIC 99   gives day only
       ....
       *> output table line
        

       ADD 1             TO Date-Request
    END-PERFORM

It works wonderfully, thank you.
```

     COMPUTE TOMORROW = THIS-DATE + 1.
READ-LOOP.
     IF DATA-DATE < THIS-DATE PERFORM STILL-VALID END-IF

Sure we could do <= , but that doesn't make the above code invalid or not
meaningful.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Sets and subsets

_(reply depth: 17)_

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com
- **Date:** 2003-04-29T11:09:31-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<b8m4jb$b5s$1@panix1.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<b7tepn$9tb$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <3eabdf6c.242091596@news.optonline.net> <b8jidp$e15$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <3eade498.374507313@news.optonline.net>`

```
In article <3eade498.374507313@news.optonline.net>,
Robert Wagner <robert@wagner.net> wrote:
>"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote:

[snip]

>I'm thinking there's something wrong with this medium. It's designed for people
>with a short attention span. Correct me if I'm wrong. 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>Julian dates), but not in COBOL. 

Mr Wagner, this is tedious.  Please read the following through, in its 
entirety, before you respond.

Consider the following example using what have been called 'IBM Julian
dates':

05  JULDATE      PIC 9(5) VALUE ZEROES.
05  REDEF REDEFINES JULDATE.
    10  JUL-YY   PIC 9(2) VALUE ZEROES.
    10  JUL-DDD  PIC 9(3) VALUE ZEROES.
...
MOVE 20365 TO JULDATE.
ADD 1 TO JULDATE.

In that 20365 is a whole number, without a decimal or fractional 
component, it is (according to http://www.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integer ) an 
integer.  Given the format of an 'IBM Julian date' it is an integer which 
represents 30 Dec 2000.

Adding 1 (another integer) to 20365 gives 20366, yet another integer.  
Given the format of an 'IBM Julian date' it is an integer which represents 
31 Dec 2000.

Conclusion: an arithmetic operation had been performed, in COBOL, on an 
integer representation of a valid date which yielded another integer 
representation of a valid date.

Now if the ADD is repeated then JULDATE contains 20367.  20367 is *not*, 
given the format of an 'IBM Julian date', an integer which represents a 
valid date.

Conclusion: while arithmetic operations which are performed on 'IBM Julian
date' integer representation of a valid date *can* result in another 'IBM
Julian date' integer representation of a valid date not *all* arithmetic
operations which are performed on 'IBM Julian date' integer
representations of valid dates result in another 'IBM Julian date' integer
representation of a valid date.

Is that clear, Mr Wagner?  Some do, some don't.  If some do then it can be 
done; if it 'can be done' then to say 'it cannot be done' - which has been 
your assertion - is contrary to fact.

Likewise, consider the integer representation of 29 Apr 2003 in YYYYMMDD 
format: 20030429.

Adding 1 to this gives 20030430, an integer representation of 30 Apr 2003 
in YYYYMMDD format.

Again it is demonstrated that one can add 1 to an integer representation 
of a valid date and the result will be an integer representation of a 
valid date.

Adding 1 to 20030430 gives 20030431; this is not an integer representation 
of a valid date.  As in the example above, one instance of addition 
generates an integer representation of a valid date, one does not.  Again, 
sometimes one can generate an integer representation of a valid date by 
adding 1 to an integer representation of a valid, sometimes one cannot.

If 'sometimes one can' is true then to say without qualification (as has 
been your assertion) that 'one cannot' is false.

Now that you have finished reading this before assaying your reply, Mr 
Wagner, please be so kind as to, without emotion, hyperbole, metaphor or 
other rhetorical devices, point out the errors in the definitions used or 
the logical steps taken which invalidate the conclusion.

DD
```

Well, ....

       01  My-Number       PIC S9(6).

   MOVE ZERO       TO My-Number
       PERFORM 1000000 TIMES
           ADD 1       TO My-Number
                On Size Error
                      Perform Too-Big
           End-Add
       END-PERFORM

 Well, what do you know, ZERO times in a million I get a wrong answer.

    ***

Similarly - in the 2002 Standard

     01  Integer-YYYYMMDD       PIC 9(8).

   MOVE NumVal  (Function Current-Date (1:8)) TO Integer-YYYYMMDD
   ADD 1       TO Integer-YYYYMMDD
   If Function TEST-DATE-YYYYMMDD (Integer-YYYYMMDD) =  0
             Perform Add-1-valid-date
   Else
            Perform ADD-1-Invalid-Date
   End-IF
```

```
I agree.

But Bill may have added an unnecessary complication in his second example.
The results of CURRENT-DATE are well defined, as are the results of a MOVE
from an eight-character alphanumeric elementary item to an eight-character
numeric elementary item ("when the sending operand is described as being
alphanumeric, data is moved as if the sending operand were described as an
unsigned numeric integer.")  Since we know CURRENT-DATE(1:8} contains
nothing but digits in USAGE DISPLAY form, the NUMVAL is not needed.

Thus, I think the original extraction of the current date via
    MOVE NUMVAL (Function CURRENT-DATE (1:8)) TO Integer-YYYYMMDD
could reasonably be simplified to
    MOVE CURRENT-DATE (1:8) TO Integer-YYYYMMDD
without any loss of reliability or accuracy.

I believe the FUNCTION keyword is optional for intrinsic functions and for
any user-defined functions declared in the REPOSITORY paragraph in COBOL
2002.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

Let's say I wrote a book on good practices.   You read it and come across
statements like:

Don't use the ALTER verb.
Don't use spaghetti code.
Check file status of I/O.
Use lots of periods.
Use meaningful names.

Which of those statements would draw a response from you?   Certainly some of
those statements are satisfactory to you as they are.  What more is there to
say?   If your boss got my book and wanted to add them to your shop standards,
you won't change your code.

But at least one of those statements is something you don't agree with.   Either
you don't think it should be part of a standard (my argument), or you think it
can actually be harmful.

So what do you talk about on this newsgroup?   Everything that you agree with?
Or whatever you disagree with?

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Style (was: Sets and subsets

_(reply depth: 13)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2003-04-27T13:51:46-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<b8h8s8$c6r$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<b7tepn$9tb$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <3EA55E79.6090702@newsguy.com> <3ea5ed65.19257366@news.optonline.net> <3EA69C04.7090208@newsguy.com> <3ea88130.21326254@news.optonline.net> <b8ab71$1dv$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz> <3eaaa1e8.160795747@news.optonline.net>`

```

"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:3eaaa1e8.160795747@news.optonline.net...
> Richard Plinston <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:
>
> >Robert Wagner wrote:
<snip>
>
> The issue is rhetorical style rather than claiming to be an authority.
When
> people are criticized here for bad programming practice, their defense is
'a
> matter of style'. Most posters seem to regard that as valid rebuttal. If
it's
> valid for programming, it should be valid for English as well.

The claim
    "If it's valid for programming, it should be valid for English as well."

seems totally wrong to me.

I won't get into the (already progressed) arguments on what the purpose of a
programming language or a specific piece of programming code is, but I
simply can't accept that argements that apply to an ARTIFICIAL language
(such as a programming language) should (much less do) apply to "natural
languages".

As far as the statement,
     "When people are criticized here for bad programming practice, their
defense is 'a  matter of style'.

this seems to be based on the asumption that there is such a thing as
universally accepted "bad programming practice".  Just to give a few
examples of why I don't agree with this (in most cases), one must consider
issues such as:

 - Is the source code developed within a "shop" where multiple or different
programmes may be expected to maintain or enhance the code - or is it
developed by a "stand-alone" programmer who NEVER distributes applications
in source code format?
 - If the code is developed in a "large shop" environment, what is the style
to which most other programmers are accustomed (and which "communicates"
best to them and with which they would have the greatest ease in
maintaining)? Are there "shop standards"?
 - What are the "operating system" impacts on the code? (e.g. are there
external SORT's that are commonly used?  Are you dealing with
performance-sensitive applications where a "nightly batch run" must be
completed within a certain time frame or with an online application where
response time is critical?)
 - What tools are "common" to those working with the COBOL source code (e.g.
Do they use Report-Writer? Are Assembler subprograms common?  Are there O/S
API's in common usage?)
 - Is the source code expected to be ported to other compilers or
environments?  How about the "output" data?

All of the above (and many others)  MAY impact whether a specific COBOL
"coding style" is APPROPRIATE (not "good or bad") to a specific application
(in my opinion).  Anyone claiming that a specific "functionally correct"
technique is a "bad practice" withOUT knowing "my" environment, appears to
me to be somewhere between "arrogant" and "ignorant".
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Style (was: Sets and subsets

_(reply depth: 14)_

- **From:** robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner)
- **Date:** 2003-04-28T01:26:21+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3eac5f18.274780521@news.optonline.net>`
- **References:** `<b7tepn$9tb$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <3EA55E79.6090702@newsguy.com> <3ea5ed65.19257366@news.optonline.net> <3EA69C04.7090208@newsguy.com> <3ea88130.21326254@news.optonline.net> <b8ab71$1dv$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz> <3eaaa1e8.160795747@news.optonline.net> <b8h8s8$c6r$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net>`

```
"William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com> wrote:

>"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
>news:3eaaa1e8.160795747@news.optonline.net...
…[22 more quoted lines elided]…
>languages".

Why do you say that? You didn't give any substantiation.

It seems to me style is the exercise of personal choice in any language,
artificial or natural. 

>As far as the statement,
>     "When people are criticized here for bad programming practice, their
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
>maintaining)? Are there "shop standards"?

This is based on the assumption that shops have a single style. Most that I've
seen did not. Style changed over the years due to evolution and personnel
turnover. Management won't spend the time and money rewrite old code (I have,
but that's another thread), so if there _were_ to be a mono-style, it would have
to be that of the company's first year with COBOL. There are shops like that,
still using shop standards written in the '70s and justified by existing code.
There can be no progress in a shop like that, only revolution i.e. replacing
COBOL with another language. 

> - What are the "operating system" impacts on the code? (e.g. are there
>external SORT's that are commonly used?  Are you dealing with
>performance-sensitive applications where a "nightly batch run" must be
>completed within a certain time frame or with an online application where
>response time is critical?)

I don't see how style is affected much by performance concerns, except in
specialized cases. Moreover, the days when a programmer needed to eke speed out
of slow machines are pretty much over. It is still an issue in database access,
but that's SQL not COBOL. Can you elucidate?

As for SORTs, in mainframe shops a COBOL sort invokes the exact same third-party
code as an external sort. The only performance implication is that COBOL SORT is
faster, because it saves two writes and two reads. 

> - What tools are "common" to those working with the COBOL source code (e.g.
>Do they use Report-Writer? Are Assembler subprograms common?  Are there O/S
>API's in common usage?)

I concur about Report-Writer but how is COBOL style affected by the other
external factors? If you need to talk to the operating system, you need to call
something. COBOL doesn't natively support much more than date/time, dynamic call
and (in 2002) memory allocation.

> - Is the source code expected to be ported to other compilers or
>environments?  How about the "output" data?

Nobody knows until it happens. It is unusual for designers to plan a port to
another computer, just as it is unusual for companies to plan being acquired.
Heck, we didn't even plan for Y2K until it was on the horizon, despite the fact
that it was obviously forseeable. 

In my opinion, ALL files should be exportable. If no one wants it today, someone
will tomorrow. That means 100% DISPLAY format. XML would be a lot better, but
COBOL doesn't offer that option. That's a shame because XML is rapidly becoming
the de facto standard in the 'information broker' industry, where we deal with
large volumes coming from divers sources. It's a lot better than X12 because
it's self-defined rather than externally defined. I believe it will be around a
long time because I can't imagine how another standard could improve on it.

"The nice thing about standards is there are so many of them."
```

```
robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote 

> In my opinion, ALL files should be exportable. If no one wants it today,
> someone will tomorrow. That means 100% DISPLAY format. 

100% DISPLAY format does not make it 'exportable'.  Aside from the
minor details of deleted records and slack space that apply to C-ISAM
files, most MF systems would use the default IDXFORMAT"3" (C-ISAM is
only default on Unix), Fujitsu indexed files are not even close to
being directly readable.

These more complex file structures give additional features such as
variable records, compression, integrated indexes.

But even with 100% DISPLAY and ignoring the minor issues that C-ISAM
has, the data is not particularly useful as blocks of unnamed
characters.

My files are all 'exportable'. Not becasue they are DISPLAY, but
becasue I have a sub-system that is a Report Writer that can export
any file based on selection criteria with specified fields and
calculations to reports, or CSV, or HTML tables or flat file.

SQL gives exportabilty as well as multi-language access, including
Cobol.

Arguing for DISPLAY format in Cobol indexed files misses the point
entirely.


> XML would be a lot better, but COBOL doesn't offer that option. 

Cobol can write XML files.  I do it via templating.

> That's a shame because XML is rapidly becoming
> the de facto standard in the 'information broker' industry, where we deal with
> large volumes coming from divers sources. It's a lot better than X12 because
> it's self-defined rather than externally defined. 

Being 'self-defined' is not useful unless the programs know what the
defined names will be.

> I believe it will be around a
> long time because I can't imagine how another standard could improve on it.

XML is a language for writing data applications. It is not, in itself,
a solution.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Sets and subsets

_(reply depth: 8)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2003-04-22T23:52:08-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<b8565s$jum$1@slb4.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<b7tepn$9tb$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <3ea2c17e.150090828@news.optonline.net> <b7utlb$j1k$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz> <3ea33042.178450965@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0304202059.377ac2c3@posting.google.com> <3ea4782a.262407453@news.optonline.net> <217e491a.0304212127.59859a3a@posting.google.com> <3ea5e85d.17968743@news.optonline.net>`

```


"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:3ea5e85d.17968743@news.optonline.net...
> riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote:
>
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> integer.

Unsupported (and incorrect) claim.

The set of *all* integers is made of of an (ifinitie) number of individual
integers.

A set including any (but not all) of the members of the set of all integers
is also a set of integers - but is not the set of ALL integers, hence it is
a "proper subset" of the set of all integers (see separate post for
details).

An individual member of the set of all integers is CORRECTLY refered to as
an integer - no matter what other context it is used in - and no matter what
*other* sets it belongs to (such as the
 - the set of all even integers
 - the set of all positive integers
 - the set of integers ending with "00"
 - the set of integers that also represent dates in the format YYYYMMDD

None of *these* sets is the set of ALL integers, but rather they are a
proper subset of that superset - and each member of these subsets is
(remains) an integer.
```

robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote 

> >You cannot 'run out' of an infinite set of numbers, that is why it is
> >infinite.
> 
> No, you don't understand the abstraction of greater than. 

Continuing to show your lack of understanding of any abstraction of
infinity does not progress the discussion further.

> Given that there are
> more irrationals than integers, there is no way to assign an ordinal number to

Set Theory is an merely a mechanism (and just one of many such
mechanisms) for organising a subset of reality, it is not reality
itself.  It is an abstraction.

There is no _actual_ need to assign ordinals to irrational numbers for
them to exist. Doing so in set theory is done by people who
_understand_ it.

I suggest you get a book on set theory and such.

- **From:** "Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz>

```

"Richard Plinston" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message
news:b7v065$k54$1@aklobs.kc.net.nz...
> Robert Wagner wrote:
>
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Amazing coincidence...

I arrived back yesterday, early evening, and was met at Auckland by a friend
who was going
through to Tauranga and could offer a lift.

By the time we got through the gorges we were both getting hungry, so we
stopped at a restaurant on the main road between Katikati and Tauranga
called "Forta Leza".

I happened to notice on the menu the old traditional favourite, "Filet
Mignon". In the old days, in New Zealand, this meant fillet steak, wrapped
in bacon and smothered with mushrooms, but over the years as Kiwis have
become more sophisticated, they have learned a lot more about food and it is
hard to get these "old time" delights.

Anyway, I ordered it. When it came, it was like eating in a time warp...

My mind rolled back thirty years as I tucked into tender fillet that you
could cut with your finger, cooked to perfection (I asked for "medium rare"
and it WAS "medium rare") wrapped in local farm smoked bacon, and smothered
with a delicious creamy black sauce made from field mushrooms (which taste
completely different to
the poor anaemic offerings we are used to from the supermarket), washed down
with a good Ozzie Cab. Sav., and served with a smile and a "Hi Pete, good to
see you back..."

My friend ordered the famous "carpet bag" steak...Prime fillet, stuffed with
fresh oysters and enhanced with extra oysters on the side. She pronounced it
"Excellent".

It is good to be home <G>.

Pete.

<docdwarf@panix.com> wrote in message news:b80sma$86i$1@panix1.panix.com...
> In article <3ea3cd6d$1_1@127.0.0.1>,
> Peter E.C. Dashwood <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Quite right, Doc. Goats, pigs, and Human Beings are omnivorous. And I am
certainly more omnivorous than carnivorous.

Nevertheless, I still qualify as a carnivore. (see argument below...)

Carnivorism is simply a subset of omnivorism, in this sense.

(Do you really want to go into the set theory thing <G>?)

I can actually be carnivorous (meat eating) and herbivorous (vegetable
eating) without losing my membership of the omnivore set, IF the literal
meaning of these terms is used.

The use of these terms to mean ONLY meat-eating or ONLY vegetable eating is
something that is useful to biologists when classifying species. (Even then
it isn't strictly true...for example, chimpanzees which are classed as
herbivores do actually eat meat occasionally...).

Rest assured, I eat (almost) anything...<G>.

Pete.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Sets and subsets

_(reply depth: 7)_

- **From:** robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner)
- **Date:** 2003-04-22T02:57:06+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3ea48004.264418078@news.optonline.net>`
- **References:** `<b7tepn$9tb$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <3ea314ee_1@127.0.0.1> <3ea33bf2.181443202@news.optonline.net> <3ea3cd6d$1_1@127.0.0.1> <b80sma$86i$1@panix1.panix.com>`

```
docdwarf@panix.com wrote:

>In article <3ea3cd6d$1_1@127.0.0.1>,
>Peter E.C. Dashwood <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
>definitions I was taught, more 'omnivorous' than 'carnivorous'.

You are correct -- humans are omnivores. But the phrase "side order" gives it
away. Modern humans want meat to the Main Course and vegetables to be an
afterthought -- the kind of thing you heat up in a microwave and don't pay much
attention to. 

Mediterranean and Middle-Eastern cuisine turns this attitude on its head. It
assumes the Main Course is vegetable with meat added (if at all) for flavoring. 

So there's no confusion, vegetarians so not subsist on 'green leafy table
vegetables'. One would starve to death on that. Their diet is based on grains
and beans.  Wheat, corn and rice are all grasses, so you might say they subsist
on 'grass seed'. Wheat contains 10% protein; quinoa (a South American grain)
contains over 20% protein. 

I use 'green leafys' for flavor rather than nutrition. For instance, today I had
farfale (butterfly pasta) with kale, artichoke hearts and a light lemon sauce. A
side dish was carrot 'flan' (custard). Either would have rated 24 on the Zugat
scale for judging restaurant food.
```
