###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Sets and subsets

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2003-04-20T19:35:24-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<b7vecf$6p$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net>`
- **References:** `<b7tepn$9tb$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <3ea2e7c6_2@127.0.0.1> <217e491a.0304201542.781bbe1f@posting.google.com>`

```
Sorry if my "simplistic" (intended to be semi-humorous) "logic problem"
causes "serious" issues with categories of 'humans".

What I was trying to point out was the FALLACY in the logic,

The set of all "X's" includes items with categories A and B

If an individual member A  belongs to a subset of Y's with category A but
not Category B, then (according to RW's logic on dates of format "whole
numbers represented in YYYYMMDD" are not integers), then individual item A
should not be called an "X" - because it is also a member of the subset
fully defined as all "Y's".

Bottom-line:
  (ignoring the word "integer")  Any item that belongs to a subset of a
larger set; remains a member of the larger set - even when only talking
about the specific subset (of which it is ALSO a member).

This does differ slightly (as I understanding it) from intersecting sets
mentioned elsewhere in CLC  (or at least requires a slight clarification).

  If set A and set B intersect then the intersecting set, C, is defined as
all items that belong to *both* A and B.  Therefore, it is true that items
of set C may always be correctly ALSO called members of Set A and members of
Set B; OTOH, there is no (required) logical relationship between members of
set A and set B, i.e. a member of set A may or may NOT also be a member of
Set B and a member of set B may or may not also be a member of set A.

    ***

A COBOL example (ignoring again "integers") of intersecting sets are
operands of statements used as sending operands or as receiving operands.
In most statements, it is true that an operand is ONLY a member of one of
the sets of sending versus receiving operands.  However, there is a
(limited) set of operands that are SIMULTANEOUSLY both a sending and
receiving item.  The most "interesting" of these (that I can think of - off
the top of my head) is  a data-item used in an

    INSPECT ident-1 Tallying ...  Replacing ...

In this statement it is critical for the correct functioning of INSPECT,
that ident-1 be treated as a SENDING operand for the TALLYING phrase and a
receiving operand for the REPLACING phrase.

This is an example of how, being a member of the intersected subset, does
not stop ident-1 from being either a sending operand or a receiving
operands - just because it is also a member of the other category.
Similarly (and getting back to the original "issue") it most certainly does
NOT stop it from being an OPERAND, just because it is also a member of (2
separate) intersecting subsets.
```

same degree of usefulness, whether they admit they are wrong or insist they
are right...<G>)

> My current suggestion is that we all use "eat meat" (instead of the
> "traditional" mentioning of "Hitler" and see if this can SHUT up his
useless
> posts.  When he actually posts useful and correct information (which has
> happened a couple of times), then we can try and give him "positive
> feedback".  SOMETHING has to work to get the "value" back to CLC.
>
I can see you are getting to the end of your rope over this, Bill. I suspect
you are not alone.

While I sympathize, it is up to each of us to handle this in the best way we
can. In doing so, there is growth for all concerned, including Robert...

Your last sentence causes me grave concern, so that is the one I'll
address...

I don't believe that ANY one individual can diminish the value of CLC.

For all the irritation you have experienced, you have responded and Robert
has been a stimulus (albeit, a sometimes irritating one to some...) which
has prompted you to think about what is being said and respond.

That process alone has some value.

I don't want to go into corny analogies about how pearls are produced, but
sometimes, an irritant can produce a good result.

If you are really being "wound up" then simply don't respond for a while,
until you are less stressed by what you read.

There IS value in this NG.

Part of that value is found in the contributions of people like yourself.
You have been a pillar of support over years here and I never cease to
wonder at the extent and detail of your knowledge. If you allow your
exasperation to mean that you no longer post anything useful or worthwhile,
and just end up responding to the posts of one person, in a fruitless
endeavour to get him to admit he is wrong, then CLC is indeed being
devalued.

There always has been  value here and the arrival of someone who is
aggressive or abrasive or arrogant or stupid or in any way unpleasant,
cannot and should not diminish that.

The only way it can do so is if the group as a whole, LETS it...<G>

Pete.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: *MY* latest strategy for RW (was: Sets and subsets

_(reply depth: 10)_

- **From:** "Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2003-04-22T22:14:30+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3ea516f7_2@127.0.0.1>`
- **References:** `<b7tepn$9tb$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <3ea2e7c6_2@127.0.0.1> <217e491a.0304201542.781bbe1f@posting.google.com> <b7vecf$6p$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net> <01c307ad$a7c136a0$ea95f243@chottel> <3EA36719.9BEB1687@shaw.ca> <3ea3836b.199743692@news.optonline.net> <b803qm$iij$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net> <3ea3e06c_2@127.0.0.1> <3ea49cb3.271762189@news.optonline.net>`

```

"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:3ea49cb3.271762189@news.optonline.net...
> "Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz> wrote:
> >
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> >exasperation to mean that you no longer post anything useful or
worthwhile,
> >and just end up responding to the posts of one person, in a fruitless
> >endeavour to get him to admit he is wrong, then CLC is indeed being
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> You are really good at negotiating compromise. You could be manager in my
shop
> any time.
>
I am a trained negotiator, but the problem here is that you simply bring out
the worst in people <G>.

The "members" of CLC (for the most part) are intelligent, decent people who
meet here through a shared love of COBOL and discuss all kinds of things. It
is a very valuable forum. When you insult them and undermine them you can
hardly expect them to react kindly.

The trouble is, Robert, that you don't even realise how offensive some of
what you  are saying can be to people who(like yourself) have spent a
lifetime working with COBOL. I honestly believe that a lot of what you say
is said without malice or intent to wound, it's just  that it is presented
in such an unpalatable manner that there HAS to be fireworks...<G>. The
people here are intelligent problem solvers. They don't like loose
descriptions and sweeping statements about stuff and neither do they like
being bullied or coerced to a point of view, or having the ground constantly
changed when they are attempting to have a rational discussion. Being
dismissive of them and their work is unlikely to encourage them to cut you
any slack...

Personally, I really don't care too much about the emotional interaction, as
all the people involved are adults and able to take care of themselves. (And
it seems to me that most people give as good as they get...)

I am much more concerned about the underlying principles of an unmoderated
public forum.

If one individual causes such a negative reaction that the group as  a whole
decide to stop that person, then we have lost something very worthwhile. (At
least, it is very dear to MY heart...<G>).

Having said that, it is unreasonable to expect people to have their patience
tried indefinitely <G>. (Despite the importance of the underlying principles
here, NO-ONE gets carte blanche to upset and insult indefinitely...). Either
the offending poster will be killfiled or people will simply stop responding
(or leave the group...). None of these are useful outcomes.

My hope is that you will have some corners knocked off by the group, and the
group will become less sensitive and more understanding of your "style".
That way everybody wins. (Sometimes there are intimations that this is
beginning to happen, but then one side or the other simply loses it and the
progress made is lost...)

> I agree with  your concept of value in group. What happens when group
members
> disagree? If you don't step in, it devolves to name calling and flaming.
One
> would hope edicated people would know the protocol for rebuttal, but they
don't.
>

That simply isn't true. There have been some amazing cases made and lost
here and the protocol for rebuttal is well established. It is arrogant to
assume that people here don't know how to argue, or understand the devices
of rhetoric...

Naming and flaming is a "phase" that people go through. Eventually, all
parties concerned realise it is stupid and pointless and it stops.

What happens when group members disagree?

Then they look for a compromise, with goodwill, and if they cannot obtain
one, they agree to disagree. It is helpful under those circumstances to
ensure that we keep in mind the reason why we come here. It is healthy for a
group to have conflicting viewpoints, but there must be enough mutual
respect to allow the conflict to be limited to points of view, and not
individual personalities...

Pete.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: *MY* latest strategy for RW (was: Sets and subsets

_(reply depth: 12)_

- **From:** "Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net>
- **Date:** 2003-04-23T14:13:45+00:00

In article <3ea602d2.24742312@news.optonline.net>,
Robert Wagner <robert@wagner.net> wrote:

[snip]

>You are correct when you say without malice, but I do intend to wound dinosaurs.
>My motivation is not injury, it's to show them there's a better way.

Mr Wagner, your use of the verb 'wound' seems to be not only idiosyncratic 
but it appears to contradict established definition.  To 'wound' as a 
transitive or intransitive verb is to cause/inflict a wound; a 'wound' as 
a noun is defined as an injury.

Consider:  'I intended to strike her but I did not intend to come in 
contact with her'.

DD
```

I tested your postulate by looking up quotations containing the word wound. Here
are examples of accepted use:

Rest assured that there is nothing which wounds the heart of a noble man more
deeply than the thought his honour is assailed.
Moliere (1622 - 1673)

O! many a shaft, at random sent,
Finds mark the archer little meant;
And many a word, at random spoken,
May soothe or wound a heart that's broken!
Sir Walter Scott (1771 - 1832)

A broken bone can heal, but the wound a word opens can fester forever.
Jessamyn West

Slander is a vice that strikes a double blow; wounding both him that commits,
and him against whom it is committed.
Bernard Joseph Saurin

Hope is both the earliest and the most indispensable virtue inherent in the
state of being alive. If life is to be sustained hope must remain, even where
confidence is wounded, trust impaired.
```

"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message

simply wasted energy.

because it has to do with years of their lives.

emotional reactions... I guess what I'm trying to say is that if you provoke
an emotional reaction, it is then unfair to say they reacted emotionally
rather than logically...

Numbers of the Faithful are seriously declining...<G>

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: *MY* latest strategy for RW (was: Sets and subsets

_(reply depth: 14)_

- **From:** "Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2003-04-25T20:53:13+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3ea8f78e_1@127.0.0.1>`
- **References:** `<b7tepn$9tb$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <3ea2e7c6_2@127.0.0.1> <217e491a.0304201542.781bbe1f@posting.google.com> <b7vecf$6p$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net> <01c307ad$a7c136a0$ea95f243@chottel> <3EA36719.9BEB1687@shaw.ca> <3ea3836b.199743692@news.optonline.net> <b803qm$iij$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net> <3ea3e06c_2@127.0.0.1> <3ea49cb3.271762189@news.optonline.net> <3ea516f7_2@127.0.0.1> <3ea602d2.24742312@news.optonline.net> <3ea711d3_1@127.0.0.1> <3ea8834e.21868478@news.optonline.net>`

```

"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message
news:3ea8834e.21868478@news.optonline.net...
<snip>
> > but there is no need for an argument to START from a
> >hostile position. I read many posts here that provoke me to think (I
usually
> >state that when I respond to them), so aggression and force are not an
> >essential part of thought provocation <G>.
>
> How do you disabuse bad ideas which have been held for decades? If you
come
> across as Mr. Nice Guy, saying 'it's all a matter of style', they'll
ignore you
> and proceed as before. I think you must grab them by the lapels and shake
some
> sense into them.
>
First of all, you need to be aware of exactly what your own position is...
If you have a problem with it "all being a matter of style" (in other words,
you REALLY believe there is only ONE "right" way), then the way you approach
it will be different than if you believe that style is just that, and can
cut enough slack to accommodate other approaches, while still making the
case for your preferred one...

You COULD grab them by the lapels and shake them (the last guy who did that
to me lost two  teeth (it was a long time ago and I had not come to the Zen
realisation I have now <G>),  and I still don't know what he was on
about...)...

OR...you MIGHT question their position so that they must think about
it...<G>

You MIGHT even present a prepared position of your own, perhaps at odds with
the received wisdom, but logically supportable, and enter into an exchange
of ideas. Without bombast, authoritarianism, cheap shots, (on either side),
seeking to impress with the argument from authority, or striving for
credibility by making stronger statements than you can really support, but
with good manners, careful though, and mutual respect.(Just like I'm doing
as I write this...<G>)

You MIGHT even state what your own particular experience has been on a
certain topic (God knows, you have enough experience...<G>) and see how that
stacks up against the viewpoint of others. (Do this WITHOUT deciding that
YOUR personal experience is representative of the industry as a
whole...<G>).

I believe that any and all of the above "strategies" are likely to be better
received than "let's kick some dinosaur ass".

Note I am NOT suggesting they will be as much "fun" <G>, but it depends on
your definition of "fun", and how serious you are about making a
contribution here.

> >> >The "members" of CLC (for the most part) are intelligent, decent
people
> >who
> >> >meet here through a shared love of COBOL and discuss all kinds of
things.
> >It
> >> >is a very valuable forum. When you insult them and undermine them you
can
> >> >hardly expect them to react kindly.
> >>
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> >For the most part, they do. Remember, that for most people here the
topics
> >discussed are pretty dear to their hearts. They take it pretty seriously
> >because it has to do with years of their lives.
>
> Same here. 40 years.
>

Well, there, you should understand how they feel...

> >If you are disrespectful, rude, flippant and dismissive, you can expect
> >emotional reactions... I guess what I'm trying to say is that if you
provoke
> >an emotional reaction, it is then unfair to say they reacted emotionally
> >rather than logically...
>
> I don't just say 'you guys are fools'. That's what adolescents do. I
always
> suggest alternatives. If they dislike my ideas, they should rebut. They
> shouldn't say 'RW is an ignorant jerk and everything he says is wrong.'
That's
> an emotional response.
>

Yes, you have evoked some emotional responses here, but surely it was to be
expected?

> >If you seriously intend to wound dinosaurs in the cause of "education"
then
> >you must expect to get stomped on.
>
> Of course I do. In mainframe shops, they stomp on my style without
provocation
> via 'standards' written in the 60's and 70's. My posts here are payback
that I'm
> not allowed to voice in real life. There's no freedom of speech in the
corporate
> world.
>

This is revealing, Robert. I understand exactly how you feel and it is
another reason why this forum serves an important purpose. It is a release
valve for some of the steam we all accumulate in the stress of our chosen
career.

There IS freedom of speech in the corporate world once you attain the right
level. (And that level is not attainable by beating knowledge into people).
I get to say what I think and I actually get paid considerable sums of money
to do so. (Much more importantly, I get to encourage other people to do so,
too...) But that didn't happen overnight. Credibility has to be established,
and it isn't established by being hostile or authoritarian.

If you want people to listen to you, you have to make sure they get some
benefit from doing so.

> >> Indeed ... freedom of speech.
> >>
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> It's not gratuitous, there's a point. As for bad manners, it depends on
what you
> mean. If you mean crude language and/or personal insults, I couldn't agree
more.
> I'm embarrassed by some of my lapses. If you mean 'offending' old-timers
by
> voicing contrary opinion, those are legitimate issues of debate, not
insults.
>

OK, I'll let that one go...<G>

> >I have had many exchanges with Richard, the Doc, Bill Klein, Howard and
all
> >the people you consider are "on your case".
>
> It's not my consideration, they _are_ on my case. They initiate flames. I
don't
> flame them except occasionally in retaliation. Why do they think it's
acceptable
> to flame me? It seems like a double standard.
>

Oh Dear...what's this? Poor me...??? <G>

As an impartial (and I am striving to be neutral in this; my instinct is to
help the underdog ...) observer, I can assure you that the flames have been
50:50. The difference is that you simply don't realise you are being
provocative and "asking" to be flamed. If you lead with your chin you can
expect to be hit... If it's any consolation, the general tone has improved a
bit lately and there is less flaming from both sides...see my earlier post
about this being a phase...


> > If you stay the course you will develop the same respect and
> >regard (although it may be hard for you to see that just at this
> >moment...<G>).
>
> Actually, I _have_ developed some respect for JerryMouse and Richard. They
have
> the 'chops' of Real Programmers.
>

Well, that's a start...<G>

> >Be that as it may, think on what I've said <G>.
>
> I have, and as a result decided to be less abrasive.
>
Good. Let's see how it goes...

Pete.

"James J. Gavan" <jjgavan@shaw.ca> wrote in message news:<3EA36719.9BEB1687@shaw.ca>...
> Charles Hottel wrote:
> 
…[22 more quoted lines elided]…
> Wise up guys, that includes you Bill, Chuck, DD  and Richard. IGNORE the SOB !

Oh Dear! He certainly seems to have wounded you, Jimmy. (Was it the
crack about re-wrapping COBOL 85 as OO <G>? Maybe you should take your
own advice...)

> What the apostle of free speech now back in NZ does - I could care less, if he
> wants to stroke the geek's ego.

I'm NOT stroking ANYONE's ego, Jimmy and your comment is way out of
order.

Respecting someone's right to post in an unmoderated forum, whether I
agree with what is posted or not, is nothing to do with stroking egos.

Your advice to ignore the posts is something I have been advocating
for some time here, but I didn't phrase it as arrogantly as you did.

Finally, I am surprised that (as a U.K. native), you have adopted the
North American mis-usage of "could care less". What you mean is you
"COULD NOT care less" about something. If you "could care less" the
implication is that you already care a lot, and I don't think that is
what you were seeking to convey...

Your meaning is exactly right and you should NOT care whether I
respond to RW's (or anybody else's...) post, but you need to express
your disdain more accurately if it is to have the devastating effect
you are hoping for <G>.

Pete.

(Please note I did NOT take your advice and "IGNORE the SOB" in
responding to this post...<G>)

Peter E.C. Dashwood wrote:
 >> On 21-Apr-2003, dashwood@enternet.co.nz (Peter E. C. Dashwood)
 >> wrote:
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
 > It would make sense to say " I could care MORE...".

If you put the emphasis on "could", the phrase could be construed as "I
could care less, but it would require a great effort on my part to do
so, because I am already caring very little".

Natural language being what it is, though, what it really comes down to
is this is an idiom, and it means what people think it means.  If that
happens to be something close to what the speaker wished them to
understand, then we have a felicitous use of language and everyone can
go home happy.
```

Joe Zitzelberger wrote:

>
> Speaking of which, do any of the UK posters know the etimology of "bobs
> your uncle"?  I always wondered about that.  It seems to be a
> superlitive of some sort...

I haven't a clue. But three possibilities, (1) some historical reference, (2) a
quote line from literature, and (3) Cockney rhyming slang, like 'Let's have a
butchers" = "butcher's hook" =  "Let's have a look'.

I posted a reference here to a poem with 'aitches' missing, "'Arry went to
'Ampstead etc....." Out of the blue, six or more months later, comes a query from
an academic in Newfoundland. What was my source - he teaches folklore. I'm in the
wrong racket ! You get money for teaching FOLKLORE !

He OWES me one. I'll write to him and ask <G>

Jimmy, Calgary AB

> While many in the USofA misuse and abuse, like, everything, there

"I could care less" when you already don't care at all.

If you don't care at all, it is impossible to care any less.

1. I couldn't care less.

2. I could care more.

Think about it...<G>

Then decide it doesn't matter...

Because it doesn't...<G> I only mentioned it to bug Jimmy...<G>

Problem is 'mais' obviously means 'maize' - or does it ?  We distinctly called
it maize in England where originally grown as a cattle feed - same crop as S.
American maize =  Green Giant Corn.

Hanging on my living room wall is a reproduction of John Constable's 'The
Cornfield" - and it ain't corn = maize; it is a generic term for grain crops
including wheat, barley. Same goes for mid Victorian Corn Laws - no way were
they a reference to maize.

Can't lay my hands on the OED, but from Websters :-

Corn ........3a) a cultivated American cereal plant (Zea mays) of the grass
family, with the grain borne on cobs enclosed in husks; maize; Indian corn....
4) (Brit)  a) the seeds of all cereal grasses, such as wheat, rye, barley,
etc......"

My best guess - corn flour/starch is made from wheat ?.

"Grits" is what I was brought up calling "cornmeal porridge", which is what
you are also refering to ... however in the southern states is is normally
made from white corn rather than our yellow corn.  I believe that cornstarch
is a manufactured product, a byproduct of corn sugar  (or maybe corn sugar
is a byproduct of cornstarch). The product that Pete was looking for is
normally called cornmeal here in Ontario, though I have also heard it
refered to as corn flour. It is basically ground corn, and can be just about
any colour, depending on the variety of corn. If you ask for "grits" in a
grocery store, you will get cornmeal made from white corn.

What we call corn, and the europeans call maize, is actually an american
crop, as is the bean.  Brought to the world courtesy of the american indian.
The bean was the dietary staple of  the Inca civilization, as rice was for
the orient and the potato was for northern europe. Maize was the crop of the
six nations, and was called corn here because it was considered a grain
crop.

Donald

This could go on forever <G>. To kill it I went Googling :-

http://www.cooking.com/advice/adgloss.asp?GlossType=ingr&Item=Cornstarch

"Cornstarch is a fine, powdery flour ground from the
endosperm,                             or white heart, of the corn kernel.
Lacking gluten, it is used as a
neutral-flavored thickening agent in such preparations as sauces
and fillings and to give baked goods a delicate texture. It is also
known as cornflour."

The second one supports my 'wheat' guess :-

http://www.cafecreosote.com/dictionary.php3?searchTerms=cornstarch

"A white, powdery thickener finer than flour. It is extracted from the starch
 endosperm of wheat or corn. It must be dissolved in a cold liquid before it is
 added to a hot mixture or it will lump. It results in a glazy, opaque finish. "

```
"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote:

>
>On 23-Apr-2003, "Donald Tees" <Donald_Tees@sympatico.ca> wrote:
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
>(and really grabs the flavors of butter and salt & pepper).

There are three kinds of corn: 

1.  Sweet corn. The kind we eat.
2.  Specialty corn, notably the Waxy Corn grown in China and used to produce ALL
cornstarch; popcorn, Indian Corn, and others used primarily in chemistry.
3.  Field corn (US term?), used for animal feed. The agricultural community
regards this as a grain and sweet corn as a vegetable. When you see statistics
on corn production or trade, they're talking about field corn. The figures do
not include sweet corn, which is less than 1% of field corn. There are two kinds
of field corn: dent and flint. Dent is so named because it has a dimple on the
sides of kernels. It is waxy. Flint is higher in protein and has a harder bran
(hull). 

Hominy is field corn which has been treated with lye to remove the outer hull.
Humans do not eat field corn unless is it very young or been treated this way. 
Hominy is not "different from plain corn"; it IS plain corn with the hull
removed. 

Grits are grains ground to the consistancy of grit, as found on sandpaper and at
the beach. Any grain can be made into grits. Yellow grits are made from sweet
corn (without the lye treatment). Hominy grits are made from hominy. Barley
grits are also available. 

Cornmeal is pretty much the same as yellow grits, except it might be ground
finer. 

Hominy grits are traditionally seasoned with either butter or cheese or, less
often, Louisiana hot sauce. I recommend oregano. You read it here first.

Now, can we get back to COBOL?
```

```
"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote:

>On 25-Apr-2003, robert@wagner.net (Robert Wagner) wrote:
>
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>sweet over the last few decades.

Critics say it lacks the flavor of corn; all you taste is sugar. Personally, I
like the new varieties. 

Another big advance in the last ten years has been shelf life. In the old days,
you had to eat an ear within an hour or two of picking for best flavor. Now
that's been extended to days or perhaps more than a week. This makes it possible
to get good corn from a supermarket. 

I remember being in rural Iowa, buying boiled corn from a stand. I asked, "how
fresh is this corn?" In most places the response would be a puzzled 'huh?' The
woman answered, "I picked it myself an hour ago."

Sweet corn really doesn't need to be cooked. It tastes as good or better raw. If
you do cook it, the maximum time should be two minutes. My mother used to cook
it in a pressure cooker. Go figure.

The difference between yellow and white corn is carotene, a yellow/pink/red food
coloring and source of Vitamin A. It has no flavor. Did you know flamingos are
born white? They become pink from their diet of crayfish and shrimp. Chickens
also have white skin. In the old days, you could spot an over the hill chicken
because its skin was yellow. Tyson changed that by feeding its chickens carotene
and telling you yellow skin was a sign of health. Nonsense, they're obscuring a
sign of shelf decay. That consumers bought it is testimony to the gullibility of
carnivores. 

Before Tyson, butchers used to dunk over the hill chickens in a vat of laundry
bleach (Clorox) to make them white again. 

>> >  I don't know that I have ever had yellow grits.
>>
>> It's also called corn grits and polenta.

>Oh, I've had polenta fairly frequently.   My wife likes fried cornmeal mush.  

I thought cornmeal and corn grits were made from sweet corn. I looked it up and
found they are not. They are made from sweet varieties of dent (field) corn.
They sure don't taste like field corn or hominy. 

Hominy is actually more nutritious than cornmeal. The lye treatment makes B
vitamins and protein more 'available'. Pellagra was a problem in the US South
before they (re)discovered hominy. This has been known for centuries in South
America and Mexico, where hominy is known as pasole and used to make tortillas.

There is a similar situation with rice. East Indians learned centuries ago that
parboiling rice before milling it (removing the hull) moved water-soluable B
vitamins into the kernel. Other cultures, especially in Southeast Asia, never
figured that out. As a result, they have a pellagra problem even today. 

Along comes Uncle Ben, rice mogul of Southeast Texas, with his invention of
'converted' rice. It wasn't 'converted' at all, nor was it an invention. All he
did was adopt the Indian technique of parboiling and give it a new name. It's
still a good idea, and the Uncle Ben company's quality standards are the best,
but it's not new. 

I no longer use plain rice. I use Basmati (Indian) or Texmati (US clone), which
have a lot more flavor. They're expensive in the supermarket, relatively
inexpensive when bought in bulk. As with computers, 'if it has a brand name, you
paid too much.'
```

```
"JerryMouse" <nospam@bisusa.com> wrote:

>
>"Charles Hottel" <chottel@cpcug.org> wrote in message
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
>chair.

Lazy scholars have been doing that for ages. It's called a Review Study or Meta
Analysis.

Actually, meta analysist can be useful under the right conditions. Suppose you
have ten studies _of the same thing_ which all came up negative. The reason they
were negative could be because their individual sample sizes weren't big enough
to overcome beta: the probability of a false negative. The recirocal of beta is
called Power. In other words, they didn't have enough power. This is very
common.

Mathematically combining them produces a study with (approximately) 10 times as
many subjects. It may have enough power to produce a positive finding. The US
Food and Drug administration does this routinely. They are employ experts who
write papers and books about how to do it properly. They literally have 'written
the book on the subject.'
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Caring v. Sharing

_(reply depth: 15)_

- **From:** "Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2003-04-26T12:46:09+12:00

"Robert Wagner" <robert@wagner.net> wrote in message

> There are three kinds of corn:
>
> 1.  Sweet corn. The kind we eat.
> 2.  Specialty corn, notably the Waxy Corn grown in China and used to
produce ALL
> cornstarch; popcorn, Indian Corn, and others used primarily in chemistry.
> 3.  Field corn (US term?), used for animal feed. The agricultural
community
> regards this as a grain and sweet corn as a vegetable. When you see
statistics
> on corn production or trade, they're talking about field corn. The figures
do
> not include sweet corn, which is less than 1% of field corn. There are two
kinds
> of field corn: dent and flint. Dent is so named because it has a dimple on
the
> sides of kernels. It is waxy. Flint is higher in protein and has a harder
bran
> (hull).
>
> Hominy is field corn which has been treated with lye to remove the outer
hull.
> Humans do not eat field corn unless is it very young or been treated this
way.
> Hominy is not "different from plain corn"; it IS plain corn with the hull
> removed.
>
> Grits are grains ground to the consistancy of grit, as found on sandpaper
and at
> the beach. Any grain can be made into grits. Yellow grits are made from
sweet
> corn (without the lye treatment). Hominy grits are made from hominy.
Barley
> grits are also available.
>
> Cornmeal is pretty much the same as yellow grits, except it might be
ground
> finer.
>
> Hominy grits are traditionally seasoned with either butter or cheese or,
less
> often, Louisiana hot sauce. I recommend oregano. You read it here first.
>
> Now, can we get back to COBOL?
>

thank you ... and sure, why not?

Donald

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Sets and subsets

_(reply depth: 4)_

- **From:** "Peter E.C. Dashwood" <dashwood@enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2003-04-21T23:07:12+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<3ea3d0f0_1@127.0.0.1>`
- **References:** `<b7tepn$9tb$1@slb9.atl.mindspring.net> <3ea2e7c6_2@127.0.0.1> <217e491a.0304201542.781bbe1f@posting.google.com> <b7vecf$6p$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net>`

```

"William M. Klein" <wmklein@ix.netcom.com> wrote in message
news:b7vecf$6p$1@slb6.atl.mindspring.net...
> Sorry if my "simplistic" (intended to be semi-humorous) "logic problem"
> causes "serious" issues with categories of 'humans".
>
> What I was trying to point out was the FALLACY in the logic,
>

Ah, sorry. I take logic far too seriously...<G>

> The set of all "X's" includes items with categories A and B
>
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Nonsense...

> Bottom-line:
>   (ignoring the word "integer")  Any item that belongs to a subset of a
> larger set; remains a member of the larger set - even when only talking
> about the specific subset (of which it is ALSO a member).
>

Correct.

> This does differ slightly (as I understanding it) from intersecting sets
> mentioned elsewhere in CLC  (or at least requires a slight clarification).
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> of set C may always be correctly ALSO called members of Set A and members
of
> Set B; OTOH, there is no (required) logical relationship between members
of
> set A and set B, i.e. a member of set A may or may NOT also be a member of
> Set B and a member of set B may or may not also be a member of set A.
>

Absolutely. "Intersection" corresponds to Boolean "AND"; union corresponds
to Boolean "OR", thus set theory (when we define a few other essentials like
multiplicative and additive identities, along with negation ("...is NOT an
element of"), is a Boolean Algebra. We can therefore operate on sets exactly
as we do with Boolean Algebras and can derive a propositional calculus from
set theory, just as we can from the symbolic logical equivalents.

(Robert is wrong when he says nobody here understands set theory...I am not
the only one <G>)

>     ***
>
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> receiving item.  The most "interesting" of these (that I can think of -
off
> the top of my head) is  a data-item used in an
>
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> Similarly (and getting back to the original "issue") it most certainly
does
> NOT stop it from being an OPERAND, just because it is also a member of (2
> separate) intersecting subsets.
>

That's a very substantial analogy. It works because TALLYING and REPLACING
are NOT mutually exclusive. (It becomes a much more interesting argument if
they were...<G>)

Pete.
